# 3.3 Sketch Switching 

In this section, we describe the sketch switching framework introduced by [ BJWY22 ]. The framework maintains a relatively small number of independent instances A1, . . . , A λ of a non-robust strong tracking streaming algorithm. The main intuition is to change the output of the algorithm very rarely, so that these small number of independent instances can collectively achieve a “good” approximation over the entire course of the stream. Specifically, at each time in the stream, only one of these independent instances is “active”. As long as the current output by instance Ac is a sufficiently accurate multiplicative approximation of output by the active instance, then the overall framework outputs the same value and the active instance remains the same. However, when the current output by instance Ac is no longer a sufficiently accurate multiplicative approximation of output by the active instance, we update the output of the overall framework and replace the active instance Ac with the next independent instance Ac+1 of the algorithm. The main point is that the overall framework carefully exposes the randomness of the independent instances A1, . . . , A λ. By the strong tracking guarantee of each independent instance, we have correctness of the active algorithm until the output of the overall framework changes. However, at that point, the active instance is replaced with the next independent instance anyway, so we retain correctness throughout the course of the stream. The main intuition is that many functions of interest can only increase a “small” number of times, so we do not need too many instances λ.To capture this property, we first define the following quantity: 

Definition 3.3.1 (Flip number) . Given an accuracy parameter ε ≥ 0 and a stream length m, let 

y = ( y0, y 1, . . . , y m) be a sequence of real numbers. We say that the ε-flip number λε(y) of the sequence y is the maximum integer k such that there exist k subindices 0 ≤ t1 < . . . < t k ≤ m such that ytj−1 /∈ (1 ± ε)ytj , i.e., either ytj−1 < (1 − ε)ytj or ytj−1 > (1 + ε)ytj .For a fixed function f : Rn → R and a class C ⊆ ([ n] × Z)m of stream updates, the (ε, m )-flip number λε,m (f ) of f over C is the maximum, over all sequences (( a1, ∆1), . . . , (am, ∆m)) ∈ C of the 

ε-flip number of the sequence y = ( y0, . . . , y m) defined by yt = f (x(t)) for all 0 ≤ t ≤ m, where x(t)

denotes the underlying frequency vector defined by the first t updates of the stream. 

– 51 – Observe that the flip number is monotonic in ε, so that λε′,m (f ) ≥ λε,m (f ) if ε′ < ε . Moreover, the flip number is cleanly preserved under approximations: 

Lemma 3.3.2. [ BJWY22 ] For a fixed ε ∈ (0 , 1) , let u = ( u0, . . . , u m), v = ( v1, . . . , v m), and 

w = ( w0, . . . , w m) be three sequences of real numbers such that: (1) For any 0 ≤ i ≤ m, we have vi = (1 ± ε

Definition 3.3.3 (Strong tracking) . For each t ∈ [m], let x(t) be the frequency vector defined after the first t updates of an arbitrary but fixed data stream. Let f : Rn → R be a function on frequency vectors. Then we say an algorithm A achieves (ε, δ )-strong tracking for f if at each step t ∈ [m],the algorithm outputs an estimate Zt such that 

|Zt − f (x(t))| ≤ ε · | f (x(t)|

for all t ∈ [m] simultaneously, with probability at least 1 − δ.

Theorem 3.3.4 (Sketch switching) . For a fixed function f : Rn → R, let A be an (ε, δ )-strong tracking streaming algorithm that uses space S(ε, δ ) for any accuracy parameter ε ∈ (0 , 1) and failure probability δ ∈ (0 , 1) . Then Algorithm 5 is an adversarially robust streaming algorithm that outputs a (1 + ε)-approximation for f (x(t)) at every time t ∈ [m] with probability 1 − δ. Moreover, the algorithm uses O (S(ε/ 8, δ/λ ) · λ) space, where λ = λε/ 8,m (f ).Proof. that also succeeds with probability greater than δ, across the coin flips of Algorithm 5. Hence, – 52 – we fix the string of randomness to the adversary, which results in a deterministic adversary. Moreover, since each instance fails with probability δλ , then by a union bound, all λ instances provide strong tracking with probability at least 1 − δ.Toward correctness, it remains to show that all updates of the stream can be handled before the λ independent instances A1, . . . , A λ are exhausted. To that end, we apply Lemma 3.3.2 with the sequence u = ( f (x(0) ), . . . , f (x(m))) , v = ( f (x(0) ), A 1(1) , . . . , A 1(t1), A 2(t1 + 1) , . . . ) and w being the output of the Algorithm 5. Since w is generated precisely according to v in the statement of Lemma 3.3.2, then it follows that λ = λε/ 8,m (f ) independent instances A1, . . . , A λ suffice. Finally, it remains to analyze the space complexity of Algorithm 5. There are λ instances, each which use S(ε/ 8, δ/λ ) space. Thus, the algorithm uses O (S(ε/ 8, δ/λ ) · λ) space in total. We now discuss a number of applications for the sketch switching technique, and particularly for Theorem 3.3.4. 

3.3.1 Applications to Moment Estimation 

We first consider the problem of moment estimation, defined as follows: – 53 – Definition 3.3.5 (Moment estimation) . Given a vector v ∈ Rn and a parameter p > 0, the Fp

moment of v is defined as 

Fp(v) = ∥v∥pp = ∑

> i∈[n]

|vi|p = |v1|p + . . . + |vn|p.

Given an accuracy parameter ε ∈ (0 , 1) , the goal is to output a (1 + ε)-approximation to Fp(v).

We remark that contained within the above definition of the Fp moment is the definition of the 

Lp norm ∥v∥p, defined as follows: 

Definition 3.3.6 (Norm estimation) . Given a vector v ∈ Rn and a parameter p > 0, the Lp norm of v is defined as 

∥v∥p =

>  ∑
> i∈[n]

> 
> 1/p

.

Given an accuracy parameter ε ∈ (0 , 1) , the goal is to output a (1 + ε)-approximation to ∥v∥p.

The Fp-moment estimation problem is frequently referred to as the norm estimation problem and vice versa. In particular, an algorithm that produces a (1 + ε)-approximation for one can be adapted to approximate the other within a (1 + ε) factor by appropriately adjusting ε (for constant 

p). Therefore, we refer to these problems interchangeably. We first show that the flip number of these problems is bounded. 

Lemma 3.3.7. [BJWY22 ] Let g : Rn → R be a monotone function, so that g(x) ≥ g(y) whenever 

xi ≥ yi for all i ∈ [n]. Suppose further that g(x) ≥ T −1 for all non-zero x, and g(M ·⃗ 1 ) ≤ T ,where M is an upper bound on the magnitude of the entries in the frequency vector and ⃗ 1 is the all-ones vector. Then the (ε, m )-flip number of g in the insertion-only streaming model satisfies

> (1
> ε

such that for each i ∈ [k − 1] , we have g(f (yi)) < (1 − ε)g(f (yi+1 )). Since we ignore the 0-th step, the flip number is at most k + 1 .After each yi, the function value increases by a factor of at least 11−ε . The number of such multiplicative increases needed to go from T −1 to T is O

Definition 3.3.14 (Distinct element estimation) . Given a vector v ∈ Rn, we define the number of nonzero coordinates in v to be 

F0(v) = ∥v∥0 = |{ i : vi̸ = 0 }| .

# 3.4 Bounded Computation Paths 

Whereas the sketch switching framework in Section 3.3 created a number of independent instances of non-robust streaming algorithms to handle each time the function value f of the data stream increased, in this section, we describe an approach that just uses a single instance of a non-robust streaming algorithm to achieve adversarial robustness. For a fixed function f : Rn → R, let A be an (ε, δ )-strong tracking algorithm for f that uses space S(ε, δ ). Consider an algorithm A′ that simply runs A with a finer accuracy ε′ and a smaller probability of failure δ′. 3.4.2 Applications to Subspace Embeddings

Definition 3.1.20 (Lp subspace embedding) . Given a parameter p > 0, a matrix A ∈ Rn×d and an approximation parameter ε > 0, the goal is to construct a smaller matrix M ∈ Rm×d with m ≪ n

such that for all x ∈ Rd, the following Lp norm condition holds: 

(1 − ε)∥Ax ∥p ≤ ∥ Mx ∥p ≤ (1 + ε)∥Ax ∥p.

# 3.5 Difference Estimators 

Although both sketching switching and bounded computation paths offer simple means to achieve adversarially robust streaming algorithms from existing oblivious streaming algorithms, the resulting space complexity is not tight with the optimal results known for the insertion-only model. Namely, these approaches incur extra factors in both 1 

> ε

and log n due to the dependency on the flip number. In this section, we give a framework by [ WZ21b ] that does not lose these factors and are therefore tight with the optimal (non-adaptive) insertion-only results up to polylog  

> (1
> ε
> )

factors. The main takeaway from the sketch switching (and also bounded computations paths) technique is that each time an underlying function F increases by (1 + ε), we need to run an additional independent instance of a non-adaptive streaming algorithm, which generally results in O  

> (1
> ε

log n

> )

overall copies, translating to O  

> (1
> ε

log n

> )

multiplicative overhead. However, this seems wasteful – 58 – because the function has only increased additively by ε · F and so we should intuitively only need to roughly estimate the increase, say to a constant-factor approximation. The following algorithmic building block, called a difference estimator, offers a means to achieve this. Intuitively, a difference estimator computes (or approximates) how much a function changes between two different times throughout the course of a (non-adaptive) data stream. 

Definition 3.5.1 (Difference Estimator) . Let u and v be frequency vectors, ε ∈

> (

0, 12

> )

be an accuracy parameter, δ ∈ (0 , 1) be a failure parameter, and γ ∈ (0 , 1] be a ratio parameter. We say an algorithm is a (γ, ε, δ )-difference estimator for a function F if the algorithm outputs an additive ε·F (u)

approximation to F (u + v) − F (u) with probability at least 1 − δ, given F (u + v) − F (u) ≤ γ · F (u).

For the purposes of adversarial robustness, one should interpret u in Definition 3.5.1 as a frequency vector that arrives in some prefix of the stream and v as a frequency vector that arrives afterwards. Then the goal is to estimate the difference F (u + v) − F (u) caused by the arrival of v as it continuously evolves, while u is fixed. Due to this reason, [ WZ21b ] defines this notion as a fixed-prefix difference estimator. In particular, [ WZ21b ] also defines a fixed-suffix difference estimator, where v is fixed and u is changing, and shows such a primitive can be used to improve upon algorithms in the sliding window model, where only the most recent W updates in the data stream are considered to be the dataset of interest. Although the sliding window model has a rich history of research [ LT06a , LT06b , BO07 , BOZ12 , CMS13 , BGO14 , BLLM15 , BLLM16 , CSS16 ,BGL +18 , BWZ21 , WZ21b , ABJ +22 , EMMZ22 , JWZ22 , BLMZ23 , WZZ23b , CJY +25 , BGW +26 ,BWWZ26 , CWXZ26 , NPW +26 ], our goal is to focus on adversarial robustness in the insertion-only model in this section. Thus whenever we refer to difference estimators, we mean the fixed-prefix difference estimators in the context of [WZ21b]. 

3.5.1 Framework 

In this section, we describe the framework for adversarial robustness using difference estimators. We defer the discussion of the implementation of difference estimators for various functions to subsequent sections. 

Sketch stitching. Let F be a function defined over frequency vectors, and let v be a frequency vector implicitly defined via a data stream. Suppose there exists a (γ, ε, δ )-difference estimator B for 

F , and a streaming algorithm A that provides a (1 + ε)-approximation to F (u) for any frequency vector u. To build intuition, assume that both A and B use space ˜O 

> (1
> ε2
> )

.[ WZ21b ] introduces a new framework called sketch stitching , which partitions the stream into contiguous blocks. Let u1, . . . , uβ be frequency vectors induced by stream prefixes of increasing lengths t1 < . . . < t β , such that v = uβ+1 . Then: 

F (v) = F (u1) + 

> β∑
> k=1

(F (uk+1 ) − F (uk)) .

If we maintain a (1 + ε)-multiplicative approximation to F (u1) and a (1 + ε)-multiplicative approx-imation to each difference F (uk+1 ) − F (uk), then we can approximate F (v) to within a (1 + ε)

factor. In this construction, A estimates F (u1), while B estimates each difference. These estimates can be summed to obtain a final approximation, allowing us to stitch together contributions from individual blocks. Importantly, the vectors u1, . . . , uβ are unconstrained, and we next describe a principled way to define them. – 59 – Granularity changing. Assume v ⪰ u, meaning vj ≥ uj ≥ 0 for all j. Let i be the smallest integer such that F (v) ≤ 2i, and let t be the first time at which the prefix frequency vector u satisfies 

F (u) ≥ 2i−1. Then the difference F (v) − F (u) ≤ 12 F (v), so combining a (1 + ε 

> 2

)-approximation to 

F (u) with a (1 + ε)-approximation to F (v) − F (u) yields a (1 + ε)-approximation to F (v).Generalizing this, instead of performing a single granularity change at scale 2i−1, we introduce multiple granularity changes at progressively finer dyadic scales. Fix the reference prefix frequency vector u0 := u at time t, and let v denote the final frequency vector. For each k, define tk to be the last time before the increase F (uk) − F (uk−1) exceeds τk := F (u)2k , if such a time exists. Otherwise, we say that level k is inactive, omit it from the decomposition, and set tk = tk−1. Then we define each frequency vector uk as the vector defined by the updates in the time interval (tk−1, t k]. Observe that if we compute a 

> (

1 + 2k εβ

> )

-approximation to each difference F (uk+1 ) − F (uk), with uβ+1 = v,then their sum is a (1 + ε)-approximation to F (v). The key idea is that since each difference 

F (uk+1 ) − F (uk) ≤ 12k · F (v), a lower-accuracy estimator suffices for later blocks. We refer to the estimator used at level k as a level k difference estimator .By choosing β = Θ 

> (

log 1

> ε
> )

, we ensure that the contribution of the final block is O (ε) · F (v), so even omitting it adds only a small additive error. Thus, it suffices to sketch only the first β blocks with increasing coarseness in estimation accuracy. We emphasize that this is not a hierarchical data structure in the traditional sense. The sketches are not applied to differences of frequency vectors such as v − u, which correspond to F (v − u).Rather, we use sketches of u and v to estimate F (v)−F (u) directly, a technique requiring specialized difference estimators. This construction enables sketching with stronger guarantees than what is achievable using only A.Stream: v

Adversarial robustness. We now describe how to implement the framework in the adversarially robust streaming model. Recall that sketch-switching only reveals outputs at geometrically increasing intervals—specifically, when internal estimates grow by a (1 + ε) factor. This requires maintaining 

O  

> (1
> ε

log n

> )

independent algorithms and switching to fresh, unused sketches each time an output is revealed. The approach of [ WZ21b ] instead computes a (1 + O (ε))-approximation of F (u), where u is the frequency vector at the earliest time for which F (u) ≥ 2i−1, for some integer i, and the suffix vector 

v (representing the rest of the stream) satisfies F (u + v) ≥ 2i; we shall use O (log n) instances of this approach to handle the geometric scales 2i. Before u can grow to a vector u1 such that 

F (u1) ≥ 32 · F (u), it must first pass through a vector w1 where F (w1) ≈ (1 + ε) · F (u). Rather than maintaining a high-accuracy level-1 estimator for w1, we instead use a low-accuracy level-β estimator – 60 – for the small difference F (w1) − F (u), with β = O

> (

log 1

> ε
> )

. Once the stream reaches w1, we estimate 

F (w1) by stitching together the estimates of F (u) and F (w1) − F (u), then discard the estimator for F (w1) − F (u). As the stream progresses to a vector w2 for which F (w2) ≈ (1 + 2 ε) · F (u),we maintain a level-(β − 1) estimator for F (w2) − F (u), and again reveal the output once w2 is reached, discarding the sketch afterward. It is important to track progress using additive steps of ε rather than geometric ones. For instance, if we used a single sketch to track an increase from (1 + 2 ε) · F (u) to (1 + 4 ε) · F (u),then exposing the estimate at (1 + 3 ε) · F (u) could compromise the sketch’s randomness, and if we withheld the output, we could lose the ability to form a (1 + ε) approximation to F at that point. Instead, at each increment, we define wk such that F (wk) ≈ (1 + kε ) · F (u) and recursively estimate differences F (wk) − F (wk−1), each using a level-β estimator if the difference is small. Because the difference F (wk) − F (wk−1) is roughly εF (u), we only need low-accuracy sketches. To manage overlapping estimators, we leverage the binary representation of k to determine which level-j estimators to use. This effectively encodes a path through a binary tree over the stream, c.f., Figure 3.2, where each estimator corresponds to a different block whose internal randomness is only revealed once. Thus, we require O

> (

2k)

instances of level-k estimators for each k ∈ [β]. Since each level-k estimator only needs accuracy (1 + 2 kε), the total space does not grow too rapidly. The resulting space dependence remains bounded by ˜O 

> (1
> ε2
> )

, achieving the desired efficiency. Finally, we recall that we require O (log n) instances of this approach, to handle the geometric scales 2i, i.e., when the value of the function doubles. 

Space optimization. We can further optimize our framework using standard streaming techniques as in [BJWY22]: (1) Instead of storing O (log n) instances of A, it suffices to retain only O

> (

log 1

> ε
> )

at any time, since we can discard prefixes contributing just a poly( ε) fraction of F (v).(2) If A and B satisfy the strong-tracking property, then we only need to union bound over 

poly 

> (

log n, log 1

> ε
> )

sketches, instead of all m possible prefixes (where m is the length of the data stream). Note that our framework does not rely on existing sketches, so we must establish strong tracking for our difference estimators to fully utilize the second optimization. For the ease of presentation, we present a slightly sub-optimal framework for adversarially robust streaming algorithms in Algorithm 6. We clarify that within the context of Algorithm 6, A(s, t, η, δ )

is a stronger tracker for a function F between a start time s, an end time t, with an accuracy parameter η, and a failure probability δ. Similarly, B(s, t 0, t, γ, η, δ ) is a difference estimator where the prefix vector is defined by the updates between start time s and end time t0, and the suffix vector is defined by the updates between start time t0 + 1 and end time t. Moreover, the accuracy parameter is η and the failure probability is δ, while the difference is guaranteed to have a value that is at most a γ-factor of the value of the prefix. 3.5.2 Difference Estimator for F2 Estimation 

In this section, we describe the construction of a difference estimator for F2 estimation by [ WZ21b ]. To that end, we estimate F2(v) − F2(u) by expressing F2(v) as F2(( v − u) + u) = F2(v − u) + 2 ⟨v −

u, u⟩ + F2(u). Consequently, when F2(v) − F2(u) ≤ γF 2(u), a multiplicative 

> (

1 + εγ

> )

approximation to the difference F2(v) − F2(u) implies an additive approximation with error at most ε · F2(u).We next argue that if a streaming algorithm A produces a (1 + ε) approximation to F2(u) and a (

1 + ε√γ

> )

approximation to F2(v − u), then it can approximate the inner product ⟨v − u, u⟩ up to additive error ε√γ · ∥ v − u∥2∥u∥2 ≤ ε · F2(u), assuming F2(v − u) ≤ γ · F2(u) and noting that 

F2(u) = ∥u∥22. Therefore, for the case p = 2 , algorithm B can be implemented directly using the sketches produced by A, with space complexity scaling as γε2 .We first show that the expected product of two sketched dot products is exactly the dot product.

Optimized F2 algorithm. We now briefly describe how to optimize the adversarially robust F2

algorithm. The framework in Algorithm 6 tracks the active instances Ac and Bc,b for a counter c.Instead of maintaining all sketches Ac and Bc,b simultaneously, observe that it suffices to maintain sketches Ac and Bc,b for O

> (

log 1

> ε
> )

values of c at a time. Since the output increases by a constant factor each time the counter c increments, it suffices to maintain the sketches Ai and Bi,b for only the smallest O

> (

log 1

> ε
> )

values of i that are at least 

c. In particular, any larger index will have only missed O (ε) fraction of the F2 moment of the stream prior to the time when the algorithms are initialized. Therefore, the resulting output is still a (1 + ε)-approximation.

Heavy-hitters. As a straightforward corollary, we observe that our framework also applies to the L2-heavy hitters problem, where the goal is to identify the “frequent” items of a data stream. Formally, the problem is defined as follows: 

Definition 3.5.14 (L2-heavy hitters) . Given a frequency vector x ∈ Rn, the goal is to output a set 

S ⊂ [n] such that with probability 1 − δ, i ∈ S for every i ∈ [n] such that |xi| ≥ ε∥x∥2 and i / ∈ S for every i ∈ [n] such that |xi| ≤ ε 

> 2

· ∥ x∥2.

We remark that the problem is also often stated as producing a frequency vector ̂ x ∈ Rn such that |x −̂ x|∞ ≤ ε · ∥ x∥2, since by scaling the error parameter ε to say ε 

> 4

, then there is additive error ε 

> 4

· ∥ x∥2 to each coordinate i ∈ [n], which suffices to differentiate whether xi ≥ ε · ∥ x∥2 or 

xi ≤ ε 

> 2

· ∥ x∥2. By executing independent L2-heavy hitter streaming algorithms for each difference estimator A and strong tracker B, where the threshold for identifying heavy hitters is aligned with the accuracy guarantee of the corresponding algorithm, we can recover a list that includes all potential heavy hitters along with an estimated count for each item. We use the following L2-heavy hitter streaming algorithm. 

Theorem 3.5.15. [BCI +17 ] For any ε > 0 and δ ∈ [0 , 1) , there exists a streaming algorithm, denoted (ε, δ )-BPTree , which with probability at least 1 − δ outputs a set of ε 

> 2

-heavy hitters that includes all ε-heavy hitters, along with approximate frequencies for each reported item up to an additive error of ε 

> 4

· L2. The algorithm uses O

3.5.3 Difference Estimator for Fp Estimation, 0 < p < 2

In this section, we use the framework of Algorithm 6 to give an adversarially robust streaming algorithm for Fp moment estimation, with p ∈ (0 , 2) . To apply Algorithm 6, we require an Fp strong tracker and an Fp difference estimator. There exist constructions for Fp strong trackers, so we devote the majority of this section toward the development of an Fp difference estimator. Finally, we also again present an optimization of Algorithm 6 to achieve near-optimal space guarantees, i.e., matching the best known Fp algorithm on insertion-only streams, up to polylog

Definition 3.5.17 (p-stable distribution) . A distribution Dp over R is called p-stable if for any vector x ∈ Rn and independent random variables Z1, . . . , Z n ∼ D p, the linear combination ∑ni=1 Zixi

has the same distribution as ∥x∥p · Z for Z ∼ D p.

Theorem 3.5.18 (Existence of p-stable distributions) . [Zol89 ] For every 0 < p ≤ 2, there exists a

Derandomization of p-stable random variables. Observe that the previous analysis assumes that the independent p-stable random entries of A can be generated and stored. Hence, it remains to derandomize A To that end, we first require the following property: – 76 – Lemma 3.5.26 (Lemma 8 in [ JW18 ]) . Let A be any streaming algorithm that stores only a linear sketch A · x on a vector x ∈ Zn with entries bounded by M = poly (n), where the entries of A ∈ Rk×n

are i.i.d., and can be sampled using O (log n) bits. Then for any fixed constant c ≥ 1, A can be implemented using a random matrix A′ using O (k log n(log log n)2) bits of space, such that for all 

y ∈ Rk with entry-wise bit complexity of O (log n),

> ∣∣

Pr [A · x = y] − Pr [A′ · x = y]∣ ∣ < n −ck .

Unfortunately, our difference estimator actually stores both A · (u + v) and A · u, so we cannot immediately apply Lemma 3.5.26. Hence, we require the following generalization: 

Corollary 3.5.27. [WZ21b ] For a constant q ≥ 1, let x1, . . . , xq ∈ Zn with entries bounded by 

M = poly (n) be vectors defined by a stream S, such that for each i ∈ [q], xi is defined by the updates of S between given times ti, 1 and ti, 2. Let A be any streaming algorithm that stores linear sketches 

A · x1, . . . , A · xq, such that the entries of A ∈ Rk×n are i.i.d. and can be sampled using O (log n)

bits, and outputs g(A · x1, . . . , A · xq) for some composition function g : Rq → R. Then for any fixed constant c ≥ 1, A can be implemented using a random matrix A′ using O (k log n(log log n)2) bits of space, such that for all y ∈ Rk with entry-wise bit complexity of O (log n),

| Pr[ g(A · x1, . . . , A · xq) = y] − Pr[ g(A′ · x1, . . . , A′ · xq) = y]| < n −ck .

Proof. Without loss of generality, we assume that all entries A · xi are integers bounded by poly (n)

for each i ∈ [q], due to the bit complexity of A and xi. Let wi = A · xi for each i ∈ [q] and suppose the maximum entry of A satisfies ∥A∥∞ ≤ nα for some constant α. Let N = M · nα so that by 

∥A · xi∥∞ ≤ ∥ A∥∞ · ∥ xi∥∞ ≤ nα · M = N, 

for all i ∈ [q]. We define the vector v = ∑qi=1 N 2iwi, so that ∥v∥∞ < q · N 2q ≤ N ′ for N ′ = N 3q.Since N ′ = poly (n), then all entries of v can be stored in O (log n) bits. Moreover, each coordinate vj

with j ∈ [n] has a unique N 2-ary representation, due to the magnitude of N and the multiplication with N 2j . Specifically, if vj = ∑qi=1 N 2iwi,j where wi,j represents the j-th coordinate of vector wi,then there is a unique solution to the system vj = ∑qi=1 N 2iαi constrained to the condition that 

|αi| ≤ N . Therefore, the vectors wi = A · xi can be computed from the vector v.Observe that by Lemma 3.5.26, it suffices to use a random matrix A′ with O (k log n(log log n)2)

bits of space, so that for all y ∈ Rk with entry-wise bit complexity of O (log n):

> ∣∣

Pr [A · v = y] − Pr [A′ · v = y]∣ ∣ < n −ck .

Because a streaming algorithm A can extract the vectors A · x1, . . . , A · xq from the vector A · v

by the above argument, then A can subsequently compute the composition g(A · x1, . . . , A · xq).Therefore, ∣∣Pr [g(A · x1, . . . , A · xq) = y] − Pr [g(A′ · x1, . . . , A′ · xq)]∣ ∣ < n −ck .

It thus remains to show that the pseudorandom generator of Corollary 3.5.27 can be used to derandomize the correctness guarantees of the difference estimator. – 77 – Lemma 3.5.28 (Fp difference estimator for 0 < p < 2). [ WZ21b ] For 0 < p < 2, there exists a 

(γ, ε, δ )-difference estimator for Fp that uses space 

O 

> (

γ2/p log nε2 (log log n)2

.

Proof. The difference estimator for Fp(u + v) − Fp(u) requires an input splitting time t1 at which the frequency vector u concludes and the frequency vector v begins. We must then argue correctness over all possible stopping times t with t > t 1, provided Fp(u + v) − Fp(u) ≤ γF p(u) for p < 1

or Fp(v) ≤ γF p(u) for p ≥ 1. We apply Corollary 3.5.27 with q = 2 , so that there exists a pseudorandom generator that succeeds with high probability 1 − 1poly( n) for any fixed value of t.Now, since the stream has length m = poly (n), by taking a union bound over all m possible values of the stopping times t, then the pseudorandom generator is correct with high probability over all possible stopping times t. Therefore we obtain the claimed guarantees for the difference estimator, from Lemma 3.5.25. In particular, recall that the difference estimator is used to choose a final stopping time for the purposes of the framework in Theorem 3.5.5, i.e., when the estimated difference is sufficiently large. Therefore, the framework only requires that the marginal distribution of the difference estimator is correct at all times, rather than requiring the joint distribution to be correct. Specifically, the framework uses a sequence of outputs st1+1 , . . . , s t from the difference estimator over the course of the stream to choose a final stopping time t2, based on the first output that exceeds a certain threshold T . Therefore, we do not fool the final stopping time t2 chosen by the framework, as that requires a conditional statement on the output sequence st1+1 , . . . , s t of the difference estimator not exceeding the threshold T .For example, suppose the probability over the distribution of the independent p-stable random variables that st1+1 exceeds the threshold T is 12 , i.e., Pr [st1+1 ≥ T ] = 12 . Similarly, suppose the probability that st1+2 exceeds the threshold T is 12 , i.e., Pr [st1+2 ≥ T ] = 12 . Furthermore, suppose that we have that st1+2 cannot exceed T conditioned on the event that st1+1 < T , so that 

Pr [st1+2 ≥ T | st1+1 < T ] = 0 . Then since Theorem 3.5.5 chooses the first time that exceeds T ,the framework can never choose t2 to be t1 + 2 , i.e., Pr [t2 = t1 + 2] = 0 . On the other hand, our derandomization does not use independent p-stable random variables and only fools the marginal probabilities (of a pair of times (t, t 1) for each t > t 1). Hence, it is possible for Theorem 3.5.5 to select t2 = t1 + 2 , using our derandomized difference estimator. On the other hand, t1 + 2 is still a valid stopping time if the difference estimator is correct at time t1 +1 , and the output does not exceed 

T . That is, we need not fool the joint distribution, and thus not fool the choice of stopping time. Therefore, even though the difference estimator derandomized using the pseudorandom generator could induce a different distribution on the final stopping time, the final stopping time t′ 

> 2

output by our derandomized algorithm can still be used in our framework because t′ 

> 2

corresponds to the first output of the algorithm that exceeds the threshold T . Moreover, the derandomization retains correctness at all intermediate times between t1 and t′

> 2

.

Bit complexity and rounding of p-stable random variables. Finally, we describe how to store each inner product in Lemma 3.5.28 using O (log n) bits of space, by rounding the p-stable random variable to O (log n) bits of precision. Observe that due to the rounding, each summand changes additively by 1poly( n) . Hence, the total estimate also changes by an additive 1poly( n) . Then the total error of the estimate of the difference Fp(u + v) − Fp(u) is ε · Fp(u) + 1poly( n) . Now, as – 78 – long as Fp(u)̸ = 0 , then the additive 1poly( n) can be absorbed into the ε · F (u) term with a rescaling of ε. On the other hand, in the case Fp(u) = 0 , then our estimator will output 0 regardless, so that the rounding of the p-stable random variables will not affect the overall guarantees of our algorithm, i.e., the difference estimator still achieves additive error ε · Fp(u).

3.5.4 Difference Estimator for Fp Estimation, Integer p > 2

In this section, we use the framework of Algorithm 6 to give an adversarially robust streaming algorithm for Fp moment estimation, for integral p > 2. We again require both an Fp strong tracker and an Fp difference estimator to use Theorem 3.5.5. We note that for integer p > 2, the dominant space factor is n1−2/p [CKS03 , Gan12 , WZ12 , WZ21a ]. Definition 3.5.36 (Lp sampling) . Let x ∈ Rn, δ ∈ (0 , 1] be a failure probability, and c > 0 be any input constant. A perfect Lp sampler is an algorithm that either outputs a failure symbol ⊥ with probability at most δ or an index i∗ ∈ [n] such that for each i ∈ [n],

with low variance, so that the expected value of Y Z would be roughly ⟨up−k, vk⟩ and moreover, the variance of the estimator is small. Unfortunately, one issue with this approach is that perfect Lp samplers for p > 2 are not known. Indeed, while perfect Lp-samplers are known for p ≤ 2 [ JW18 , SWZ25 ], their constructions are based on duplicating each stream update poly (n) times, so that adapting these constructions to build perfect Lp-samplers for p > 2 would require (poly (n)) 1−2/p ) space rather than polylog ( poly (n)) ,though this gap has since been resolved by [ WXZ25 ]. In particular, observe that the former requires space larger than n while the latter remains polylog (n). 3.5.5 Difference Estimator for F0 Estimation 

In this section, we use the framework of Algorithm 6 to give an adversarially robust streaming algorithm for the distinct elements problem, or equivalently, F0 moment estimation. We again require an F0 strong tracker and an F0 difference estimator so that we can apply Theorem 3.5.5. Fortunately, we can use similar sketches for both the F0 strong tracker and the F0 difference estimator. # 3.6 Separation for Graph Coloring 

So far, we have presented a number of techniques to achieve adversarial robustness for different problems on insertion-only data streams. Frameworks such as sketch switching, bounded computation paths, and difference estimators can all be used to design adversarially robust streaming algorithms for a number of central problems such as norm/moment estimation, distinct element estimation, heavy-hitters, and entropy estimation. In fact, the difference estimator framework by [ WZ21b ]showed that up to polylog  

> (1
> ε
> )

terms, there is no “price” for adversarial robustness on insertion-only streams. That is, the space complexity for the aforementioned problems is nearly the same for the non-adaptive setting and the adversarially robust setting. A natural question then, is whether adversarial robustness can always be achieved for insertion-only stream with nearly the same space complexity. In this section, we present a result by [ CGS22 ], which shows a significant gap between the non-adaptive setting and the adversarially robust setting for the graph coloring problem. 

3.6.1 Graph Coloring in the Streaming Model 

We first define the graph coloring problem in the streaming model, where we first fix a vertex set 

[n] prior to the stream. The stream consists of a sequence of edges {u, v } being added to the graph; no edge deletions occur in the insertion-only model. A semi-streaming algorithm for graph streams is one that uses ˜O (n) = n · polylog( n) bits of memory. 

Definition 3.6.1 (Graph coloring) . In the K-coloring problem , the input is a graph stream, and the goal is to assign a color from [K] to each vertex such that adjacent vertices receive different colors. That is, the output is a vector in [K]n where no edge {u, v } ∈ E(G) has color (u) = color (v).

We remark that the value K in the graph coloring problem may depend on a graph parameter such as the maximum degree ∆ of G. Throughout, we assume ∆ is a sublinear function of n,for example ∆ ≤ nα for some α ∈ (0 , 1) . Since the output requires Θ( n log K) bits, we view any semi-streaming algorithm for K-coloring that uses ˜O (n) space as having essentially optimal space complexity. We first recall the following property upper bounding the maximum degree of a random graph. 

Lemma 3.6.2. [CGS22 ] Consider a graph G with n vertices and m edges, selected uniformly at random. Let ∆G denote the maximum degree of G. Then, for all 0 ≤ ε ≤ 1, we have: 

Pr 

> [

∆G ≥ 2mn (1 + ε)

> ]

≤ 2n exp 

> (

− ε2

3 · 2mn

> )

.

– 90 – The one-way communication game avoid (t, a, b ) is defined by [CGS22] as follows: 

• Alice receives a subset S ⊆ [t] such that |S| = a;

• Bob must output a subset T ⊆ [t] with |T | = b such that S ∩ T = ∅.Let avoid k(t, a, b ) denote the problem of solving k independent instances of avoid (t, a, b ) simultane-ously. The following result by [ CGS22 ] gives a lower bound on the total amount of communication necessary to solve the avoid k problem with probability 1 − δ.

Lemma 3.6.3. [ CGS22 ] Any public-coin one-way communication protocol that solves the avoid k(t, a, b )

problem with probability at least 1 − δ must use log (1 − δ) + kab/ (t ln 2) bits of communication. 

3.6.2 Reducing Multiple Subset Avoidance to Graph Coloring 

We now show that graph coloring can solve the avoid k problem. This will ultimately show that the graph coloring problem requires a certain amount of space in the adversarially robust setting. To describe the reduction from the avoid k problem to graph coloring, we begin by analyzing a special case. Suppose we are given a streaming algorithm A that is adversarially robust and maintains a (∆ + 1) -coloring of a graph. We describe a protocol that uses A to solve an instance of 

avoid (t, a, b ).Let t = (n 

> 2
> )

so that the universe corresponds to all possible edges in an n-vertex graph. Assume Alice’s input set A has size a ≈ n2 

> 8

. Using public randomness, Alice randomly maps her elements to edges, inducing a graph G that, with high probability, has maximum degree approximately ∆ ≈ n 

> 4

,as morally speaking, each of the n2 

> 8

edges has probability roughly 2 

> n

of being incident to a fixed vertex. Alice streams the edges of G to A and then sends the resulting internal state of A to Bob. Bob queries A to obtain a (∆ + 1) -coloring of G and then pairs vertices of the same color to form a maximal matching. Since at most one vertex in each color class can be left unmatched, Bob obtains at least n−∆−12 disjoint pairs. Each such pair corresponds to a non-edge of G, i.e., an element that is not in Alice’s set. Because the edge-label mapping is determined by public randomness, Bob knows exactly which elements these are. Bob then constructs a matching from these pairs and adds the corresponding edges to A. Bob then queries the algorithm again to get a new coloring. The addition of the matching increases the maximum degree by at most 1, so the new coloring uses at most ∆ + 2 colors. Bob can continue this process—forming matchings from monochromatic pairs, inserting the edges, and querying 

A—and still expect correct colorings due to the adversarial robustness of the algorithm. This process continues until the graph reaches maximum degree n − 1, at which point each vertex may require a unique color and further progress is not possible. Across all iterations, Bob adds a total of Θ(( n − ∆) 2) edges corresponding to missing elements. When ∆ ≈ n 

> 4

, this total is Θ( n2). Thus, the protocol solves an instance of avoid (t, a, b ) with t = (n

> 2
> )

and a, b = Θ( n2), which requires Ω 

> (ab t
> )

= Ω( n2) bits of communication. This implies that A must use at least Ω( n2) = Ω( n∆) bits of space. With further refinement, this argument can be generalized to work for any value of ∆ in the range 1 ≤ ∆ ≤ n 

> 2

, by using the communication complexity of avoid k(t, a, b ) with carefully chosen parameters. A more detailed analysis also allows the result to extend to any f (∆) -coloring algorithm, not just those using (∆ + 1) colors. – 91 – Algorithm 7 Protocol for avoid

By invoking Theorem 3.6.4 with the choice K = f (L), we directly derive the following corollary, which illustrates some particularly insightful parameter regimes. 

Corollary 3.6.5. [CGS22 ] Let f be a monotonically increasing function and let L be an integer satisfying L = Ω( log n) and f (L) ≤ n 

> 2

. Consider a coloring algorithm A for graphs with maximum degree at most L that uses at most f (∆) colors at any time, where ∆ is the current maximum degree, and has overall failure probability at most 34 against an adaptive adversary. Then, the space complexity S of A satisfies the lower bound 

S = Ω  

> (

nL 2

f (L)

> )

.

In particular: 

• If f (∆) = ∆ + 1 , or more generally f (∆) = O (∆) , then the required space is S = Ω( nL ).

• To achieve space usage S = ˜O (n), we require f (∆) = ˜Ω(∆ 2).

• If f (L) = Θ( n), then the space lower bound becomes S = Ω( L2).

We remark that the distinction between L and ∆ happens because the graph changes over time, and the goal is to use a number of colors that is a function of the current maximum degree, and not 

L.By comparison, it is known that there exists a semi-streaming algorithm, i.e., algorithm that uses ˜O (n) bits of space, which gives a (∆ + 1) -coloring [ACK19]. 

Theorem 3.6.6. [ACK19 ] There exists an insertion-only semi-streaming algorithm that uses ˜O (n)

bits of space and gives a (∆ + 1) -coloring of an input graph. 

Theorem 3.6.6 follows from an application of the Palette Sparsification Theorem by [ ACK19 ], which states that for any graph with n vertices and maximum degree ∆, if we independently and uniformly sample O (log n) colors for each vertex from {1, . . . , ∆ + 1 }, then with high probability there exists a proper (∆ + 1) -coloring in which each vertex receives a color from its sampled palette. Unfortunately, such a result is no longer true when the graph edges are not independent of the sampled palette. The streaming algorithm then performs a decomposition of the graph into “dense” and “sparse” vertices, recovering enough edges to color each vertex. In summary, Corollary 3.6.5 states that Ω(∆ 2) colors may be necessary to color a graph by an algorithm using ˜O (n) space in the adversarially robust setting. By comparison, Theorem 3.6.6 gives an algorithm that uses ˜O (n) space to color a graph with (∆ + 1) colors in the oblivious (non-adaptive) setting. Thus, Corollary 3.6.5 and Theorem 3.6.6 together show a separation for the graph coloring problem between the non-adaptive and the adversarially robust settings. – 94 – Chapter 4 

# Differential Privacy and Adaptive Data Analysis 

- -

Differential privacy can be used to hide the randomness of an algorithm from an adversary, improving the number of adversarial queries an algorithm can tolerate. 

In this section, we show surprising connections between adversarial robustness and both differ-ential privacy and adaptive data analysis. In Section 4.1, we first describe a framework introduced by [ HKM +22 ] that utilizes differential privacy to transform non-adaptive streaming algorithms to adversarially robust streaming algorithms; chronologically, these results first appeared in [ HKM +20 ]. In Section 4.3, we describe a result by [ KMNS21 ], which showed another separation between the (non-adaptive) insertion-only streaming model and the adversarially robust streaming model. This separation is for a more statistical problem, rather than a graph-theoretic problem 1

# 4.1 Differential Privacy 

We describe a connection drawn by [ HKM +22 ] between adversarial robustness in streaming algo-rithms and the concept of differential privacy , a rigorous framework for ensuring privacy when analyzing sensitive data. Consider a dataset that includes private information about individuals. An algorithm is said to satisfy differential privacy if its output distribution does not significantly change when the data of a single individual is modified. This means that no individual data has a substantial influence on the algorithmic outcome, thereby limiting the amount of information that can be inferred about any specific person. Intuitively, differential privacy ensures that anything learned about an individual could have also been learned had their data been arbitrarily changed or removed altogether. Formally, differential privacy provides a probabilistic guarantee that prevents the output from revealing whether any particular individual’s data was included in the input: 

> 1

In fact this result by [ KMNS21 ] pre-dated the result by [ CGS22 ] and asked for a separation for a more “natural” problem as an open question, which was subsequently resolved by [CGS22]. 

– 95 – Definition 2.3.1 (Differential privacy, [ DMNS06 , DKM +06 ]) . Given a privacy parameter ε > 0

and an additive parameter δ ∈ (0 , 1) , a randomized algorithm A is (ε, δ )-differentially private if for all datasets D1 and D2 that differ on a single element, and all subsets S of the range of A,

Pr [A(D1) ∈ S] ≤ eε · Pr [A(D2) ∈ S] + δ. 

For preliminaries on differential privacy, see Section 2.3 A central conceptual contribution of [HKM +22] is to demonstrate that differential privacy can be leveraged as a principled tool for designing new adversarially robust streaming algorithms. At a high level, the key idea is to apply differential privacy techniques to safeguard the internal state of the algorithm. Informally, this approach restricts, in a well-defined manner, the extent to which the internal state can depend on the adaptively chosen items in the stream. As a result, it becomes possible to reason about the algorithm’s utility guarantees even in adversarial or adaptive settings. It is important to emphasize that differential privacy is not employed here for the usual purpose of protecting the privacy of the stream data itself. Instead, it is used to obscure the internal randomness and state of the algorithm, thereby providing robustness against adaptive inputs. 

Framework overview. Consider an oblivious streaming algorithm A for computing a function g.[ HKM +22 ] constructs an adversarially robust version of A by running k independent instances of the algorithm in parallel, each with its own independent source of randomness. The input stream is fed simultaneously to all k instances. Upon receiving a query, the framework computes an aggregated response from the k copies in a way that masks their internal randomness using differential privacy. This ensures that the aggregation does not leak sensitive information about the individual internal states, thereby achieving robustness. Furthermore, under the assumption that the stream has a low flip number, then this aggregation only needs to be performed at a small number of time steps. To identify the relevant time steps, the framework invokes the sparse vector technique, c.f., Algorithm 1. Once such a time step is detected, an approximate median of the k responses is computed using a differentially private selection algorithm. The framework is given in Algorithm 8. The following statement shows that Algorithm 8 preserves the privacy of the internal randomness of the algorithms A1, . . . , Ak.

Lemma 4.1.1. [HKM +22 ] Let r1, . . . , r k be the internal randomness of A1, . . . , Ak in Algorithm 8 and let R = {r1, . . . , r k}. Then Algorithm 8 is (ε, δ )-differentially private with respect to R.

The statement holds from the observation that the inner loop in Line 6 is an application of 

AboveThreshold , c.f., Algorithm 1 and Theorem 2.3.7. On the other hand, the outer loop in Line 4 applies the private median algorithm PrivMed with a sufficiently low privacy loss to be satisfactorily amplified by advanced composition, c.f., Theorem 2.3.6. We next show that all outputs of Algorithm 8 are sufficiently accurate, while the outer loop has run at most λ times. 

Lemma 4.1.2. [HKM +22 ] Let A be an oblivious strong tracking algorithm for a function f that provides a (1 + α

> 10

Applications for DP framework. We mention that the framework of Algorithm 8 can be used to answer a number of adaptive queries in a number of other settings [ BKM +22 , ACSS23 , CSW +23 ], e.g., for graph algorithms in the dynamic setting, for matrix-vector norm queries, linear regression, half-space queries, point queries on turnstile streams, adaptive distance estimation, and adaptive kernel density estimation. Finally, we emphasize that since the results of [ HKM +22 ] achieve sublinear dependence on the flip number, they achieve space sublinear in the stream length for the important setting of turnstile streams. By comparison, previously discussed techniques such as sketching switching, bounded computation paths, and difference estimators achieve space with a linear dependency in the flip number, and thus not necessarily space sublinear in the stream length for turnstile streams. 

# 4.2 Robust Data Structures 

We now isolate a specific algorithmic paradigm from the framework in Section 4.1, for answering a number of adaptive queries. In particular, the framework was introduced for the streaming model by [ HKM +22 ] and for the dynamic model by [ BKM +22 , ACSS23 ], streamlined in Algorithm 9. We remark that this framework can be used to achieve an adversarially robust algorithm for F2 moment estimation on turnstile streams of length m, using ˜O (√m) space for ε = Ω(1) .

4.2.1 Applications to Matrix-Vector Norm Approximation 

In the matrix-vector norm query problem, we are given a matrix A ∈ Rn×d and wish to process Q

adaptive queries x(1) , . . . , x(Q) for an approximation parameter ε > 0, such that each query x(i) ∈ Rd

> i∈[d]

(1 − ε)∥Ax ∥p ≤ ∥ Mx ∥p ≤ (1 + ε)∥Ax ∥p,

c.f., Definition 3.1.20. However, since such embeddings must preserve the norm for all possible inputs, the required number of rows is typically m = Ω

> (dε2

factor. The constraint p ∈ (0 , 2] arises because p-stable distributions are only defined in this range. Combining Theorem 4.2.4 with Theorem 4.2.1, we obtain the following:

– 102 – 4.2.2 Applications to Linear Regression 

In the linear regression problem, we are given a fixed matrix A ∈ Rn×d and seek to answer Q

adaptive queries b(1) , . . . , b(Q) for an accuracy parameter ε > 0. For each query vector b(i) ∈ Rn,the goal is to return a (1 + ε)-approximation to the regression cost min x∈Rd ∥Ax − b(i)∥2.As with previous problems, one strategy is to compute a subspace embedding M = SA ∈

Rm×d using a sketching matrix S, and then for a given query b(i), solve the reduced problem 

min x∈Rd ∥SAx − Sb (i)∥2 [CW13]. 

Theorem 4.2.6. [CW13 ] Let A ∈ Rn×d and ε ∈ (0 , 1) be given. Then there is an algorithm that constructs a data structure using O  

> (d2
> ε2

log 2(nQ )

> )

bits of space and, with high probability, returns a 

(1 + ε)-approximation to min x∈Rd ∥Ax − b∥2 for any fixed query b ∈ Rn.

However, this approach may not remain reliable under multiple interactions. For instance, an adversary could learn the kernel of S and then issue a query b(i) that lies entirely in the kernel. This would cause Sb (i) to be zero, resulting in the algorithm returning the zero vector as an approximate minimizer, which might be a poor approximation to the true solution. To guard against such failures, a naïve solution is to use a separate embedding for each query, leading to total space complexity of ˜O 

> (Qd 2
> ε2
> )

Finally, we describe an approach by [ FFL +25 ] to approximately solve regression for a number of adaptive queries. In particular, suppose the design matrix A is defined through a number of adaptive, entry-wise perturbations via updates Vt ∈ Rn×d. Formally, the guarantees are as follows:

After processing the update, the algorithm outputs a vector xt that, with probability at least 1 − δ, is a (1 + α)-approximate solution to the regression problem defined by (At, bt), and does so in time

Here, each update Vt is either a matrix in Rn×d so that At = At−1 +Vt (in which case bt = bt−1)or a vector in Rn so that bt = bt−1 + Vt (in which case At = At−1). Moreover, κ is the condition number and ω is the exponent for matrix multiplication time. We now briefly outline the approach; we refer to [ FFL +25 ] for the full details. A natural extension of the private median framework introduced by [ HKM +22 , BKM +22 ] is to produce an – 103 – approximate solution vector. The algorithm follows a generic template: it generates k independent sketching matrices S1, . . . , Sk and pre-processes the data as (SiA, Sib) for each i ∈ [k]. When an update arrives, the corresponding sketches are updated efficiently. For each query, the algorithm samples s = polylog (n, d, k ) sketches from the k total sketches. The algorithm solves the respective regression problems for each of the sketches, e.g., argmin x ∥SiAx − Sib∥ for each sampled index i,and obtains solution vectors x(1) , . . . , x(s) ∈ Rd.These are then aggregated using a coordinate-wise private median mechanism, as follows. Namely, to construct the output vector g ∈ Rd, for each coordinate i ∈ [d], the algorithm takes the private median mechanism with input (( x(1) )i, . . . , (x(s))i) and sets gi to be the output. Since each entry is computed privately and individually, the advanced composition theorem guarantees overall differential privacy. Although this approach requires √Qd sketches—compared to the √Q required in [BKM +22], it remains efficient when d ≪ Q.The main technical challenge lies in proving utility, as the coordinate-wise private median mechanism could destroy the accuracy of the output solution. By triangle inequality, the regression error ∥Ag − b∥2 satisfies 

∥Ag − b∥2 ≤ ∥ Ax ∗ − b∥2 + ∥A(x∗ − g)∥2,

where x∗ is the optimal solution and the second term is upper bounded by 

∥A(x∗ − g)∥2 ≤ σmax (A) · √d · ∥ x∗ − g∥∞,

where σmax (A) is the largest singular value of A. Thus, upper bounding ∥x∗ − g∥∞ is sufficient for upper bounding the total regression error. Each vector x(i) satisfies ∥Ax (i) − b∥2 ≤ (1 + α)∥Ax ∗ − b∥2, and when A is well-conditioned, this implies an upper bound on ∥x(i) − x∗∥2. However, in order to effectively run the coordinate-wise median procedure, we need upper bounds on each entry of x(i) − x∗.While in general ∥x(i) − x∗∥∞ may be as large as ∥x(i) − x∗∥2, for sketching matrices such as the Subsampled Randomized Hadamard Transform (SRHT), it has been shown [PSW17] that 

∥x(i) − x∗∥∞ ≤ α

√d · ∥Ax ∗ − b∥2

σmin (A) .

Therefore, we use the SRHT for the sketching matrix S, after which it follows that the regression error can be upper bounded by 

∥A(x∗ − g)∥2 ≤ ακ (A) · ∥ Ax ∗ − b∥2,

where κ(A) is the condition number. By scaling α down by a factor of κ(A), we achieve the originally desired utility guarantee. This highlights the private median as a novel use of ℓ∞ guarantees in sketching, an aspect that has received limited attention in the literature. To further accelerate both pre-processing and update time, the SRHT is composed with CountSketch [CCF04]. 

4.2.3 Applications to Half-Space Queries 

Consider a set P of n points in Rd. The range search problem asks us to pre-process P so that, given a query region R from some fixed family, we can efficiently determine or count the points in – 104 – the intersection P ∩ R. This is a foundational problem in computational geometry [ TOG17 ]. A particularly important case is when the query regions are half-spaces, i.e., one side of a hyperplane, since many algebraic constraints can be represented as hyperplanes in a lifted space. However, exact solutions to this problem are notoriously difficult in high dimensions, as the query time typically scales exponentially with d—a manifestation of the curse of dimensionality [ BCP93 ,Cha00]. To address this, [ CLM08 ] proposed an approximate data structure that can answer half-space queries in polynomial time. Their notion of approximation works as follows: for a point set P

contained in the unit L2 ball, a hyperplane R, and accuracy parameter ε > 0, the data structure returns an estimate of the number of points on one side of R, with an additive error at most equal to the number of points within distance ε of the hyperplane boundary. This is called an ε-approximate half-space query. 

Theorem 4.2.9. [ CLM08 ] Let P be a set of n points in the unit L2 ball. There exists a data structure using ˜O

> (

dn O(ε−2))

space that answers ε-approximate hyperplane queries with high probability. The query time is ˜O (d/ε 2).

The construction in [ CLM08 ] is randomized, relying on techniques such as random projection for dimensionality reduction. Because of this randomness, the data structure may not remain accurate under multiple adaptive queries. To address this, by applying the general framework from Section 4.2 and Theorem 4.2.1, we obtain a more robust guarantee: 

Theorem 4.2.10. [CSW +23 ] Let P be a set of n points contained in the unit L2 ball. Then there exists a data structure using ˜O

> (

√Qdn O(ε−2))

space that can answer Q adaptive ε-approximate hyperplane queries with high probability. Each query is processed in ˜O 

> (dε2
> )

time. 

4.2.4 Applications to Point Queries in Turnstile Streams 

In the turnstile streaming model, we observe a sequence of m updates to a frequency vector x ∈ Rn.Each update affects a coordinate i ∈ [n], modifying fi by some value ∆i ∈ [−∆, ∆] , where ∆ is a polynomial in n. For any time step t ∈ [m], let x(t) denote the frequency vector after the first t

updates. The point query problem is to return the value of x(t) 

> i

for various choices of i and t, up to an additive error of ε∥x(t)∥1, for a fixed constant ε > 0.

Theorem 4.2.11. [ AY20 ] There exists an algorithm that, with high probability and for ε = 0 .1,uses O

> (

> (

> (

Theorem 4.2.12. There exists an algorithm that supports Q adaptive point queries with ε = 0 .1 and high probability, using O

– 105 – 4.2.5 Applications to Nearest Neighbor Search 

In the approximate nearest neighbor (ANN) problem, we are given a dataset of n points in Rd,represented as a matrix U ∈ Rn×d, which may be preprocessed in advance. The task is to construct a data structure that, given a sequence of possibly adaptive queries v1, . . . , vQ, answers each query efficiently and with high probability of correctness. Specifically, for each query vq, where q ∈ [Q],the data structure should return a c-approximate near neighbor from U, meaning that if the true nearest neighbor to vq lies at distance r, the output should be a point in U at distance at most cr ,where c > 1.We first recall prior work on ANN search data structures. A natural starting point is deterministic data structures, which have been studied extensively since the 1970s [ MOMM08 , CLRS22 ] and are adversarially robust by design. However, in high-dimensional settings, and without making any assumptions about the structure of the query distribution or dataset, these methods encounter the curse of dimensionality: both pre-processing time and space requirements grow exponentially with the dimension d, rendering them impractical in moderate dimensions. More efficient deterministic structures can be constructed under strong geometric assumptions, such as bounded growth conditions, e.g., when the number of points within a ball of radius 2r centered at a query point is only a constant factor larger than that within radius r [Cla97 , KR02 , KL04 , BKL06 ]. These assumptions permit data structures with polynomial pre-processing time and space complexity and logarithmic query time. However, such assumptions are quite restrictive and do not apply to many real-world datasets. Instead, [ FFL +25 ] considered solutions based on differential privacy (DP), which motivated them to look at the private selection problem. Suppose we are given n categories and s binary vectors 

b(1) , . . . , b(s) ∈ { 0, 1}n. The task is to identify the category j∗ ∈ [n] that appears most frequently across these vectors, i.e., j∗ = argmax j 

> ∑si=1

b(i),j . A standard approach is to first compute the total count vector B = ∑si=1 b(i), then add independent Laplace noise Lap  

> (1
> ε
> )

to each coordinate of B, and finally report the index with the highest noisy value. Importantly, the privacy parameter 

ε remains independent of both n and s, since only a single index is released. [ FFL +25 ] now connects approximate nearest neighbor (ANN) search to this selection task. By assigning a category to each point u ∈ U, and instructing each ANN data structure to return all nearby neighbors (instead of just one), we obtain a binary indicator vector for each structure, where the i-th entry is 1 if ui is found. Collecting these indicator vectors across multiple data structures, we can apply the DP selection mechanism described above and return the point with the highest noisy count. This construction ensures differential privacy with respect to the internal randomness of the data structures: for fixed dataset U and adaptive query v, the indicator vector depends only on the randomness within each structure. Consequently, the mechanism satisfies (ε, 0) -DP, allowing us to invoke the advanced composition theorem and reduce the number of independent data structures from Q to ˜O (√Q).To show utility, [ FFL +25 ] first proves that the selection mechanism is differentially private, and then applies generalization guarantees from DP, similar to the argument in Section 4.2, to conclude that a constant fraction of the data structures must succeed. There are two technical issues however. First, assuming only one near neighbor is overly restrictive. Fortunately, the approach generalizes: if there are at most s approximate near neighbors, then querying ω(s log n)

data structures suffices. By the pigeonhole principle, some point will have count significantly above the noise. Since LSH-based ANN methods return at most nρ candidates, [ FFL +25 ] notes that one can select s = O (nρ). Equivalently, a structural property can be imposed: that the ball of radius cr 

– 106 – around any point intersects at most s other such balls. This condition is satisfied, for example, in the Hamming setting and in the model of [KMS25]. Second, a naïve implementation of the DP selection process requires adding noise to each of the 

n entries in the count vector, resulting in Ω( n) time per query. On the other hand, the vector B

is s-sparse and its nonzero entries typically exceed the noise level. Prior work on private sparse vector release [ CPST12 ] has considered this issue, but existing methods either weaken the privacy guarantee or convert the algorithm to Las Vegas form with only expected runtime bounds. To address this, [ FFL +25 ] introduces a new sparse argmax mechanism , which efficiently simulates exponential noise addition over sparse vectors. The algorithm works by (1) adding s exponential 

Exp  

> (1
> ε
> )

samples to the nonzero entries, (2) drawing a threshold X from the n-th order statistic of Exp  

> (1
> ε
> )

, and (3) flipping a biased coin (heads with probability sn ). If heads, it generates s − 1

additional exponential samples (none exceeding X) and adds them to the support, including X, then returns the maximum index. If tails, it generates s such values, includes X as a random index from the complement support, and returns the maximum index overall. [ FFL +25 ] shows that with high probability, this procedure runs in O (s log n) time and faithfully reproduces the output distribution of full-size exponential sampling. Putting things together, the final guarantees for approximate nearest neighbor search is as follows: 

Theorem 4.2.13. [ FFL +25 ] Let U ⊆ Rd be an n-point dataset, and suppose fv : ( Rd)n → (Rd)s is a predicate function such that for any query v, the output fv(U) contains at most s candidates. Let 

A be an oblivious algorithm which, for any fixed query v ∈ Rd, returns a point (or subset) in fv(U)

with probability at least 1 − δ whenever fv(U) is non-empty. Suppose A has pre-processing time 

Tprep , space complexity Sspace , and query time Tquery . Then there exists an adaptive algorithm ˜A

that: (1) pre-processes U in time ˜O (√Q · s) · T prep ;(2) uses space ˜O (√Q · s) · S space ;(3) for any query vq with q ∈ [Q], it returns a point (or subset) in fvq (U) with probability at least 

1 − δ, using query time ˜O (s) · T query .In particular, the amortized cost per query is ˜O 

> (s√T
> )

· T prep + ˜O (s) · T query .

# 4.3 Adaptive Data Analysis 

Adaptive data analysis refers to the common scenario where an analyst makes a sequence of queries on a dataset, with each query potentially depending on the outcomes of previous ones. This adaptivity arises in many real-world workflows, such as scientific discovery, online experimentation, and iterative model development. Although the dataset itself may be static, classical statistical methods can still break down in adaptive settings, as they typically assume queries are fixed beforehand. Hence, the setting is inherently related to adversarial robustness and there has been a large body of work exploring attacks and robust algorithms [ HU14 , DFH +15b , DFH +15a , SU15 ,BNS +16 , FS18 , CN20 , CLN +22 , KSS22 , CSW +23 , CNSS23 , DSWZ23 , NST23 , CNS +24 , FFL +25 ]. Let X be a data domain and let S ∈ X n be a dataset drawn i.i.d. from an unknown distribution 

D. In adaptive data analysis, an analyst interacts with an algorithm over multiple rounds. In each – 107 – round t, the analyst selects a query qt : X → [0 , 1] , where the choice of qt may depend arbitrarily on all previous queries and answers. The algorithm returns an answer at, possibly randomized, based on S and the interaction history. The goal is to ensure that, with high probability, the answers at

remain close to the corresponding population values E 

> x∼D

[qt(x)] for all adaptively chosen queries. 

4.3.1 Simple Attacks on Empirical Means 

To motivate the formal lower bounds for adaptive data analysis, we first describe two fundamental attacks. These examples demonstrate how an analyst, by choosing queries based on previous results, can force a simple algorithm, namely, the algorithm that merely returns empirical means, to output answers that fail to generalize to the underlying distribution D.

The Decoding Attack (High Precision). When an algorithm provides the empirical mean of linear queries with arbitrarily high precision, an analyst can effectively “decode” the entire sample 

S = {x1, . . . , x n} in a very small number of steps. For instance, if the data points xi are bits, the analyst can query the empirical mean of q(xi) = 2 i. The resulting answer a = 1 

> n
> ∑ni=1

2ixi is a value whose binary representation reveals every xi in the sample. Once the sample is decoded, the analyst can define a “targeted” counting query qtarget (x) = 

I[x ∈ S]. The empirical mean on the sample is exactly 1, yet for any sufficiently large domain, the true expectation E 

> x∼D

[qtarget (x)] will be nearly 0. This shows that even a single high-precision answer allows an adaptive analyst to construct a query with maximal generalization error. 

The Correlation Attack (Linear Number of Queries). If the analyst is restricted to counting queries, i.e., q(x) ∈ { 0, 1}, and the algorithm returns empirical means, a similar attack is possible using a number of queries linear in the sample size n. In this attack, the analyst first issues k = O (n)

random counting queries q1, . . . , q k and receives their empirical means a1, . . . , a k.By comparing each answer aj to its known population mean μj , the analyst can identify which queries happen to have a positive or negative bias on the specific sample S. The analyst can then construct a new query Q(x) = ∑kj=1 wj qj (x), where the weights wj are chosen to correlate with the sampling error, e.g., wj = sign (aj − μj ). This constructed query Q will, by design, have an empirical mean significantly higher than its true expectation. These simple attacks illustrate that adaptivity allows an analyst to “search” for the sampling noise inherent in any finite dataset. While the fingerprinting attack discussed in the following sections provides a more general O (n2) lower bound for any mechanism, these basic examples highlight why standard empirical estimation is fundamentally insecure over adaptive interactions. 

4.3.2 The Streaming Adaptive Data Analysis (SADA) Problem 

In this section, we present a result by [ KMNS21 ] that uses adaptive data analysis to demonstrate a separation between non-adaptive insertion-only data streams and adversarial insertion-only data streams. Namely, they constructed a streaming problem that admits an efficient oblivious algorithm, but any robust algorithm must use polynomially larger space. Throughout this section, we follow the notation and exposition of [ KMNS21 ]. We first present a number of necessary preliminaries for adaptive data analysis. Recall the definition of the accuracy game Acc , as defined by [KMNS21]. – 108 – Algorithm 10 Accuracy game Acc n,ℓ, M,A 

> 1:

Input: A dataset S ∈ Xn. 

> 2:

for i = 1 to ℓ do  

> 3:

The adversary A adaptively selects a statistical query qi. 

> 4:

The mechanism M uses S to compute an answer zi.

The answer zi is disclosed to the adversary A.

Pseudorandom generators. We next recall a number of results regarding information-theoretic security of pseudorandom generators in the bounded storage model , i.e., against adversaries with bounded space. Per the exposition of [ KMNS21 ], we give the formulation presented by Vad-han [Vad04]. In the bounded storage model, a short secret seed K ∈ { 0, 1}b (hidden from the adversary) is used alongside a long sequence of public random bits X1, X 2, . . . (accessible to all parties). A 

bounded storage model (BSM) pseudorandom generator is a function 

PRG : {0, 1}a × { 0, 1}b → { 0, 1}c,

where typically b, c ≪ a. Two honest parties begin with a shared seed K ∈ { 0, 1}b hidden from the adversary. At each time step t ∈ [T ], the next a bits from the public stream, (X(t−1) a, . . . , X ta ), are revealed. Although the adversary observes this stream, it is limited in memory and cannot retain the entire sequence. The honest parties apply the pseudorandom generator to the observed bits and the seed K to produce an output Yt ∈ { 0, 1}c of pseudorandom bits via PRG (·, K ).To define security in this setting, suppose the adversary has storage capacity limited to βa bits. Let St ∈ { 0, 1}βa denote the adversary’s internal state at time t. Informally, security is defined by comparing two scenarios: the real setting, where PRG is applied, and an ideal setting, where the output is truly random. The adversary A updates its state and, based on its stored information, attempts to distinguish between these two cases at the end of the process. Observe that at every time step, the adversary is granted access to all previously generated values Yi without this access counting toward its storage limit. Additionally, at the final step, the adversary is also given the key K.

Definition 4.3.1. [ Vad04 ] Given β ∈ [0 , 1] , we say that PRG : {0, 1}a × { 0, 1}b → { 0, 1}c is an 

ε-secure BSM pseudorandom generator against storage rate β if, for any adversary A with at most 

βa bits of memory and for every T ∈ N, the distinguishing advantage between the real and ideal experiments is at most T ε . That is, 

|Pr r [A (Y1, . . . , Y T , S T , K ) = 1] − Pr i [A (Y1, . . . , Y T , S T , K ) = 1] | ≤ T · ε, 

where Pr r denotes the probability over the real experiment and Pr i denotes the probability over the ideal experiment. 

We recall a result of [ Vad04 ], which gives a construction of a BSM PRG with the following guarantees: 

Theorem 4.3.2. [ Vad04 ] For every ε > exp 

> (

−a/ 2O(log ∗ a))

, every a ∈ N, and every c ≤ a/ 4, there exists a BSM pseudorandom generator PRG : {0, 1}a × { 0, 1}b → { 0, 1}c satisfying the following properties: 

– 109 – Real Experiment: 

• Let X = ( X1, X 2, . . . , X T a ) be a sequence of uniformly random bits, let the secret key be 

K ← { 0, 1}b, and initialize the adversary’s memory state as S0 = 0 βa .

• For each round t = 1 , . . . , T :

◦ Compute the pseudorandom output Yt = PRG

X(t−1) a+1 , . . . , X ta , K 

> )

◦ Update the adversary’s state as St = A

• Finally, the adversary outputs A (Y1, . . . , Y T , S T , K ) ∈ { 0, 1}.

(1) The generator PRG is ε-secure against adversaries with storage rate β ≤ 12 .(2) The key length is b = O (log aε

We now define the Streaming Adaptive Data Analysis (SADA) problem introduced by [ KMNS21 ], who showed a strong positive result for this problem in the oblivious setting and a strong negative result in the adversarial setting. Again, we follow the exposition of [ KMNS21 ] throughout the section. Let X = {0, 1}d × { 0, 1}b denote the data domain, let γ ≥ 0 be a fixed constant, and let 

PRG : {0, 1}a × { 0, 1}b → { 0, 1}c be a BSM pseudorandom generator with output length c = 1 .At each time step i ∈ [m], the stream provides an update xi = ( pi, k i) ∈ X. The first n updates, 

x1, . . . , x n, are interpreted as pairs of “data points” and their associated “keys”. We let S denote the multiset formed from these initial n updates. Additionally, for technical purposes, S is augmented with γn  

> 1−γ

copies of a special placeholder element ⊥. This multiset S remains fixed after time step n.– 110 – Starting at time step j = n + 1 , each block of (a + 1) · 2d updates specifies a new “function” (or “query” in the context of the adaptive data analysis problem) that the streaming algorithm must evaluate over the fixed multiset S. Specifically: 

• For each p ∈ { 0, 1}d (processed in lexicographic order), perform the following steps: (1) Let u1, . . . , u a ∈ { 0, 1} be the next a stream updates, and define Γp ∈ { 0, 1}a = u1 ◦. . . ◦ua

to be the corresponding bitstring. (2) Let σ be the following stream update. (3) For every k ∈ { 0, 1}b, compute Yk = PRG (Γ p, k ) and define the function value f (p, k ) = 

σ ⊕ Yk.

• In addition, define f (⊥) = 1 .Observe that these updates implicitly define a truth table for the function f : {0, 1}d×{ 0, 1}b → { 0, 1}.Using these truth tables, the SADA problem is defined as follows: 

Definition 4.3.3 (The (a, b, d, m, n, γ )-SADA Problem) . At the end of each block that defines a function f , the streaming algorithm must compute or approximate the average of f on the multiset S,i.e., 1 

> |S|
> ∑
> xi=( pi,k i)∈S

f (pi, k i). For the other time steps, the algorithm is permitted arbitrary output. Here, m is the total number of updates, i.e., the length of the stream, n is the total number of data points in S, γ is a small constant, and a, b, and d are the parameters defining the domain and the 

PRG .

The main point of the design is to link the query’s output to a pseudorandom bit derived from a hidden key, making it difficult for an algorithm to learn about the underlying data without knowing the key. 

An oblivious algorithm for the SADA problem. We briefly describe a streaming algorithm for the SADA problem in the oblivious setting. The algorithm performs as follows. For s =

Theorem 4.3.4. [KMNS21 ] For constant α, β, γ , there exists an algorithm that uses space O (( b + d) log m)

and is (α, β )-accurate for the SADA problem on a non-adaptive data stream. Proof. For Q ≤ m, let f1, . . . , f Q be the non-adaptive queries fixed by the data stream. Finally, we remark that if the SADA problem is instantiated using the PRG of Theorem 4.3.2, then the PRG itself uses O (b + d) bits to store. Therefore for constant α and β, the total memory required is O (( b + d) log m) bits. 

4.3.3 An Impossibility Result for Adaptive Streaming 

In this section, we show that an adversarially-robust streaming algorithm for the SADA problem requires significantly more space than a streaming algorithm for the SADA problem in the non-adaptive setting. The underlying intuition is as follows. Any space-bounded streaming algorithm can only retain a small subset of the elements it processes and must effectively forget the rest of the stream. An adaptive adversary can then define queries over the entire dataset. Because the algorithmic outputs are based only on the limited subset it remembers, it quickly overfits to those elements and fails to answer the adaptive queries correctly. In contrast, in the non-adaptive setting an oblivious algorithm is easy to construct: standard concentration bounds together with a union bound imply that storing only a logarithmic number of elements already suffices. We now formalize this notion. Suppose there exists a streaming algorithm A that is robust against adversarial inputs for the SADA problem. We describe in Algorithm AnswerQueries , c.f., Algorithm 11, how to use A to build an algorithm that takes as input a dataset P of n elements from {0, 1}d and is able to respond to a sequence of adaptively chosen queries of the form q : {0, 1}d → { 0, 1}.We now show that if A is accurate for the SADA problem, then AnswerQueries (with the blue text) outputs a good estimate of the average of the query q on the input dataset P . This fact shall ultimately be used to show a contradiction to the adaptive data analysis problem. 

Lemma 4.3.5. [ KMNS21 ] Suppose A is (α, β )-accurate for the SADA problem. Then the simplified instance of AnswerQueries is   

> (α
> 1−γ

, β 

> )

-accurate over m−n 

> (a+1) ·2d

adaptive queries, with respect to an input dataset of size n.Proof. Let q be the query for a fixed iteration, corresponding to a function f whose truth table is given to A. Observe that for every pair (p, k ), we have f (p, k ) = q(p) by construction. In particular, the answers produced by A are α-accurate with respect to the extended dataset P ∪ {⊥ , . . . , ⊥} 

with high probability. Since at most γ fraction of this extended dataset consists of ⊥, then the algorithm is α 

> 1−γ

-accurate with respect to the original dataset P .

Definition 4.3.6. [DFH +15a ] A mechanism M that supports ℓ queries on a dataset of size n is
