We now recall the following impossibility result for the adaptive data analysis (ADA) problem. Consider a mechanism M designed for the ADA setting, which receives an input sample P =(p1, . . . , p n) and is required to answer a sequence of adaptive queries. It is known that if M produces its responses based solely on the evaluations of each query q on the sample points, i.e., as a function of the values q(p1), . . . , q (pn), then, in general, it cannot reliably answer more than n2 adaptively chosen queries. We call such a mechanism a natural mechanism. – 114 – Definition 4.3.10. [HU14 ] An algorithm that receives a sample P and responds to queries q is called natural if the following holds: For any sample P and any pair of queries q and q′ such that 

q(p) = q′(p) for all p ∈ P , the algorithm produces the same output on q and q′. That is, the outputs 

z and z′ are equal if the algorithm is deterministic, and identically distributed if the algorithm is randomized. If the algorithm maintains internal state, then this requirement must hold regardless of its current state. 

We recall the following impossibility result for adaptive data analysis by Steinke and Ull-man [ SU15 ] though we observe that less optimal impossibility results [ HU14 , USN +18 ]. These attacks thus show that storing a small set of elements cannot answer a large number of adaptive queries about the whole universe. 

Theorem 4.3.11. [SU15 ] There exists an absolute constant c ∈ (0 , 1) such that no natural algorithms can be (c, c )-statistically-accurate for O (n2) adaptive queries, given n samples over a universe of size Ω( n).

As shown earlier in Lemma 4.3.9, the algorithm AnswerQueries is statistically accurate for 

ℓ = m−n 

> (a+1) ·2d

adaptively chosen queries, where ℓ can be made larger than n2 by choosing m sufficiently large. To show, we aim to apply Theorem 4.3.11. However, the algorithm AnswerQueries is not strictly a natural algorithm since the output of the streaming algorithm A might depend on the values of the query function outside the sample P . To address this, we modify AnswerQueries to ensure it becomes a natural algorithm, c.f., Algorithm 11 using the red text instead. 

Lemma 4.3.12. [KMNS21] Algorithm AnswerQueries (using the red text) is natural. Proof sketch. The claim follows from the fact that the values of the queries outside the input sample 

P are entirely concealed from algorithm A, effectively using the classical one-time pad encryption scheme. Additionally, note that the output z produced by AnswerQueries using the red text for a query q is fully determined by the internal state of algorithm A following the completion of the corresponding iteration of Step 5. We now show that the modification to AnswerQueries in the red text has a negligible effect on the correctness of the execution and thus is both natural and accurate, ultimately leading to a contradiction to known adaptive data analysis impossibility results.

# Turnstile Streams 

- -

For many fundamental problems, any linear sketch can be broken with a polynomial length stream with insertions and deletions. 

In this chapter, we continue to study the black-box adversarial model. However, we depart from the insertion-only setting discussed in the previous chapters. Instead, we focus on turnstile streams, also called insertion-deletion streams, where updates may either increase or decrease coordinates of an underlying frequency vector. Crucially, many of the previous frameworks, such as sketch switching, bounded computation paths, difference estimators, or differential privacy, have good performance on insertion-only streams because they have explicit dependencies on the flip number of the stream, c.f., Definition 3.3.1, and Lemma 3.3.7 shows that the flip number must be small for insertion-only streams. In particular, for a stream of length m = poly (n), the (ε, m )-flip number of many interesting problems such as norm/moment estimation or distinct element estimation is at most O

( 1 

> ε

log n

)

. This is not necessarily the case for turnstile streams, where the (ε, m )-flip number can be as large as m. Thus although the results in the previous sections can still be used for turnstile streams, their resulting bounds may be polynomial in m, which is undesirable. In this section, we study whether better adversarially robust algorithms can be designed for the turnstile setting. 

# 5.1 Attack on Specific Sketches 

In this section, we analyze adversarial attacks on standard linear sketches within the streaming model. In Section 5.1.1, we demonstrate that the Gaussian sketch for L2 estimation with r rows remains robust against O (r) adaptive queries, meaning it continues to return a constant-factor approximation under such adaptivity. This implies that any successful attack on a linear sketch must make at least Ω( r) adaptive queries. Subsequently, in Section 5.1.2, we construct a general attack against norm-based estimators using only O (r) adaptive queries, thereby establishing that 

Θ( r) queries are both necessary and sufficient in this setting. – 119 – 5.1.1 Robustness of Gaussian Sketches 

To establish the existence of a linear sketch that remains accurate under O (r) adaptive queries, we first recall a well-known result establishing that a scaled Gaussian matrix A ∈ Rm×n serves as an 

L2 subspace embedding for sufficiently large m = O (n), guaranteeing that ∥Ax ∥2 ≈ ∥ x∥2 holds simultaneously for all x ∈ Rn, as stated for instance in Theorem 2.3 of [Woo14]. 

Theorem 5.1.1. Let M ∈ Rn×d be a matrix and let ε ∈ (0 , 1) be a desired approximation parameter. Suppose A ∈ Rm×n is a random matrix whose entries are drawn independently from the normal distribution N

> (

0, 1

> m
> )

. If m = O 

> (d+log(1 /δ )
> ε2
> )

, then with probability at least 1 − δ, the matrix AM is a (1 + ε) subspace embedding for M.

Moreover, when m = O  

> (1
> ε2

log 1

> δ
> )

, it holds for any fixed x ∈ Rd that 

Pr 

> [

(1 − ε)∥x∥22 ≤ ∥ Ax ∥22 ≤ (1 + ε)∥x∥22

> ]

≥ 1 − δ. 

The classical Gaussian sketch sets δ small enough to ensure correctness over many queries using a standard union bound. We prove that the Gaussian sketch remains accurate against O (r) adaptive queries, even when the adversary is given full access to the sketch responses Ax ∈ Rm, not just the norm ∥Ax ∥2. After 

O (r) queries, the adversary learns only an O (r) × O (r) submatrix M of A, since any adaptive query sequence can be simulated by queries on standard basis vectors. Importantly, the submatrix 

M is itself Gaussian and thus forms a valid subspace embedding for vectors supported on its rows. Because subspace embeddings guarantee norm preservation for all vectors in the relevant subspace, we have ∥Mz ∥2 ≈ ∥ z∥2 for all z supported on the corresponding coordinates. The adversary gains no information about the remaining rows of A, and so cannot exploit them. Thus, the sketch continues to yield a constant-factor approximation of the L2 norm for any adaptive query x.

Theorem 5.1.2. Any attack that finds x such that ∥Ax ∥2 /∈  

> (∥x∥2
> 2

, 2∥x∥2

> )

with probability at least  

> 910

requires Ω( r) adaptive queries. Proof. Let A ∈ Rr×n be a random Gaussian matrix. Suppose an adversary is allowed to adaptively query the sketch and receives both Ax (i) and ∥Ax (i)∥2 for each query x(i), for i ∈ [t], where t = cr 

for some sufficiently small constant c < 1.We can assume without loss of generality that the queries x(1) , . . . , x(t) form an orthonormal sequence. Indeed, any query can be decomposed into a component orthogonal to the previous ones and a component lying in their span. The response to the latter can be inferred from previous outputs, and the orthogonal component can be normalized and used as the new query. Further, since the adversary’s queries can be spanned by elementary vectors, we may assume 

x(i) = ei for all i ∈ [t]. Letting X = x(1) ◦ . . . ◦ x(t), the matrix AX ∈ Rr×t is a submatrix of A, and due to the rotational invariance of Gaussian distributions, it remains a random Gaussian matrix. According to Theorem 5.1.1, with high probability (at least 0.99 ), this submatrix is a constant-factor subspace embedding for Rt, assuming r = O (t). Therefore, for all y ∈ Rt, we have: 

99 100 ∥y∥22 ≤ ∥ AXy ∥22 ≤ 101 100 ∥y∥22.

This implies that for any vector w ∈ Rn that lies within the span of {e1, . . . , et}, we also have: 

99 100 ∥w∥22 ≤ ∥ Aw ∥22 ≤ 101 100 ∥w∥22.

– 120 – Now consider any vector z ∈ Rn orthogonal to the span of the previous queries. Since the adversary lacks any information about the corresponding rows of A, standard concentration bounds imply that with probability at least 0.9:

99 100 ∥z∥22 ≤ ∥ Az ∥22 ≤ 101 100 ∥z∥22.

Furthermore, due to this independence, the cross term 2⟨Ay , Az ⟩ concentrates around zero, and for sufficiently large r, satisfies |2⟨Ay , Az ⟩| ≤ 1100 (∥y∥22 + ∥z∥22) with high probability. Hence, for the final adaptive query x = y + z, composed of known and unknown components, we can expand ∥Ax ∥22 = ∥Ay ∥22 + ∥Az ∥22 + 2 ⟨Ay , Az ⟩ to obtain: 

Pr  

> [

98 100 ∥x∥2 ≤ ∥ Ax ∥2 ≤ 102 100 ∥x∥2

> ]

≥ 910 .

This demonstrates that the sketch still yields an accurate approximation to ∥x∥2 with high probability, contradicting the possibility of a successful attack within t = O (r) queries. 

5.1.2 Attack on Norm-Based Estimators 

In this section, we give an attack on norm-based estimators, which use a sketch matrix A ∈ Rr×n to maintain Ax for the underlying frequency vector x ∈ Rn, and then returns the fixed function ∥Ax ∥22.Given a linear sketch with r rows, our attack focuses only on the first Cr coordinates. Therefore, we may assume without loss of generality that A ∈ Rr×Cr . The attack proceeds as follows. We sample a random subset S ⊆ [Cr ] and construct a vector x ∈ RCr such that xj = 0 for j / ∈ S, and 

xj takes a uniformly random sign for j ∈ S. Next, we construct a vector y ∈ RCr satisfying yj = 0 

for j ∈ S, and for each j / ∈ S, we set the sign of yj to align with the sign of the bias in the inner product ⟨Ax , Ae j ⟩. The complete algorithm is presented in Algorithm 12. 

Algorithm 12 Adversarial Attack on Norm-Based Estimators 

Input: Black-box access to a norm-based estimator A over inputs in RCr 

Output: A vector z on which A fails to approximate ∥z∥22 within a 1 + Ω(1) factor  

> 1:

Sample a random sign vector x ∈ RCr  

> 2:

Query A(x) 

> 3:

for each i ∈ [Cr ] do  

> 4:

Query A(ei) and A(x + ei) 

> 5:

Set yi ← 12 (A(x + ei) − A (ei) − A (x)) ▷y i = ⟨Ax , Ae i⟩ 

> 6:

Query A(y) 

> 7:

if any query has failed then  

> 8:

Set z to be the corresponding input vector  

> 9:

else  

> 10:

Set z ← x + y 

> 11:

return z

In this section, we study the setting where the sketching matrix A ∈ Rr×n has all nonzero subdeterminants lower bounded by 1poly( r) , which is a property satisfied by all known sketching algorithms. We emphasize that we focus on linear sketches in this section, as opposed to integer sketches. Thus, the results in this section are analogous to those of Section 5.3 We describe the following result by [GLW +24]. 

Theorem 5.2.1. [GLW +24 ] Let A be a linear sketching algorithm with sketching matrix A ∈ Rr×n,and suppose all nonzero subdeterminants of A are at least 1poly( r) . Suppose A uses an estimator 

f : Rr×n × Rr → {− 1, +1 } to solve the (α + c, β − c)-gap F0 problem for constants α, β, c , and returns f (A, Ax ) on each input x.Then, there exists a randomized algorithm that, with high constant probability and using at most 

poly (r) adaptive queries to A, finds a distribution D over Rn such that A fails on D with constant probability. Moreover, this algorithm runs in poly( r) time. 

The analysis of [ GLW +24 ] follows a similar attack strategy as in the integer-valued case x ∈ Zn,aiming to identify the significant columns of A through carefully chosen queries. However, since A

is now real-valued, we need to redefine the notion of column significance and design new hard input distributions for the insignificant coordinates. In particular, we say column i is significant if there exists y⊤ ∈ Rr such that 

(y⊤A)2 

> i

≥ 1

s · ∥ y⊤A∥22.

To handle the real-valued case, we show how to iteratively eliminate a small number of columns so that the resulting matrix A′ ∈ Rr×n′

has all leverage scores at most 1 

> s

, c.f., Definition 2.1. Due to real-valued entries in the matrix A, the earlier information-theoretic arguments from Section 5.5.2 no longer apply. Instead, we rely on a volume-based argument. To that end, observe the total leverage score is at most r, so one might hope to remove at most rs columns since each removed column will have leverage score at least 1 

> s

. However, this is complicated by the fact that leverage scores can increase when other columns are zeroed out. To manage this, [ GLW +24 ] uses a more refined analysis based on volume change, previously applied to online leverage score bounds [CMP20, BDM +20]. 

Lemma 5.2.2 (Matrix determinant lemma) . For any vector u ∈ Rd and matrix M ∈ Rd×d, we have 

det( M + uu ⊤) = det( M) · (1 + u⊤M−1u).

[GLW +24 ] shows that for matrices with both bounded entries and bounded subdeterminants, only a bounded number of high-leverage columns need to be eliminated before the remaining ones have low leverage scores. This includes the important case of integer matrices with bounded entries. We also note that for general matrices with entries represented using b bits, rescaling can convert them into integer matrices with entries of magnitude at most 2b.We further remark that while the lemma below is stated for matrices whose subdeterminants are at least 1poly( r) , the result naturally generalizes to matrices with subdeterminants lower bounded by any constant κ > 0. In such cases, the number of columns removed increases to O (r2s log( κnr )).For example, if the subdeterminants are at least 1 

> npoly( r)

, this results in removing s · poly (r) · log n

columns. – 125 – Lemma 5.2.3. [GLW +24 ] Let A ∈ Rr×n be a matrix with entries bounded by poly (r) and all subdeterminants either zero or at least 1poly( r) . # 5.3 Lower Bound for Fp Estimation with Real-Valued Linear Sketches 

Although the results in Section 4.1 show that there exist non-trivial algorithms that achieve robustness on turnstile streams of length m using ˜O (√m) space, this is still quite far from non-adaptive turnstile streams that solve the corresponding problems using polylog (m) space. Thus it is natural to ask whether there is an inherent limitation for adversarial inputs on turnstile streams. A common technique for algorithmic design for non-adaptive turnstile streams is the use of linear sketches . The general approach involves defining a distribution Π over linear mappings A : Rn → Rr,where r ≪ n. A matrix A is sampled from Π, and during the online phase, when a vector x ∈ Rn is – 131 – updated, the algorithm maintains the sketch Ax . This yields a compact representation of x, which enables approximate answers to a variety of queries about x, given various choices of post-processing on the sketch vector Ax . Indeed, linear sketches have been used for distance estimation [ Joh84 ], distinct element estimation [ KNW10b ], norm/moment estimation [ AMS99 , IW05 , Ind06 , KNW10a ,AKO11 , GW18 ], heavy-hitters [ CCF04 , CM05 ], compressed sensing [ PW11 , PW13 ], subspace embeddings [ CW13 , Woo14 ], eigenvalue estimation and PSD testing [ NSW22 , SW23 ]. In this section, we present a result by [ HW13 ] that shows linear sketches are not robust to adaptive queries: 

Theorem 5.3.1 (Informal version of Theorem 5.3.19) . [ HW13 ] Given a parameter B > 2 and oracle access to a linear sketch consisting of at most r = n − O (log( nB )) rows, there exists a randomized algorithm that with high probability, constructs a distribution over inputs on which the sketch fails to provide a B-approximation to ∥v∥22, where v is the frequency vector defined by the (adaptive) turnstile stream. The algorithm performs at most poly (rB ) queries to the oracle, with each query chosen adaptively, and the total runtime is bounded by poly (rB ). Furthermore, the adaptivity is structured: the full set of queries can be divided into at most r batches, each consisting of non-adaptive queries. Thus, the algorithm operates in at most r rounds of adaptivity. 

5.3.1 Overview of Attack on Real-Valued Linear Sketches 

The attack is established by analyzing a communication protocol between two entities, referred to as Alice and Bob. Alice selects a matrix A ∈ Rr×n drawn from a distribution π. Bob submits a sequence of queries x1, . . . , xs ∈ Rn, and for each query xi, Alice observes only the sketch Ax i and returns a value f (Ax i), where f is an arbitrary deterministic function (this assumption is removed in Section 5.3.4.3). Bob’s objective is to compute the row space R(A) of the matrix A, which is an 

r-dimensional subspace of Rn.If R(A) were known, Bob could alternate between submitting the zero vector and a vector from the kernel of A, thereby creating two indistinguishable cases for Alice that lead to significantly different outputs. This would force any approximation algorithm to fail under a relative error metric. The core result presents an efficient algorithm that enables Bob to recover r − O (1) orthonormal vectors that are nearly contained in R(A), thereby constraining the effective dimension of Alice’s sketch and increasing the likelihood of error on future inputs. 

The Conditional Expectation Lemma. To begin identifying R(A), Bob samples a query from the multivariate Gaussian distribution N (0 , τ In), where τ > 0 is a scalar and In is the identity matrix. This ensures that Alice’s observation, i.e., the projection PAx of x onto R(A), is spherically symmetric and determined solely by the norm ∥PAx∥2. Alice must respond based on this projection, attempting to infer the norm of x and outputting a binary decision. The key insight is that when x has a non-trivial component in R(A), the observed norm ∥PAx∥2

may be slightly larger than its expected value, potentially leading Alice to overestimate the norm of 

x. [ HW13 ] proves a conditional expectation lemma, which guarantees the existence of a choice of τ

such that 

E  

> x∼N(0 ,τ In)
> [

∥PAx∥22 | f (Ax ) = 1 

> ]

− E  

> x∼N(0 ,τ In)
> [

∥PAx∥22

> ]

is non-negligible. This result is derived by considering the aggregate contribution over a range 

τ ∈ [1 , B ], where B denotes the desired approximation factor. For each value v = ∥PAx∥22, the – 132 – probability q(v) of outputting 1 affects the conditional expectation difference. By analyzing the weight of v under the corresponding χ2 distribution, [ HU14 ] shows that the overall sum is significantly positive, indicating that some values of τ produce meaningful gaps in the expectations. Moreover, correctness assumptions imply that q(v) is small for small v and large for large v, further amplifying the net positive contribution. 

Boosting correlation. After identifying a collection of queries x1, . . . , xm that exhibit slightly higher correlations with R(A), these vectors are aggregated into a matrix G ∈ Rm×n. The top right singular vector v∗ of G is then computed. This step, which is computationally efficient, yields a unit vector that is nearly contained in R(A); formally, ∥PAv∗∥2 ≥ 1 − 1poly( n) . This allows the dimension of Alice’s effective sketch space to be reduced by one, laying the groundwork for iterative refinement. 

Iterative refinement. Identifying a single direction in R(A) is not sufficient, as Alice may dynamically adjust the component of the sketching matrix used in the post-processing function over the course of the data stream. Therefore, the procedure is repeated: Bob generates new queries drawn from a Gaussian distribution restricted to the orthogonal complement of the subspace spanned by the previously recovered vectors. Each iteration reduces the dimension of R(A) by one. This iterative attack introduces additional challenges. The recovered vectors are only approxi-mately aligned with R(A), and such approximation errors could, in theory, be exploited by Alice. To mitigate this, global Gaussian noise is added to each query to ensure that the query distribution remains statistically indistinguishable from one generated using vectors fully contained in R(A). A generalized version of the conditional expectation lemma is then invoked to guarantee the robustness of this approach in the presence of noise.

This section introduces the foundational components required to establish the conditional expectation lemma. 5.3.3 Conditional Expectation Lemma 

The key analytic tool for the attack on real-valued linear sketches by [ HW13 ] is the so-called 

conditional expectation lemma . Intuitively, it shows that the attack can find a distribution over inputs that have a non-trivially large correlation with the unknown subspace used by the linear sketch. Formally, let U ⊆ Rn be a fixed d-dimensional linear subspace, where d is at least some sufficiently large constant. We suppose the linear sketch uses a post-processing function f : Rn → { 0, 1} which satisfies the identity f (x) = f (PUx) for all x ∈ Rn.

Definition 5.3.3 (Subspace Gaussian) . Let U ⊆ Rn be a linear subspace of Rn. We call a family of distributions G(U) = {gτ }τ ∈(0 ,∞) is a subspace Gaussian family if: (1) PUgτ is distributed like a standard Gaussian variable inside U, with E [∥PUgτ ∥2] = τ .(2) PU⊥ gτ is a spherical Gaussian distribution that does not depend on τ .(3) PU⊥ gτ is statistically independent of PUgτ .

The following claim shows that the norm is a sufficient statistic to characterize a subspace Gaussian family. 

Lemma 5.3.4. [ HW13 ] For a subspace Gaussian family G(U) = {gτ }τ , the norm ∥PUgτ ∥2 is a sufficient statistic. Specifically, for every s > 0, the distribution of gτ is independent of τ ,conditioned on s = ∥PUgτ ∥2.Proof. First, observe that we can decompose gτ = g1 + g2, where g1 is a Gaussian distribution supported on U and g2 is some Gaussian distribution independent of τ supported on U ⊥. By rotational invariance of both g1 and g2, we may assume without loss of generality that U is a coordinate subspace. In particular, we may assume U is the first d = dim (U) coordinates of the standard basis. Because g2 is independent of gτ and supported on a disjoint set of coordinates, it suffices to verify the claim for g1. By the factorization theorem for sufficient statistics [ CP97 ], a statistic T (x) is sufficient for a parameter τ if and only if the density pτ (x) can be written as 

pτ (x) = f (x) hτ (T (x)) ,

where f does not depend on τ . In our case, the Gaussian density depends on x only through ∥x∥2,so taking T (x) = ∥x∥2 gives the desired factorization. Note that this follows directly from the fact that the Gaussian density at a point x depends only on ∥x∥2, and so the desired claim holds. – 134 – The following soundness definition captures the notion that the output of the linear sketch should evaluate to 1 on inputs that have large norm and 0 on inputs that have small norm. 

Definition 5.3.5 (Soundness) . We say that a function f : U → { 0, 1}, where dim (U) = d, is 

B-sound for a subspace Gaussian family G(U) if: (1) ∫ 2Bd Bd/ 2 E [f (gτ ) | ∥ PUgτ ∥2 = s] ds ≥ 1

5.3.3.1 Noisy Orthogonal Complements 

In this section, we extend the Conditional Expectation Lemma to a family of distributions that is subspace Gaussian on a subspace of A, rather than subspace Gaussian on the entirety of A.Specifically, we consider the family of distributions that is subspace Gaussian on A ∩ V⊥, where 

V ⊆ A is a linear subspace of A.Each distribution in this family is parameterized by a subspace V and a variance σ2, intuitively corresponding to a Gaussian distribution on the subspace V⊥ with variance σ2, plus a small Gaussian supported on all of Rn of constant variance, independent of σ2. Formally, we define: – 136 – Definition 5.3.8. Let σ > 0 be a parameter, V ⊆ A be a subspace of A of dimension t ≤ r − 1. Let 

G(V⊥, σ 2) be the distribution obtained from sampling g1 ∼ N (0 , σ 2)n, g 2 ∼ N (0 , 1/4) n independently and outputting g = PV⊥ g1 + g2.Furthermore, for d = r − t, we define the family of distributions G(A ∩ V⊥) = {gτ } by setting gτ = PAg, for g ∼ G(V⊥, τ /d − 1/4) if τd > 14 and otherwise setting gτ = PAg where 

g ∼ G(V⊥, τ /d ).

We first show that G(A ∩ V⊥) is subspace Gaussian. 

Lemma 5.3.9. [HW13] G(A ∩ V⊥) is a subspace Gaussian family. Proof. Let U = A ∩ V⊥. For τd ≤ 14 , we have that E [∥PUgτ ∥2] = d · τd = τ . Moreover, since 

gτ ∼ G(V⊥, τ /d ) and U ⊆ V⊥, then gτ is orthogonal to U⊥, so that ∥PU⊥ gτ ∥ = 0 , as required. For

is a subspace Gaussian family. We now formalize the notion of correctness for our linear sketching algorithm f for inputs drawn from the distribution G(V⊥, σ 2).

Definition 5.3.10 (Correctness) . Let A be a subspace and V ⊆ A be a subspace with d =dim( V⊥ ∩ A). We call a function f : A → { 0, 1} to be (ε, B )-correct on V⊥:(1) For all σ2 ∈ [B/ 2, 2B] and g ∼ G(V⊥, σ 2), we have 

Pr [f (g) = 1] ≥ 1 − ε. 

(2) For all σ2 ∈ [0 , 2] and g ∼ G(V⊥, σ 2), we have 

Pr [f (g) = 1] ≤ ε. 

We simply call f to be B-correct on V⊥ if it is (ε, B )-correct for some ε ≤ 110( Bd )2 .

We now relate the correctness and soundness definitions.

5.3.3.2 Distance Between Subspaces

Definition 5.3.13 (Distance between subspaces) . We define the distance between subspaces V, W ⊆

Rn by 

d(V, W) = ∥PV − PW∥2 := sup

In this section, we show that no linear sketching post-processing function f : Rn → { 0, 1} that depends only on a lower dimensional subspace can correctly predict the F2-moment up to a multiplicative factor B on a polynomial number of adaptive queries. We emphasize that B can be any parameter, but the complexity of the attack depends on B, as well as the dimension of the subspace. Formally, the following definition characterizes the ability of the attack to induce failure from the linear sketch. 

Definition 5.3.16 (Failure certificate) . Let B ≥ 8 and let f : Rn → { 0, 1}. We call a pair (V, σ 2)

a d-dimensional failure certificate for f if V ⊆ Rn is d-dimensional subspace and σ2 ∈ [0 , 2B] such that for some constant C > 0, we have: 

• n ≥ d + 10 C log( Bn )

• Either σ2 ∈  

> [B
> 2

, 50 B

> ]

and Pr 

> g∼G(V⊥,σ 2)

[f (g) = 1] ≤ 1 − 1(Bn )C

• or σ2 ≤ 2 and Pr 

> g∼G(V⊥,σ 2)

[f (g) = 1] ≥ 1 

> nC

.

We now formally define the following formulation of the GapNorm problem. 

Definition 5.3.17 (GapNorm (B, α ) promise problem) . Given any fixed B ≥ 8 and α = poly (n),we say that an algorithm A solves the problem GapNorm (B, α ) if it satisfies the following condition: for every input vector x ∈ Zn, the algorithm outputs 1 whenever ∥x∥22 ≥ α · B, and outputs 0

whenever ∥x∥22 ≤ α. If ∥x∥22 falls within the interval (α, α · B), then A is permitted to return either 

0 or 1.

The intuition for the definition of a failure certificate is given by the next simple fact, which shows that a failure certificate corresponds to a distribution on which the linear sketching algorithm 

f does not decide the following formulation of the GapNorm problem up to a multiplicative factor 

Ω( B) on a polynomial number of queries. 

Fact 5.3.18. [ HW13 ] Given a d-dimensional failure certificate for a linear sketching algorithm f ,we can use poly (Bn ) non-adaptive queries and with probability at least 2/3, find an input x such that either (1) ∥x∥22 ≥ B(n − d)/3 and f (x) = 0 or (2) ∥x∥22 ≤ 3( n − d) and f (x) = 1 .Proof. Consider a set of O

> (

(Bn )C )

samples from a d-dimensional failure certificate G(V⊥, σ 2).We first consider the case σ2 ≤ 2. Since n − d is sufficiently large compared to d, then by a union bound and Gaussian concentration, we have that ∥x∥2 ≤ 3( n − d) simultaneously for all queries 

x, with high probability. On the other hand, f outputs 1 on at least one of the queries, with high probability, which produces the desired claim. The case where σ2 ≥ B/ 2 follows using a similar argument.

Theorem 5.3.19. [HW13 ] Let B ≥ 8 and suppose that B ≤ poly (n). Let A ⊆ Rn be a r-dimensional subspace of Rn, such that n ≥ r + 90 log (Br ). Let f : Rn → { 0, 1} such that f (x) = f (PAx) for all x ∈ Rn. Then there exists an algorithm that uses only oracle access to f and finds a failure certificate for f , with probability 910 . Moreover, the time and query complexity of the algorithm is bounded by poly (B, r ) and all queries of the algorithm are sampled from G(V⊥, σ 2), for some 

V ⊆ Rn and σ2 ∈ (0 , B ].

On randomized algorithms. [ HW13 ] notes that although the proof is stated for algorithms that return a deterministic function f of the sketch Ax , the conclusions remain valid for algorithms that incorporate additional randomness and output a randomized function f of Ax . The key observation is that the attack, with probability 1, never repeats a query. Therefore, for any fixed setting of the randomness used by f across all possible inputs, we obtain a deterministic function to which Theorem 5.3.19 can be directly applied. Now consider the attack described in Figure 5.2. In each round t, we allow the algorithm to apply a possibly new function ft : Rn → { 0, 1}, provided that ft(x) depends only on the projection 

PAx. Under this condition, the proof of Theorem 5.3.19 goes through without change, except that 

f is replaced by ft in round t, i.e., f (x) = ft(Ax ) at each time t. Note that since f is a norm-based estimator, it can use different randomness at time t, but it cannot be a function of other parameters such as the history of previous queries. The key requirement for the attack to succeed is simply that the subspace A remains fixed. 

# 5.4 Lower Bound for Fp Estimation with Integer Sketches 

Although the attack by [ HW13 ] in Section 5.3 describes a real-valued adaptive attack on linear sketches for Lp norms, streaming algorithms realistically require discrete-valued input data streams. In this section, we demonstrate a similar attack by [ GLW +25 ] within the streaming model, in particular on the following notion of integer sketches: 

Definition 5.4.1 (Integer sketch) . Given a data stream of length at most m = poly (n) that implicitly defines a vector in Rn with integer entries bounded in magnitude by poly (n), an integer sketch is a linear sketching algorithm whose sketching matrix consists entirely of integers with magnitude at most M = poly( n).

More generally, the lifting technique of [GLW +25] is able to extend linear sketch lower bounds from the continuous setting to the discrete setting, thereby achieving a number of additional lower bounds for streaming algorithms. A standard method for generating hard input distributions in Rn for real-valued linear sketches 

A ∈ Rr×n involves drawing inputs x from a continuous Gaussian distribution N (0 , σ 2 · In). Due to the rotational invariance of Gaussian distributions, the sketch output Ax is itself Gaussian, specifically distributed as N (0 , σ 2AA ⊤). This property allows lower bounds for sketching to be established by analyzing the total variation distance between low-dimensional Gaussian distributions. One might naturally attempt to extend such lower bounds to discrete input settings by applying discretization techniques, such as rounding Gaussian samples to the nearest multiple of 1poly( n) .– 150 – However, this approach breaks the rotational invariance of the distribution, making the distribution of Ax harder to analyze. The truncation may inadvertently leak information about x, complicating the analysis of sketch outputs. Alternatively, one could consider using discrete Gaussian distributions directly. However, if 

x ∼ D (0 , σ 2In) is drawn from a discrete Gaussian, it does not follow that Ax is distributed as a discrete Gaussian over the lattice AZn with covariance σ2AA ⊤. In fact, this discrepancy motivates a line of work in lattice-based analysis, which studies the behavior of discrete Gaussians through the geometry of lattices—particularly via bounds on the successive minima of the orthogonal lattice to the row span of A. Indeed, at a high level, [ GLW +24 ] uses lattice theory to prove a “cell” lemma, which allow the simulation of a continuous Gaussian distribution using a discrete Gaussian distribution plus additional uniform continuous noise. The integer sketch lower bounds then follow by simulating the previously known attacks over the reals, which originally required continuous Gaussian queries. In particular, the attack on real-valued linear sketches by [ HW13 ] from Section 5.3 uses continuous Gaussian queries and thus can be lifted by the techniques of [ GSWY25 ] to obtain an attack on integer-valued sketches. 

5.4.1 Lattice Theory 

We review several fundamental concepts from lattice theory, such as the definitions of a lattice, its dual lattice, the orthogonal lattice, the smoothing parameter, and the successive minima. 

Definition 5.4.2 (Lattice) . A lattice is a discrete additive subgroup of Rm. Given a basis of linearly independent vectors B = {b1, . . . , bn} ⊂ Rm with m ≥ n ≥ 1, the lattice generated by B is defined as 

L = L(B) = 

> {

Bz =

> n∑
> i=1

zibi | z ∈ Zn

> }

.

Definition 5.4.3 (Successive minima) . For each i ∈ [n], the i-th successive minimum λi(L) of a lattice L is defined as the minimal radius r such that the ball of radius r centered at the origin contains at least i linearly independent vectors from L.

Definition 5.4.4 (Orthogonal lattice) . For a lattice L ⊂ Zn, the orthogonal lattice L⊥ is defined as 

L⊥ = {y ∈ Zn | ⟨ y, x⟩ = 0 for all x ∈ L} .

Recall from Definition 2.1.1 that we define for any s > 0, the probability density function of the spherical Gaussian function with center μ ∈ Rn by

Definition 5.4.5 (Dual lattice and smoothing parameter) . For any lattice L, its dual lattice L∗ is defined by 

L∗ = {x ∈ Rn | ⟨ x, y⟩ ∈ Z for all y ∈ L} .

Furthermore, given a lattice L and ε > 0, the smoothing parameter ηε(L) is the smallest positive real number s such that 

ρ1/s (L∗ \ { 0}) ≤ ε.

We also restate Siegel’s lemma, which provides an upper bound on the size of the entries of integer vectors that lie in the kernel of a matrix A.

Lemma 5.4.8 (Siegel’s lemma) . [SH00 ] Let A ∈ Zr×n be a nonzero integer matrix with r < n , and suppose all entries of A are bounded in magnitude by M . Then there exists a nonzero integer vector 

x ∈ Zn satisfying Ax = 0r, whose entries are bounded in magnitude by (nM )r/ (n−r).

5.4.2 Lifting Framework 

In this section, we introduce the lifting framework of [ GLW +25 ], to prove a lower bound against adversarially robust algorithms based on integer-valued sketches on turnstile streams. We first give a technical overview of the approach, following the exposition of [GLW +25]. 

Smoothing parameter and successive minima. Let A ∈ Zr×n have columns Ai, and define the lattice L(A) = {∑ni=1 ziAi : z ∈ Zn}. The successive minima λi(L) describe the smallest radius needed to capture i linearly independent lattice vectors centered at the origin. For convenience, we use λmax (L(A)) to denote λrank( A)(L(A)) .– 152 – To argue that the total variation distance between Ax and y is small for x ∼ D Zn,S and 

y ∼ D AZn,SA ⊤ , we rely on the previously introduced tools from lattice theory [ AR16 , AGHS13 ]. These works often reduce the task of upper bounding the total variation distance between Ax 

and y to the task of upper bounding the smoothing parameter , which in turn depends on the successive minima of the orthogonal lattice L⊥(A), i.e., the set of integer vectors orthogonal to all rows of A. The main goal is to find a short basis for L⊥(A), i.e., with λn−r(L⊥(A)) ≤ poly (n).However, arbitrary sketching matrices A may not admit such a basis directly, so [ GLW +25 ] designs a pre-processing step to ensure this property. 

Pre-processing to upper bound the successive minima. Given an integer matrix A ∈ Zr×n

with r < n and entries bounded by M ≤ poly (n), the goal is to augment A such that the resulting matrix A′ satisfies λmax (L⊥(A′)) ≤ poly (n), has at most m ≤ n − Ω( n) rows, and can be assumed without loss of generality in the lower bounds. The approach involves iteratively appending kernel vectors to A, using Siegel’s lemma to guarantee short integer solutions. After T = 0 .49 n − r such steps, we obtain a set of short kernel vectors, each bounded in norm by (nM )2. [ GLW +24 ] then generates an additional 0.51 n vectors using Siegel’s lemma and adds them to A to form A′, yielding an orthogonal lattice with polynomially bounded maximum successive minimum. Since these rows only strengthen the sketch, we can assume without loss of generality that the final sketching matrix is A′.

A better pre-processing. The previous method adds Θ( n) rows, which is too costly for sublinear lower bounds. To reduce this overhead, [ GLW +25 ] constructs n − 4r short, linearly independent vectors in L⊥(A) by a probabilistic method: sampling Θ( M 1.5r) integer vectors with entries in 

{0, 1, . . . , M − 1} and exploiting the birthday paradox to find a pair with matching images under 

A but linearly independent differences. This is repeated until n − 4r such vectors are obtained. Then, Siegel’s lemma can again be used to generate 3r additional vectors orthogonal to both the row span of A and the constructed vectors, not necessarily with polynomially bounded entries. This gives a final sketch matrix A′ ∈ Z4r×n for which λmax (L⊥(A′)) ≤ √nM 2 = poly (n). Although A′

may contain entries larger than poly (n) due to the additional 3r vectors, this is not an issue, as the subsequent argument only requires bounded successive minima. 

Cell lemma. Let A ∈ Zr×n be the sketching matrix obtained after applying the pre-processing described above, so that the maximum successive minimum λmax (L⊥(A)) is at most polynomial in 

n. Define the fundamental parallelepiped F of the lattice L(A) as 

F = {x ∈ Rr | xi ∈ [0 , ℓ i)} ,

where ℓi is the smallest non-zero value of |(Ax )i| over all x ∈ Zn. This region tiles Rr into disjoint “cells”, each centered at a lattice point Az for z ∈ Zn. We may also assume without loss of generality that A has orthonormal rows. This can be achieved via a change of basis in a post-processing step. Crucially, this does not alter the structure of the fundamental parallelepiped: let U⊤ be a matrix with orthonormal rows spanning the same row space as A. Then the projection onto this row space can be written as A⊤(AA ⊤)−1A = UU ⊤. Because the rows of A span a rational subspace, the projection matrix UU ⊤ has rational entries, and the projection UU ⊤x is lower bounded for any non-zero x ∈ Zn. Hence, the fundamental parallelepiped defined by U⊤ remains well-formed. – 153 – This setup allows us to compare the probability mass functions of Ax and y, where x ∼ D Zn,S

is a discrete Gaussian and y ∼ D AZn,SA ⊤ is a projected discrete Gaussian. Let ρ1 denote the probability mass function of Ax , and ρ2 that of y. Suppose the covariance matrix Σ = S⊤S satisfies 

σn(Σ) ≥ α for 

α = poly( n) > λ n−m(L⊥(A)) 2 · ln(2 n(1 + 1 /ε )) 

π ,

which is guaranteed by our pre-processing. Then, applying the lattice theory result Lemma 5.4.6 from [AGHS13], point-wise closeness between the distributions can be obtained: 

1 − 1poly( n) ≤ ρ1(x)

ρ2(x) ≤ 1 + 1poly( n) .

While the discrete and continuous Gaussians are close on lattice points, their total variation distance over each unit cell of L(A) is large. Specifically, the discrete distribution has no support in the interior of these cells. This poses a challenge when trying to lift lower bounds that rely on continuous Gaussian inputs. To overcome this, [ GLW +25 ] notes that for each integer input x ∈ Zn, the sketching algorithm can be assumed (without loss of generality) to observe Ax + η, where η is sampled uniformly from the fundamental cell F. This is valid because the algorithm can always recover Ax by rounding 

Ax + η to the nearest lattice point in L(A). Define p as the probability density function of Ax + η,where x ∼ D Zn,S and η is drawn uniformly from F. Let q be the probability density function of the continuous Gaussian N (0 , AΣA⊤). Then, with high probability over the randomness of η,

1 − 1poly( n) ≤ p(x)

q(x) ≤ 1 + 1poly( n) .

This lemma shows that the distribution of Ax + η closely approximates a continuous Gaussian, allowing us to simulate Gaussian inputs even when restricted to integer-valued queries. As a result, we can simulate lower bounds based on real-valued Gaussian inputs to the integer setting using the lifting framework in Theorem 5.4.12. The above argument establishes point-wise multiplicative closeness of probability densities after smoothing , namely, for a fixed realization of the random shift η, the density of Ax + η is close to that of a continuous Gaussian. However, this form of closeness is not preserved under rounding or other nonlinear post-processing, and therefore does not imply small total variation distance between the corresponding discrete distributions. In particular, although Ax + η is close in density to a Gaussian, it does not follow that rounding a Gaussian sample to the nearest lattice point yields a distribution close (in total variation distance) to that obtained by first rounding an independent Gaussian sample and then applying A. That is, sampling a Gaussian g, rounding it to obtain g ∈ Zn, and outputting Ag need not produce a distribution close to rounding Ag ′ for an independent Gaussian g′.As a concrete example, let A = [1 , N ] and consider Ax for x ∼ D Z2,I2 and y ∼ D AZ2,A⊤ . Even though the support of Ax and y are both AZ2, the probability mass of y concentrates around 0,while the probability mass of Ax concentrates around multiples of N . Hence, the point-wise values of the probability mass functions are quite different, c.f., Figure 5.3. 

Formal analysis. We begin with the following matrix pre-processing lemma, which proves that given a matrix A ∈ Zr×n, it is possible to append at most 3r integer vectors to its rows to obtain a new matrix A′ such that λn−r(L⊥(A′)) ≤ √nM 2.– 154 – Fig. 5.3: Differences in probability mass functions 

Lemma 5.4.9 (Matrix pre-processing lemma) . [GLW +25 ] Let A ∈ Zr×n be a full-rank integer matrix with r ≤ n 

> 4

and entries bounded in magnitude by M , for some M ≥ n. Then there exists a pre-processing procedure that extends A by adding additional rows to obtain a matrix A′ ∈ Zm×n

with m ≤ 4r, such that 

λn−r(L⊥(A′)) ≤ √nM 2.

Proof. We first show that it is possible to find n−4r linearly independent integer vectors with entries bounded in magnitude by M in the kernel of A. To do this, we use a probabilistic construction to build these vectors iteratively. Suppose we have already selected t ≤ n − 4r linearly independent vectors in the kernel of A. Let B ∈ R(n−t)×n be a matrix whose rows form a basis for the orthogonal complement of the span of these t vectors. Without loss of generality, we can choose B to contain 

n − t columns that form a diagonal matrix with non-zero diagonal entries, as the row space of B

can be selected arbitrarily. Now, consider the following sampling process: draw Θ( M 1.5r) random integer vectors v1, v2, . . . , vs

with entries in {0, 1, . . . , M − 1}. Let E1 denote the event that there exist indices i and j such that 1 ≤ i < j ≤ s and Av i = Av j . Each coordinate of Av i lies in {− nM 2, . . . , nM 2} and M ≥ n,so there are at most M 3r possible values of Av i. Therefore, by the birthday paradox, we have 

Pr [E1] ≥ 0.9.Next, consider the difference x = vi − vj , which is an integer vector with entries in {− (M −

1) , . . . , M − 1}. Since B is a diagonal matrix with n − t non-zero entries, and the remaining t

coordinates of x are fixed, there is at most one assignment to the remaining coordinates that satisfies 

Bx = 0n−t. Hence, we have Pr [Bx = 0n−t] ≤ 

> (1
> M

A′ ∈ Z4r×n. Since these new rows are orthogonal to the original ones, we have

We introduce the following notion of smoothness to formally characterize functions that, infor-mally speaking, exhibit stability under small perturbations. 

Definition 5.4.11 (Smoothness) . Let I be any distribution over Zn. A function f : Rn → S, where 

S is a finite subset of Z, is said to be δ-smooth with respect to the distribution D(μ, Σ) + I if 

Pr X∼D (μ, Σ)+ I, Y ∼N (μ, Σ)+ I [f (X)̸ = f (Y )] ≤ δ. 

For instance, consider a function that behaves very differently on integers compared to real numbers, such as f (x) = 1 if x ∈ Z and f (x) = 0 if x ∈ R \ Z. Such a function is not smooth, as even a small perturbation can cause a drastic change in the output. These types of functions are not well-suited to our framework, since minor changes within the fundamental parallelepiped of a lattice (defined by the sketch matrix) can yield entirely different outcomes after post-processing. We are now ready to prove the main result of this section.

5.4.3 Preliminaries for Adversarial Attack 

Recall that a linear sketch consists of a distribution M over r × n matrices, along with an estimator 

F : Rr×n × Rn → { 0, 1}, which can be arbitrary. A streaming algorithm A proceeds by first sampling a matrix A ∼ M , and then for any query vector x ∈ Zn, it returns F (A, Ax ) though often F (Ax )

suffices. We may assume without loss of generality that A has full row rank, and we denote the row space of A by R(A). For simplicity, we will identify the matrix A with its row space, which – 159 – forms an r-dimensional subspace of Rn. As a result, we can represent the sketch as a function 

f : Zn → { 0, 1} such that f (x) = f (PAx), where PU is the orthogonal projection operator onto the subspace U. We now describe our attack in the context of the GapNorm promise problem, which we restate below: 

Definition 5.3.17 (GapNorm (B, α ) promise problem) . Given any fixed B ≥ 8 and α = poly (n),we say that an algorithm A solves the problem GapNorm (B, α ) if it satisfies the following condition: for every input vector x ∈ Zn, the algorithm outputs 1 whenever ∥x∥22 ≥ α · B, and outputs 0

whenever ∥x∥22 ≤ α. If ∥x∥22 falls within the interval (α, α · B), then A is permitted to return either 

0 or 1.

For convenience in the analysis of our discrete attack, we recall the family of distributions 

G(V⊥, σ 2) that was used for the real-valued attack by [HW13]. 

Definition 5.3.8. Let σ > 0 be a parameter, V ⊆ A be a subspace of A of dimension t ≤ r − 1. Let 

G(V⊥, σ 2) be the distribution obtained from sampling g1 ∼ N (0 , σ 2)n, g 2 ∼ N (0 , 1/4) n independently and outputting g = PV⊥ g1 + g2.Furthermore, for d = r − t, we define the family of distributions G(A ∩ V⊥) = {gτ } by setting gτ = PAg, for g ∼ G(V⊥, τ /d − 1/4) if τd > 14 and otherwise setting gτ = PAg where 

g ∼ G(V⊥, τ /d ).

Now, we define the analogous family of discrete Gaussian distributions from which queries are drawn in our attack for integer sketches. 

Definition 5.4.14 (Distribution D(V⊥, σ 2)). Let V ⊆ A be a subspace of dimension t ≤ r − 1, and define d = r − t. We define the distribution D(V⊥, σ 2) as DZn (0 , Σσ2 ), where Σσ2 is the covariance matrix associated with the Gaussian distribution G(V⊥, σ 2). Specifically, the covariance is given by 

Σσ2 = 3σ2

4 P⊤ 

> V⊥

PV⊥ + σ2

4 · In.

5.4.4 Discrete Conditional Expectation Lemma 

In this section, we establish a discrete analogue of the Conditional Expectation Lemma from [ HW13 ], c.f., Lemma 5.3.6, that we showed in Section 5.3.3. To begin, we recall the definition of correctness to intuitively characterize the expected behavior of an estimator f on inputs sampled from the distribution D(V⊥, σ 2) or more generally any distribution D.

Definition 5.3.10 (Correctness) . Let A be a subspace and V ⊆ A be a subspace with d =dim( V⊥ ∩ A). We call a function f : A → { 0, 1} to be (ε, B )-correct on V⊥:(1) For all σ2 ∈ [B/ 2, 2B] and g ∼ G(V⊥, σ 2), we have 

Pr [f (g) = 1] ≥ 1 − ε. 

(2) For all σ2 ∈ [0 , 2] and g ∼ G(V⊥, σ 2), we have 

Pr [f (g) = 1] ≤ ε. 

We simply call f to be B-correct on V⊥ if it is (ε, B )-correct for some ε ≤ 110( Bd )2 .

Definition 5.3.5 (Soundness) . We say that a function f : U → { 0, 1}, where dim (U) = d, is

5.4.5 Algorithm for Adversarial Attack 

In this section, we present an adversarial attack for the GapNorm promise problem, succinctly described in Figure 5.4. Ultimately, the goal of the attack is to find the following notion of a failure certificate, which slightly differs from Definition 5.3.16 due to the input having support over a discrete distribution rather than a continuous distribution. 

Definition 5.4.16 (Failure certificate) . Let B ≥ 8, σ2 ∈ [α, 2αB ], and consider a function 

f : Rn → { 0, 1}. We define a pair (V, σ 2) to be a d-dimensional failure certificate for f if V ⊆ Rn

is a subspace of dimension d, and there exists a constant C > 0 such that n ≥ d + 10 C log (Bn ) and one of the following holds: 

• If σ2 ∈ [αB/ 2, 50 αB ], then:

For the purposes of our attack, we will consider subspaces V spanned by vectors with bounded precision, i.e., appropriately scaled integer vectors. To that end, we begin by demonstrating that a failure certificate for f can be used to construct a discrete input vector x on which f makes an error. This mirrors Fact 5.2 from [ HW13 ] in Fact 5.3.18, which establishes a similar result but only for real-valued vectors x.

Theorem 5.4.17. [GLW +25 ] Let η = 1poly( n) be a fixed small parameter, and suppose α = poly (n) is sufficiently large. Then, given a d-dimensional failure certificate for f , there exists an algorithm that makes poly (Bn ) non-adaptive queries and, with probability at least 23 , returns a vector x ∈ (η · Z)n

such that either ∥x∥2 > αB (n−d)3 and f (x) = 0 , or ∥x∥2 < 3α(n − d) and f (x) = 1 .

> (

(αBn )C )

such that f (Ax ) = 1 .The case when σ2 ≥ Bα

Furthermore, since D(V⊥, σ 2) is a failure certificate, with high probability there exists x ∈ X such that f (Ax ) = 0 .We now state the guarantees of the adaptive attack described in Figure 5.4, demonstrating that it successfully identifies a failure certificate. Since the proof relies on several structural lemmas that will be established in the following sections, we postpone the proof of Theorem 5.4.18 until Section 5.4.9. 

Theorem 5.4.18 (Adaptive attack against integer linear sketches) . [GLW +25 ] Suppose that 8 ≤

B ≤ poly (n) and α ≥ 4ℓA, where λmax (L⊥(A)) ≤ ℓA = √nM 2. Let A ⊆ Rn be an r-dimensional subspace with n ≥ O (r) + 90 log (Bn ), and suppose f : A → { 0, 1} is a linear sketch defined over all 

x ∈ Zn. Then there exists an attack algorithm that, using only oracle access to f , identifies a failure certificate for f with probability at least 910 . The algorithm’s runtime and number of queries are bounded by poly (r log n). Moreover, each query made by the algorithm is drawn from a distribution 

D(V⊥, σ 2) for some subspace V ⊆ Rn and variance σ2 ∈ [α, Bα ].

5.4.6 Distance Between Subspaces 

In this section, we provide a brief overview of subspace distance, which will be used to quantify how close the subspace generated by the attack’s collected vectors is to the nearest subspace contained in the sketch matrix A, as discussed in Section 5.4.7. This notion is key to establishing an invariant that remains valid throughout the execution of the attack. We recall the formal definition of the distance between subspaces: 

Definition 5.3.13 (Distance between subspaces) . We define the distance between subspaces V, W ⊆

Rn by 

d(V, W) = ∥PV − PW∥2 := sup 

> v∈Rn

∥PVv − PWv∥2

∥v∥2

.

Next, we present a structural result that upper bounds the total variation distance between two discrete subspace Gaussian distributions in terms of the distance between their corresponding orthogonal subspaces. This result serves as a discrete analogue of Lemma 4.14 from [ HW13 ] in Fact 5.3.14. 

Lemma 5.4.19. [GLW +25] For every σ2 ∈ (α, Bα ], we have 

dtv (D(V⊥, σ 2), D (W⊥, σ 2)) ≤ 40 

> √

Bn log( Bn ) · d(V, W) + 1(Bn )4 .

Proof. Let x1 ∼ D(V⊥, σ 2) and x2 ∼ D(W⊥, σ 2) be independently sampled. By choosing A as the identity in Lemma 5.4.13, we establish that D(V⊥, σ 2) is point-wise close to the rounded version of – 164 – G(V⊥, σ 2), differing by at most a multiplicative factor of 1 ± 1poly( n) , assuming sufficiently large α.Applying the same reasoning to D(W⊥, σ 2) and G(W⊥, σ 2), we obtain: 

dtv (D(V⊥, σ 2), D (W⊥, σ 2)) ≤ dtv (G(V⊥, σ 2), G (W⊥, σ 2)) + 1poly( n) .

From Lemma 4.14 in [HW13], c.f., Fact 5.3.14, for σ2 in the interval [α, αB ], we have: 

dtv (G(V⊥, σ 2), G (W⊥, σ 2)) ≤ 20 

√

Bn log( Bn ) · d(V, W) + 1(Bn )5 .

Combining these using the triangle inequality, the claimed bound follows, up to an additive loss of  

> 1poly( n)

in total variation distance. 

5.4.7 Progress Lemma 

We now demonstrate that each iteration of the adaptive attack advances toward finding a subspace that closely approximates the row space of the sketch matrix A. For each 1 ≤ t ≤ r, define Wt ⊆ A

as the (t − 1) -dimensional subspace of A that is nearest to Vt. Formally, we implicitly define Wt, so that: 

d(Vt, Wt) = min {d(Vt, W) : dim( W) = t − 1, W ⊆ A},

where we slightly abuse notation so that Vt refers to the subspace spanned by the vectors in Vt,i.e., Span (Vt). To establish the correctness of the attack, we show that the algorithm preserves the following invariant with high probability throughout its execution: 

Invariant 5.4.20. For each step t ∈ [r + 1] , we have dim (Vt) = t − 1 and d(Vt, Wt) ≤

> 40( Bn )3.5log 2.5(Bn )

We now show that in each iteration of the attack, one of two outcomes occurs: (1) either the algorithm halts and returns a failure certificate, or (2) assuming the invariant continues to hold in the following round, then the function f remains correct on the corresponding orthogonal subspace. This result mirrors Lemma 5.6 in [HW13] in Lemma 5.3.23. Note that we have not yet established that the invariant holds in the next round; this will be addressed later in the “Progress Lemma” (see Lemma 5.4.28).

In this section, we prove Lemma 5.4.29, which asserts that if we form a matrix whose rows are vectors that exhibit a small correlation with the row space of A, then the top right singular vector of this matrix has a significant correlation with the row space of A. Recall that Lemma 5.4.29 played a key role in the proof of Lemma 5.4.26, which in turn was essential for establishing the Progress Lemma in Lemma 5.4.28. We establish the following result concerning the top right singular vector of discrete Gaussian matrices with a bias. In the setting of Lemma 5.4.26, the subspace V represents the portion of the row space of A that remains undiscovered by the adversary, the parameter τ corresponds to the expected value E

# 5.5 Lower Bound for F0 Estimation with Integer Sketches 

In this section, we show lower bounds for integer and linear sketches for the F0 estimation problem, where the goal is to estimate the number of non-zero entries ∥x∥0 = |{ i : xi̸ = 0 }| in the vector 

x defined by the data stream, c.f., Definition 3.3.14. We first define the gap version of the F0

estimation problem. Formally, the problem is defined as follows: 

Definition 5.5.1 (F0 gap problem) . Let 0 ≤ α < β ≤ 1. An algorithm A solves the (α, β )-F0 gap problem if for any input vector x ∈ Zn, A outputs 0 if ∥x∥0 ≤ αn and outputs 1 if ∥x∥0 ≥ βn . If 

∥x∥0 ∈ (αn, βn ), then A may return either 0 or 1.

We shall show that for any integer sketching algorithm A that uses an integer sketching matrix 

A ∈ Zr×n, where n is the size of the universe, there exists an attack by [ GLW +24 ] that makes ˜O(r8)

queries and succeeds with high constant probability in breaking the sketch, in the sense that the algorithm A will fail to solve the F0 gap problem. We also describe an adaptive attack by [ GLW +24 ]over Rn against linear sketches A ∈ Rr×n for F0-estimation, in the setting where A has all nonzero subdeterminants at least 1poly( r) , i.e., every k × k submatrix obtained by selecting any k rows and k

columns of A has determinant either zero or at least 1poly( r) .

5.5.1 Overview of Attack on F0 Estimation 

In this section, we describe the adaptive attack of [ GLW +24 ] against integer sketching algorithms designed for the F0 gap problem. The strategy of the adaptive adversary is to incrementally learn – 176 – the structure of the sketching matrix A, and then using this knowledge to construct increasingly challenging query vectors. A sketching matrix A may encode a substantial amount of information about certain coordinates xi in the sketch Ax . For instance, when a row in A has nonzero entries in only one column, it can directly recover that coordinate. On the other hand, its dependence on other coordinates may be minimal, such as when each coordinate is always involved in linear combinations with many other coordinates. The coordinates that the sketch maintains substantial information about, referred to as significant coordinates , are particularly useful for F0-estimation in a non-adaptive setting. For example, one might design A so that it captures information about a random subset of O (1) coordinates. By examining whether these sampled coordinates are zero or nonzero, one could estimate the total number of nonzeros in x within additive error 0.1n, solving the F0 gap problem. Therefore, the attack of [ GLW +24 ] iteratively identifies significant coordinates and ensures they are set to zero in all subsequent queries. Doing so makes future queries harder for A to interpret: once a coordinate is always zero, A may still waste its capacity storing information about it, thus effectively reducing its dimensionality on the remaining coordinates. When r ≪ n, the sketch cannot preserve significant information about too many coordinates. Eventually, the sketching process is forced to rely solely on insignificant coordinates , which the sketch can only weakly reflect. The attack hinges on solving the three following key subproblems: 

• Formally define the notion of a “significant” coordinate, and prove that when r ≪ n, only a small number of coordinates can be significant. 

• Design an algorithm that reliably identifies these significant coordinates using a polynomial number of queries. 

• Demonstrate that any algorithm must fail to estimate the F0 value accurately when the input is supported only on the insignificant coordinates. To do this, we construct distributions over x with drastically different F0 values whose corresponding sketches Ax are nearly indistinguishable. Informally, a notion of “significant” coordinates will be defined conceptually, and then a pre-processing argument will upper bound the number of significant coordinates. A fingerprinting attack can be applied to identify the significant coordinates. Finally, a moment-matching argument will be used to construct “hard” distributions for sketches with support on the insignificant coordinates. We now further outline how [GLW +24] addresses each of these components. 

Fingerprinting codes. To learn significant coordinates, first consider a special case: when Ax 

simply returns a subset of r coordinates of x, i.e., each row of A is a unit vector. Here, these r

coordinates are extremely significant, while the rest are entirely insignificant. This problem aligns with the setting addressed by interactive fingerprinting codes [ SU15 ]. In that setting, an adversary P selects a hidden subset S ⊂ [n] of size k, and a fingerprinting code F

must identify S through adaptive queries ct ∈ {± 1}n. Each response at must be consistent with some coordinate cti for i ∈ S . Over repeated interactions, F tracks correlations between queried indices and the responses, accumulating to identify the hidden subset S with high probability in 

O (k2) queries. For intuition, we remark that in the special case where each row of A equals some elementary vector ei, the sketch provides exact values of individual xi. Moreover, solving the F0-gap problem demands distinguishing between ∥x∥0 ≤ αn and ∥x∥0 ≥ βn and is strictly harder than the fingerprinting code task, so the same strategy suffices for the attack. – 177 – Significant coordinates. The analysis is then extended to general matrices A by considering what constitutes significant information for a coordinate. Beyond the elementary vector case, if A

is linear, then Ax lets us compute any w⊤x where w lies in the row span of A. This motivates the following definition: coordinate i is s-significant if there exists y⊤ ∈ Rr such that 

(y⊤A)2 

> i

≥ 1

s · ∥ y⊤A∥22.

This definition is equivalent to saying that the leverage score of column i is at least 1 

> s

, which accurately captures significance if real-valued queries are allowed. However, when query vectors must have integer coordinates bounded by poly (n), leverage scores are insufficient. For example, consider a row vector (C, . . . , C, 1) in A. Most leverage scores are low, but Ax still leaks xn mod C. This is because although the vector w⊤ =

Matrix pre-processing. To aid the analysis, [ GSWY24 ] transforms A into a matrix A′ that separates significant and insignificant columns while preserving the information available to the sketch. Consider the following iterative procedure: while there exists a column satisfying (5.8) , zero it out and record its index in a significant set S. After no such column remains, add a new row ei

for each i ∈ S . This results in a sketching matrix: 

A′ =

> [

DS

> ]

,

where D is a dense matrix that contains only insignificant columns, and S is a sparse matrix that has at most one non-zero entry 1 in each row and column, with the set of non-zero columns corresponding exactly with the set of significant coordinates S. Moreover, the columns of D and S

are disjoint. Observe that the sparse component corresponds precisely to the extreme scenario discussed earlier, and the set S can be recovered using the fingerprinting code in the absence of a dense component. Additionally, [ GLW +24 ] establishes that the notion of significant coordinates, along with the pre-processing step, ensures that the sparse set remains small, i.e., |S| ≪ n. This guarantees that once S is identified and its coordinates are zeroed out in the query, the F0 estimation problem on the remaining coordinates remains non-trivial. Informally, the existence of such a pre-processing step is shown by arguing that under the uniform distribution over x ∈ {− 1, 0, 1}n, any column i satisfying (5.8) must induce a non-negligible mutual information between Ax and xi, namely, I(Ax ; xi) ≥ Ω 

> (1

The final step is to demonstrate that the dense component, i.e., the insignificant coordinates, does not contribute meaningful information to the algorithm. This is achieved by carefully constructing an appropriate query distribution. Specifically, we define a family of distributions D supported on the set {− R, −(R − 1) , . . . , R },where R = poly (n) is bounded by a small polynomial in n. The distributions in D are designed to satisfy the following properties: (1) For any Dp ∈ D with parameter p ∈ [α, β ] for some constants 0 < α < β < 1, it holds that 

Pr 

> X∼Dp

[X = 0] = p;(2) For any two parameters p, q ∈ [α, β ], and corresponding product distributions xp ∼ Dnp and

where the query vectors x are drawn from Dnp for different values of p. Due to the properties of D,the marginal distribution of Dx remains nearly invariant with respect to p. Additionally, since the nonzero columns of D and S are disjoint, the outputs Dx and Sx are conditionally independent given p. Consequently, if queries are sampled from this distribution family, the algorithm cannot utilize the dense part Dx to distinguish different values of p, and must instead rely entirely on the sparse part Sx to approximate ∥x∥0. Importantly, the distributions Dp can be incorporated into the fingerprinting code construction, ensuring that the dense component remains uninformative during the attack on the sparse part. This enables gradual identification of the significant coordinate set S

and once all such coordinates have been identified and zeroed out, we issue a final query in which all entries in S are set to zero. At this point, the algorithm observes only Dx , which, by design, is nearly independent of p, and hence must fail to produce a correct output with high probability.

In this section, we formally introduce the pre-processing procedure and justify the main properties resulting from the subsequent matrix. Suppose we use a sketching matrix A as a basis for our adversarially robust streaming algorithm for L0 estimation. We first consider a conceptual pre-processing of the sketching matrix A into sparse part and a dense part, which consists has disjoint support on the columns. Observe that this pre-processing step only increases the power of the streaming algorithm, since it only allows the algorithm to observe potentially more entries of the input vector x(t). For a real number x, we define the function Frac (x) = x − int (x) ∈ (− 12 , 12 ],where int (x) is the closest integer number to x. Similarly, for a vector x ∈ Rn, let Frac (x) ∈ Rn

be the coordinate-wise fractional parts of x, i.e., Frac (x)j = Frac (xj ). Formally, the matrix A′

formed after pre-processing A will satisfy the following several key properties, where we use Frac (x)

to denote the fractional part of a real number x.

Lemma 5.5.2. [GLW +24 ] Let A be a streaming algorithm that uses a sketching matrix A ∈ Zr×n.Then there is a pre-processing procedure that produces a new matrix A′ ∈ Zr′×n for r′ = O (rs log n)

such that: (1) The matrix A′ has the form 

> [

DS

> ]

where the matrices D and S are column-disjoint. (2) For all y ∈ Rr and j ∈ [n], we have |Frac (y⊤D)j |2 ≤ 1 

> s

· ∥ Frac (y⊤D)∥22.(3) Each row and column of S has at most one non-zero entry. Moreover, we can assume without loss of generality that the algorithm A uses sketching matrix A′

instead of A.Proof. Consider the following iterative procedure. Begin with the sketching matrix A. For each time t, let Dt−1 be the first r rows of A(t−1) . We then identify a column jt ∈ [n] such that there exists y⊤ such that ∣∣∣Frac (y⊤D(t−1) )jt

> ∣∣∣2

> 1

s · ∥ Frac (y⊤D(t−1) )∥22.

We then zero out the jt-th column of At−1 and add a new row (the elementary vector) ejt to the matrix A(t−1) . We denote this new resulting matrix by A(t).Suppose that the above procedure ends in the iteration T . From Lemma 5.5.4, which we shall prove, we have T = O (rs log n).Let D = D(T ) and S be the remaining rows of A(T ). Hence, A′ =

> [

DS

> ]

has at most r + T =

O (rs log n) columns. Moreover, it is clear from the iterative procedure that the matrices D and S

are column-disjoint. Furthermore, by construction of the added elementary rows in the iterative procedure, each row and column of S has at most one non-zero entry. At this point, it remains to show why we can assume that the streaming algorithm A uses the sketching matrix A′, rather than A. To this end, suppose that the algorithm A uses the sketching matrix A and a post-processing composition function f on Ax . Now, consider a different post-processing estimator g that takes input A′x and first inverts the row operations that we apply on A to achieve A′. Thus, g transforms A′x to Ax and then outputs the value f (Ax ). From the definition of g, we have g(A′x) = f (Ax ) for every input vector x, and thus we can assume that A

has the form g(A′x) without loss of generality. – 182 – It remains to bound the number of added rows.

Lemma 5.5.4. [ GLW +24 ] Let A ∈ Zr×n be a fixed matrix and let s > 1 be a parameter. There exists a pre-processing procedure that takes input A and outputs a matrix A′ ∈ Zr×n that zeros out at most O (rs log n log s) columns of A. Moreover for all y ∈ Rr and j ∈ [n], we have

Therefore, Cr log n ≥ Ts log s for some constant C, which implies T = O (rs log n log s), as desired. 

5.5.3 Fingerprinting Attack on Sparse Part 

In this section, we show correctness of the fingerprinting attack on the sparse part S of the matrix 

A, where we assume A has already undergone the pre-processing procedure from Section 5.5.2. We split the analysis into discussions for soundness and correctness of the attack. Let Pa,b denote the probability distribution with support [a, b ] and probability density function 

μ(p) = Ca,b 

√p(1 −p) , for a normalizing constant Ca,b . For p ∈ [0 , 1] , let ϕp : {± 1} → R be defined by 

ϕ0(c) = ϕ1(c) = 0 for c ∈ {± 1}. Moreover, let ϕp(1) = −√ p 

> 1−p

and ϕp(−1) = 

√ 1−pp for p ∈ (0 , 1) .By construction, ϕp(c) has mean 0 and variance 1 when Pr [c = −1] = p and Pr [c = 1] = 1 − p. We describe the attack on the sparse matrix in full in Figure 5.5.

5.5.3.2 Completeness 

In this section, we prove completeness of the fingerprinting attack, i.e., that the attack will correctly identify coordinates that are contained within S, the set of non-zero indices of columns in S. Note that this is necessary in conjunction with Section 5.5.3.1 to show correctness of the fingerprinting attack because even in light of Section 5.5.3.2, it could be that the fingerprinting attack does not report any coordinates in each round. 

Fourier analysis. We first show a structural property informally stating that the rate at which the average of f (v) changes with p is exactly given by how much f (v) correlates with the zero vs. nonzero pattern in v. We use h := |S| to denote the size of S.

Concentration. We now show that if the algorithm has small error and the function only looks at certain coordinates, then the difference in expected outputs for p = β and p = α is large. In particular, this crystallizes the guarantee of Lemma 5.5.12 from an abstract function to our fingerprinting attack A.

5.5.3.3 Final Guarantees for Fingerprinting Attack 

We can now put together the completeness and soundness guarantees for our full guarantees for the fingerprinting attack. In particular, this attack must still succeed even with possible interference from the dense part D of the sketch matrix A.

Theorem 5.5.19. [GLW +24 ] Let A be a linear streaming algorithm that solves the (α + c, β − c)-gap 

F0 problem for some constants α, β, and c, where the sketching matrix A ∈ Zr×n satisfies r ≪ n.Suppose A uses any estimator f : Zr×n → {− 1, +1 } and outputs f (A, Ax ) for each input query x.Then, there exists a randomized adaptive attack that, with high constant probability, after making at most ˜O (r8) queries to A, constructs a distribution D over Zn on which A fails with constant probability. Furthermore, this attack runs in time polynomial in r.Proof. First, without loss of generality, assume n = poly (r) since we can restrict queries to the first 

poly (r) coordinates and set the rest to zero, effectively focusing on the first poly (r) columns of the sketching matrix A.Next, we prove the correctness of our attack. Suppose the algorithm A uses estimator f and at time t we sample x ∼ Dpt . Consider an algorithm A′ using the same f , but receiving input 

> [

Dx ′

Sx S

> ]

,

where x′ ∼ D|D| 

> γ

for fixed γ ∈ [α, β ], independent of x. Since Dx and Sx are independent given pt,by Lemma 5.5.23 the total variation distance between 

> [

Dx (t)

> D

Sx (t)

> S
> ]

and  

> [

Dx ′

Sx (t)

> S
> ]

is at most 1poly( n) for each t. Thus, 

dtv 

> (

{A (x(t))}ℓt=1 , {A ′(x(t))}ℓt=1 

> )

≤ ℓ · 1poly( n) = 1poly( n) .

– 195 – Therefore, it suffices to show that interacting with A′ allows us to find an attack distribution on which A′ fails with high constant probability. Note that A′ depends only on xS . By Lemma 5.5.9, with probability at least 1 − 1 

> n

, no indices outside S are falsely identified. Moreover, Lemma 5.5.18 ensures that with high constant probability, the attack identifies some (or all) coordinates in S and produces a distribution on which A′ fails. From this, it follows that A also fails on this distribution with constant probability. Taking a union bound on these events, our attack finds a hard query distribution q where A fails with constant probability. Finally, regarding complexity, each of the ℓ iterations makes O (1) queries, so total queries are 

O (ℓ) = O

> (

r8 log 7 n

> )

= ˜O (r8). Since only accumulated scores sti are maintained at each iteration 

t ∈ [ℓ] and n = poly( r), total runtime is O (ℓn ) = poly( r).

5.5.4 Moment Matching on Dense Part

5.5.5 Analysis of Attack on F0 Estimation 

Theorem 5.5.24. [ GLW +24 ] Let A be a streaming algorithm that uses a sketching matrix A ∈ Zr×n

with r ≪ n and a post-processing function f to solve the (α + c, β − c)-gap F0 gap norm problem with some constant α, β and c.Then, there exists a randomized algorithm that makes an adaptive sequence of queries to A,and with high constant probability, generates a distribution D on Zn such that A fails on D with constant probability. Moreover, this adaptive attack algorithm makes at most ˜O (r8) queries and runs in poly( r) time. Proof. We first observe that we can assume n = poly (r) without loss of generality, since we can always query on the first poly (r) coordinates and set the remaining poly (r) coordinates of the query vector x to 0. This corresponds to attacking the first poly( r) columns of the sketching matrix A.Now, suppose that the algorithm A uses post-processing function f (A, Ax ) for each query x.Suppose we sample x ∼ Dpt at time t and consider instead an algorithm A′ that uses the same estimator f , but instead takes the input 

> [

Dx ′

Sx S ,

> ]

where x′ ∼ D|D| 

> γ

for a fixed γ ∈ [α, β ] that is independent of input x, and S is a small disjoint set of coordinates. Observe that Dx ′ and Sx S are independent conditioned on pt. Hence by Lemma 5.5.23, for each iteration t, the total variation distance between 

> [

Dx (t)

> D

Sx (t)

> S
> ]

and  

> [

Dx ′

Sx (t)

> S
> ]

is at most 1poly( n) , where we use D to denote the coordinates outside of S. Therefore, 

dtv 

> (

{A (x(t))}t=1 ,2,··· ,ℓ , {A ′(x(t))}t=1 ,2,··· ,ℓ 

> )

≤ ℓ · 1poly( n) = 1poly( n) .

Thus, it suffices to show that we can find the attack distribution on which A′ fails with high constant probability just by interacting with A′. Importantly, A′ only uses xS as input to its computation. By Lemma 5.5.9, we never falsely accuse any index i / ∈ S , with probability at least 1 − 1 

> n

. Moreover, by Lemma 5.5.18, the attack correctly identifies (some, or all) coordinates i ∈ S and outputs a distribution on which A′ fails, with high constant probability. From the total variation distance bounds in the above discussion, it follows that A must also fail on this distribution with high constant probability. Conditioning on these events and taking a union bound, it follows that the attack find a query distribution on which A fails with high constant probability. It remains to analyze the query complexity and time complexity of the attack. In each of the ℓ iterations, the attack makes O (1) queries. Hence, the total number of queries is O (ℓ) =

O

> (

r8 log 7 n

> )

= ˜O (r8).The attack only maintains the accumulated correlation scores sti in each iteration t ∈ [ℓ]. Thus, the total runtime of the attack is O (ℓn ) = poly (r), since without loss of generality, it suffices to assume n = poly( r).– 203 – 5.6 Dense-Sparse Decomposition 

In this section, we show a more general positive bound by [ BEO22 ], which demonstrates that we can do better than the ˜O (√m) space algorithm that results from applying the differential privacy technique from Section 4.2. As previously discussed, techniques that rely solely on the flip number λ inherently require ˜Ω(√λ) space, yielding no significant improvement for moment estimation problems where λ = Θ( m). Instead, the approach by [ BEO22 ] tracks the frequency moments by switching between two distinct regimes. In the sparse regime , the current frequency vector is explicitly maintained using standard sparse recovery methods. In the dense regime , the previous differential privacy techniques are utilized. This hybrid approach allows [ BEO22 ] to surpass the previously known ˜Θ(m1/2) space barrier for fixed p. Specifically, [ BEO22 ] achieves a space complexity of ˜O(m2/5) for estimating F2, and ˜O(m1/3) for estimating F0, the number of distinct elements. To achieve this, [ BEO22 ] exploits a crucial structural property specific to Fp-moment estimation: the p-th moment cannot undergo significant changes unless the frequency vector is either small or highly sparse. Based on this observation, [ BEO22 ] introduces a switching strategy that maintains the algorithm in either a sparse or dense state, according to a threshold T . If the frequency vector contains at most T non-zero entries, it is deemed sparse; if it exceeds 4T non-zeros, it is considered dense. Intermediate cases may be temporarily classified either way. In the sparse regime, [ BEO22 ] adopts a simple strategy of explicitly storing the frequency vector using a sparse representation, incurring only O (T ) space. This allows exact computation of the Fp

moment. In the dense regime, [ BEO22 ] applies the robust DP algorithm from Section 4.1 introduced by [ HKM +22 ], which uses differential privacy not to protect input data, but to preserve the internal randomness of a collection of k estimators. This framework introduces a ˜O(√λ) overhead due to repeated estimator queries. The key observation by [ BEO22 ] is that for dense vectors, the Fp moment cannot change too quickly. For example, when p = 0 or p = 1 , Ω( T ) updates are needed to change the moment by a constant factor; for p = 2 , at least Ω( √T ) updates are required. Consequently, the effective flip number in the dense regime is much smaller than λ, and we can reduce the number of estimator queries accordingly, significantly decreasing the overall space complexity. Finally, we must handle regime transitions carefully. When moving from sparse to dense, the frequency vector is known exactly and can be used to initialize the dense regime. Conversely, when transitioning from dense to sparse, [ BEO22 ] applies the sparse recovery techniques of Theorem 5.6.1 to reconstruct the frequency vector. To detect when this transition should occur, the robust distinct element counting algorithm of Theorem 5.6.4 is run in parallel; this subroutine only needs to be queried every Ω( T ) updates. The algorithm appears in full in Algorithm 13. We utilize the following streaming algorithm for sparse recovery, which outputs all the coordinates of an underlying frequency vector if it is sparse. 

Theorem 5.6.1. [GSTV07 ] There exists a deterministic algorithm SparseRecover that recovers a k-sparse frequency vector defined by a turnstile stream of length n. The algorithm uses k ·polylog (n)

bits of space.

(1 + ε)-approximation to ∥x∥pp.
