We now show correctness of the dense-sparse decomposition in Algorithm 13. 

Lemma 5.6.7. [BEO22 ] With high probability, the output Z by Algorithm 13 at each time t ∈ [m]

is a (1 + ε)-approximation for ∥x∥pp, where x is the frequency vector at time t.Proof. Let t ∈ [m] be a fixed time. Observe that since each block of length ℓ makes a single query to FPEst and LZeroEst , the total number of queries is at most b = mℓ and thus it suffices for these subroutines to be robust to b such adaptive queries. Let E be the event that all instances of the algorithms LZeroEst , FPEst , and SparseRecover succeed at all times 1, . . . , t − 1 prior to t. By Theorem 4.2.1, Theorem 5.6.2, and Theorem 5.6.4, we have that LZeroEst and FPEst 

succeed with high probability at all times across b adaptive queries. By Theorem 5.6.1, we have that SparseRecover is deterministic and thus succeeds. Therefore, E holds with high probability, i.e., Pr [E] ≥ 1 − 1poly( n) . Let t0 be the beginning of the block that contains time t.Conditioned on E, we have that the flag is set to SPARSE at t0 only if there are at most 2T

nonzero coordinates at time t0, by the correctness of LZeroEst at time t0. Over the course of a block of length ℓ ≤ T , at most ℓ ≤ T more coordinates in the frequency vector can be nonzero. Hence, there can be at most 2T + ℓ ≤ 3T nonzero coordinates at time t if the flag is set to SPARSE 

at time t0. By the correctness of SparseRecover for recovering all coordinates of a 4T -sparse vector, we can exactly identify the nonzero coordinates, thereby recovering x exactly, i.e., the vector 

g returned by SparseRecover is identical to x. Therefore, Z = ∥g∥pp = ∥x∥pp.In the other case, the flag at time t0, the beginning of the block of time t, must have been DENSE .In this case, there must have been at least 12 · T nonzero coordinates by the correctness of LZeroEst 

at time t0. Let h be the frequency vector at time t0, so that ̂ G is a (1 + ε

Finally, we remark by using heavy-hitter algorithms to further track large updates to a small number of coordinates, [ WZ24 ] is able to further decrease the flip number of the dense regime and therefore achieve slightly better space dependencies in m.Quantitatively, the improvement of [ WZ24 ] is mild, e.g., roughly ˜O (m0.373 ) rather than the bound of ˜O (m0.375 ) by [ BEO22 ] for Fp moment estimation for p = 1 .5. However, these results nevertheless demonstrate that the dense-sparse decomposition does not serve as a natural barrier. – 207 – – 208 – Chapter 6 

# White-Box Model 

- -

Even if an algorithm’s entire data structure is revealed to an adversary, cryptographic tools can still provide robustness. 

In this chapter, we focus on the white-box adversarial model, where an adversary Adv again has repeated interactions with an algorithm Alg through a data stream that represents the queries of 

Adv . As in the black-box setting defined in Chapter 3, the adversary Adv has access to the previous outputs of the algorithm Alg . Additionally, the adversary Adv now also has the internal state of the algorithm Alg at each time step over the duration of the stream. Formally, the white-box adversarial model can be defined through a two-player game between a streaming algorithm Alg and a source Adv of adaptive or adversarial input to Alg . As in the black-box setting, a query function Q is fixed before the game begins. Subsequently, the game proceeds over m rounds, so that in the t-th round: (1) Adv computes an update st for the stream, which possibly depends on all previous stream updates and observations by Adv .(2) Alg updates its internal data structures Dt with st, possibly drawing a fresh batch Rt of random bits, and outputs a response Zt.(3) Adv observes the response Zt, the data structures Dt, and the random bits Rt.The algorithm Alg is permitted space sublinear in the size of the input m and only a single pass over the stream. As before, the goal of the adversary Adv is to compel an incorrect response Zt to the query Q at some time t ∈ [m] throughout the stream through its choices of s1, . . . , s m.

Applications of white-box adversaries. The white-box adversarial model captures situations in which an adversary can observe and adapt to the internal state of an algorithm, going beyond what is possible in the black-box setting. This added flexibility allows the model to reflect a range of realistic scenarios in which future inputs are influenced by the algorithm’s own behavior. A canonical example comes from dynamic algorithms. Here, the goal is to maintain a data structure that produces correct answers at every time step t ∈ [m] as updates u1, . . . , u m arrive one by one, while keeping the overall running time or worst-case update time small. In some – 209 – applications, space is also a limiting factor and the data structure must use space sublinear in the input size, though this is not always required. In this setting it is common to consider adaptive adversaries [ Cha10 , Waj20 , CH21 , RSW22 ] that choose each update after inspecting the current state of the data structure, naturally fitting the white-box adversarial model. More broadly, an algorithm’s internal state may play an active role in shaping future inputs. To see this, consider a distributed streaming scenario in which a central coordinator seeks to compute statistics over data generated by many remote users. To save space, the coordinator may avoid storing all updates, and to reduce communication costs it may share parts of its internal state S,e.g., random seeds or sketch parameters, with the users. The users may then rely on this shared state in their own local processes, which in turn influences how subsequent data is generated. In this case, the data stream depends directly on components of the coordinator’s internal state, a dependence that is naturally captured by the white-box model. If some users are malicious and deliberately exploit knowledge of S to cause the coordinator to fail, the resulting inputs are both state-dependent and adversarial. Similar considerations apply when the coordinator’s state is stored in the cloud and is visible to all users. Related ideas also appear in the pan-private streaming model [ DNP +10 ], which allows the internal state of an algorithm to be partially or fully exposed. This model is motivated by applications involving distributed data curators such as hospitals, government agencies, or large technology companies. As noted in [ DNP +10 ], a data curator may be pressured to allow data to be used in ways that go beyond its original purpose, potentially influencing the distribution of future inputs. In a similar spirit, [MMNW11] studies problems such as distinct counting and heavy-hitter detection when the internal state of the algorithm is revealed, motivated by scenarios in which an insider manipulates traffic patterns while probing a system that tracks user statistics. While the focus there is on privacy, these examples also highlight the relevance of a white-box adversarial perspective, in which future inputs need not be independent of previously revealed internal information. White-box adversaries have also become central in the study of adversarial robustness in machine learning. Many of the most effective attacks rely on access to a model’s parameters and training weights in order to craft inputs that cause misclassification while remaining close to a given example [ BCM +13 , SZS +14 , GSS15 ]. Subsequent work has shown that such attacks can produce perturbations that are almost imperceptible to humans, both in digital images [ SZS +14 , HPG +17 ]and in physical settings [ KGB17a , SBBR16 , AEIK18 ]. This line of work has led to a substantial literature on designing learning algorithms that are robust to white-box attacks, e.g., [ IEM18 ,MMS +18, SST +18, TKP +18, CZM +18, KGB17b, LCLS17]. Finally, white-box considerations also arise in persistent data structures, whose purpose is to provide access to past versions of a data structure while keeping space and query time under control [ DSST89 , FK03 , Kap04 ]. In collaborative systems that provide version control over shared repositories, the underlying persistent data structures may be visible to all users. Updates made by different collaborators may then depend explicitly on previously stored states, again fitting naturally within the white-box adversarial framework. 

# 6.1 Lower Bounds 

A natural question is whether there exist robust streaming algorithms against white-box adversaries for standard problems in the streaming model, such as Lp norm estimation or distinct element estimation. Somewhat surprisingly, there exists a general technique to prove lower bounds for – 210 – randomized white-box robust algorithms through lower bounds for deterministic two-player commu-nication problems. Informally, we say a streaming algorithm solves a two-player communication game if there is a reduction from the communication problem to the streaming problem. The players encode their private inputs as parts of a single data stream and jointly simulate the streaming algorithm on this stream. The memory state of the streaming algorithm is passed between the players, and its final output is used to recover the value of the communication game. Under this reduction, the space used by the streaming algorithm corresponds directly to the communication cost of the protocol. 

Theorem 6.1.1. Suppose there exists a randomized white-box robust streaming algorithm that uses 

S(n, ε ) space and solves a two-player communication game with S(n, ε ) bits of communication with some constant probability p ∈

. Then there exists a deterministic protocol for the two-player communication game that uses S(n, ε ) bits of communication and succeeds on at least a p fraction of all possible inputs. Proof. Suppose there exists a randomized white-box robust streaming algorithm A that uses S(n, ε )

space and solves a two-player communication game P with S(n, ε ) bits of communication with some constant probability p ∈  

> (12

, 1

> ]

. We say a random string R chosen by the first player is good , if 

R results in a two-player protocol with the correct answer, over all possible inputs to the second player and at least a p fraction of the possible random strings chosen by the second player. By the definition of white-box robustness, the algorithm succeeds with probability at least p even if the second player’s input is chosen adaptively by an adversary who observes the internal state of the algorithm. Thus, the expected success probability over the first player’s random string R, evaluated against the worst-case second-player input, is at least p. An averaging argument then implies that for any local input of the first player, there exists at least one good random string R.Now, the first player can enumerate over all possible inputs to the second player as well as all possible random strings and then select a good random string to generate a state S of the algorithm 

A on their local input, which uses S(n, ε ) bits to store. The first player then sends the state S

of the algorithm to the second player, using S(n, ε ) bits of communication. Because the chosen state S guarantees a success probability of at least p over the second player’s randomness for every possible pair of inputs, an averaging argument over all inputs implies there exists a globally fixed random string for the second player that achieves the correct answer on at least a p fraction of all possible inputs. The second player can then update the algorithm with their input and a fixed string. Consequently, the white-box robust algorithm results in a deterministic protocol that solves the one-way two-player communication game with S(n, ε ) bits of communication that succeeds on at least a p-fraction of all inputs. # 6.2 Upper Bounds 

6.2.1 F0 Estimation Against Computationally-Bounded White-Box Adversaries 

In this section, we present the streaming algorithm by [ ABJ +22 ] for estimating the F0 value that is robust against white-box adversaries. As established by the lower bound in Theorem 6.1.4, it is impossible to approximate the F0 value of a frequency vector x to an arbitrarily small constant in sublinear space. However, perhaps unexpectedly, it is still possible to achieve a multiplicative approximation within a factor of nε for any small constant ε > 0 provided the adversary is 

computationally bounded . This assumption is in line with standard practices in cryptography. Our notion of a computationally bounded adversary will be based on the hardness of the Short Integer Solution (SIS) problem, a well-known assumption from lattice-based cryptography, defined as follows: 

Definition 6.2.1 (Short Integer Solution (SIS) Problem) . Let n, m, q be integers and let β > 0.Given a uniformly random matrix A ∈ Zn×mq , the short integer solution (SIS) problem is to find a nonzero integer vector z ∈ Zm such that Az ≡ 0n mod q and ∥z∥2 ≤ β.

We remark that in Definition 6.2.1, the parameters can be chosen so that there does exist such a nonzero integer vector z ∈ Zm such that Az ≡ 0n mod q and ∥z∥2 ≤ β, by a simple Pigeonhole argument, so the defined task is not vacuously impossible. Since the foundational work of Ajtai [ Ajt96 ], it has been known that the SIS problem exhibits average-case to worst-case hardness. Specifically, for suitable choices of parameters, solving an “average” case instance of SIS is at least as difficult as approximating certain key lattice problems in the worst case. 

Theorem 6.2.2. [MP13 ] Let n be an integer, and suppose m, β, and q are polynomially bounded in n, with the additional condition that q ≥ n · β. Then, solving the SIS problem with parameters 

n, m, q, β and with non-negligible success probability is at least as hard as approximating the Shortest Vector Problem (SVP) within a factor γ, where γ ∈ poly( n).

– 213 – In the cryptographic literature, lattice-based cryptographic constructions are typically designed under the assumption that approximating lattice problems within a factor γ < 2o(n log log n/ log n) is computationally hard. The best known polynomial-time algorithms, such as the LLL algorithm [Vai15 ], only achieve an approximation factor of γ = 2 O(n log log n/ log n). Any improvement beyond this barrier to a significantly smaller γ would constitute a major breakthrough in the field. Based on this, we adopt the following computational hardness assumption, which implies that breaking our algorithms would require progress that challenges fundamental cryptographic assumptions: 

Assumption 6.2.3. No polynomial-time algorithm can approximate the Shortest Vector Problem (SVP) in n dimensions within a factor of γ = 2 o(n(log log n)/ log n).

The F0 estimation algorithm by [ ABJ +22 ] only requires computational hardness for significantly smaller values of γ. The algorithm proceeds as follows. First, partition the universe [n] into n1−ε

contiguous blocks, each consisting of nε coordinates. For each block, maintain a separate vector by applying updates using a fixed sketching matrix A, which is derived from the hardness of the SIS problem. At the end of the stream, the algorithm outputs an estimate equal to the number of these n1−ε sketches that are nonzero. Importantly, the same matrix A is reused across all blocks, as described in more detail in Algorithm 14. 

Algorithm 14 F0 Estimation Algorithm on Turnstile Streams from Computationally-Bounded White-Box Adversaries 

Input: Universe size n, accuracy parameter ε, and a stream of updates u1, u 2, . . . , where each 

ut ∈ [n] denotes an update to a coordinate of the frequency vector x

Output: An nε-multiplicative approximation of ∥x∥0 

> 1:

Let A ∈ Zncε ×nε 

> q

be a uniformly random matrix, where q = poly( n) and 0 < c < 12 

> 2:

Partition [n] into n1−ε consecutive chunks, each of size nε 

> 3:

Initialize n1−ε sketch vectors in Zncε  

> q

, all initially ⃗ 0, one per chunk  

> 4:

for each update ut in the stream do  

> 5:

Determine the chunk index i and local coordinate k within that chunk corresponding to ut 

> 6:

Update the i-th sketch vector by adding ut · Ak, where Ak is the k-th column of A 

> 7:

return the number of nonzero sketch vectors at the end of the stream We now argue that if the final frequency vector x satisfies ∥x∥∞ ≤ poly (n), then our algorithm provides an nε-multiplicative approximation for the F0 value. Additionally, the algorithm can be made more space-efficient under the random oracle model introduced by Bellare and Rogaway [ BR93 ]. In the random oracle model, both the algorithm and the adversary have access to a public random function. Each query to this oracle returns a uniformly random output from a fixed domain, and repeated queries with the same input yield the same output. This model is widely used in cryptographic design [ BR93 , BR96 , CGH04 , KM15 ], and in practice, hash functions like SHA256 can serve as heuristic implementations of such oracles. We remark that the random oracle assumption was also removed by [ FJW24 ], who also gave algorithms with faster update time. Instead of the random oracle model, [ FJW24 ] leverages a suitably chosen fully homomorphic encryption (FHE) scheme satisfying mild structural properties to build the relevant hash functions. These structural properties are currently satisfied by most schemes based on LWE and Ring-LWE. At a high level, this construction bears resemblance to classical approaches for building collision-resistant functions from private information retrieval (PIR) schemes, where the PIR is instantiated using fully homomorphic encryption. – 214 – We emphasize that to apply Theorem 6.2.2, it suffices for the approximation factor γ to be 

poly (n). Moreover, our algorithm remains valid in the turnstile streaming setting, where updates to the coordinates of x may be both positive and negative. This is because Theorem 6.2.2 only requires ∥x∥∞ ≤ poly( n), not a bound on the signs of the entries. 

Theorem 6.2.4. [ABJ +22 ] Let c ∈

> (

0, 12

> )

, and suppose that Assumption 6.2.3 holds. Then there exists a streaming algorithm on turnstile streams generated by polynomial time-bounded white box adversaries, which outputs an nε-multiplicative approximation to the F0 value in the turnstile streaming model. Moreover, the algorithm has a space complexity of ˜O(n1−ε+cε + n(1+ c)ε). In the random oracle model, the space usage improves to ˜O(n1−ε+cε ).Proof. Consider Algorithm 14. From Theorem 6.2.2 and Assumption 6.2.3, no polynomial-time adversary can find a nonzero integer vector z with ∥z∥∞ ≤ poly (n) such that Az = 0 . Therefore, if a sketch vector (for a given chunk) equals zero, it implies that all coordinates within that chunk have zero frequency in x at the end of the stream. Conversely, if the sketch vector is nonzero, at least one coordinate in the corresponding chunk must be nonzero in x. Since each chunk contains at most nε coordinates, this yields a multiplicative approximation of at most nε, as desired. For the space complexity, observe that the matrix A does not need to be stored explicitly if we adopt the random oracle model; its columns can be generated on demand via oracle queries. Hence, the primary space usage arises from maintaining n1−ε vectors of dimension ncε , one for each chunk of size nε.

6.2.2 Vector Recovery 

We can further work with the SIS problem without going through the SVP problem. To this end, we remark the best known algorithm for solving the SVP within a poly (n) approximation is due to [ADRS15] and runs in ˜O(2 n) time. 

Assumption 6.2.5. Given n ∈ N, there exist parameters m, β, q ≤ poly (n) with q ≥ n · β, so that no adversary running in time o(2 n) can solve the SIS n,m,q,β problem with non-negligible probability. 

We now describe the first of multiple algorithms that follow a unified two-stage framework. The framework runs two procedures in parallel: (i) a detection algorithm that determines whether the input belongs to a structured class, such as being sparse, low rank, or both, and (ii) a deterministic recovery algorithm that efficiently reconstructs the input once membership in this class is certified. The detection procedure relies on the assumed hardness of the SIS problem to withstand adaptive, white-box adversaries, while the recovery procedure is fully deterministic and efficient in both time and space, ensuring robustness once the structure is verified. In this section, we will apply the framework to vector and matrix recovery problems, but in subsequent sections, we will also apply the framework to robust PCA, tensor recovery, and other problems as well. 

Lemma 6.2.6. [FW23 ] Suppose Assumption 6.2.5 holds and let A ∈ Zn×mq be drawn uniformly at random, where q, m, β ∈ poly (n) and q ≥ n · β. If a vector x ∈ Zmβ is produced by an adversary running in time o(2 n), then with probability at least 1 − negl (n) over the choice of A, there does not exist a k-sparse vector y ∈ Zmβ such that x̸ ≡ y (mod q) but Ax ≡ Ay (mod q), for any 

k ∈ o  

> (n
> log n
> )

– 215 – Proof. If an adversary were able to find a vector y ∈ Zmβ such that x̸ ≡ y (mod q) but Ax ≡ Ay 

(mod q), then it could compute (x − y) mod q, yielding a non-zero, short vector in the kernel of 

A, thereby solving the SIS problem. Since the entries of y are bounded by q, the total number of 

k-sparse vectors in Zmβ is at most O

We remark that given a random matrix A ∈ Zn×mq , if both vectors x and y are k-sparse with bounded entries, an information-theoretic union bound implies that, with high probability, any distinct x̸ = y will satisfy Ax ̸ = Ay . However, there can exist a binary vector x that is not k-sparse and a k-sparse vector y with bounded entries such that Ax = Ay . In these scenarios, the SIS assumption is required to argue that it is computationally hard for an adversary to find such an x

and deceive the algorithm.

Given that there are poly (n)k possible coefficient combinations for each dependent column and (√m − k) such columns, the total number of choices for dependent columns is poly( n)(√m−k)k. 6.2.3 k-Sparse Recovery Algorithm 

Theorem 6.2.8. [FW23 ] Suppose Assumption 6.2.5 holds. Given a parameter k ∈ Θ  

> (nc
> log n
> )

with any constant c > 0, and given an input vector of length n whose integer entries are bounded by 

poly (n), there exists a streaming algorithm that is secure against o(nk) time-bounded white-box adversaries. This algorithm can decide whether the input vector is k-sparse, and if so, reconstructs the k-sparse vector while using only ˜O(k) bits of space within the random oracle model. Proof. Consider Algorithm 15, which identifies and reconstructs a k-sparse vector using only ˜O(k)

bits of space. The input to the algorithm is a stream of integer updates to an underlying vector, where each coordinate is assumed to be bounded by β ∈ poly (n) at all times. Consequently, we can treat all updates as being modulo q for q, β ∈ poly( n) and q ≥ n · β.– 216 – When the input vector x is k-sparse, Lemma 6.2.6 ensures that for a uniformly random sketching matrix A, if Ay = Ax mod q, then it must be that y = x. Therefore, in this case, Algorithm 15 successfully recovers x by exhaustively checking all possible k-sparse vectors. In contrast, if the input vector has sparsity greater than k, Lemma 6.2.6 guarantees that the post-processing step will not find any k-sparse vector y such that Ay = v mod q. Hence, the algorithm correctly outputs 

None . In the random oracle model, the columns of the random matrix A can be generated on demand. Thus, the algorithm only needs to maintain a sketch vector of length f (k) · log n with entries bounded by poly( n), leading to a total space complexity of ˜O(k) bits. 

Algorithm 15 Sparse-Recovery( n, m, k)

Input: A stream of m updates ut to a vector of length n, with entries in [−β, β ] for β ∈ poly (n)

and a modulus q ∈ poly( n) with q ≫ β

Output: The frequency vector if it is k-sparse or None otherwise Let f (k) ∈ ω(k) ∩ ˜O (k)

Sample a uniformly random matrix A ∈ Zf (k)·log n×nq

Initialize a vector v ∈ Zf (k)·log nq with all zeros 

for each update ut ∈ [n] with t ∈ [m] do 

Let i be the coordinate index updated by ut

Update v ← v + ut · Ai, where Ai is the ith column of A

for each k-sparse vector y ∈ Zn with entries in [−β, β ] do if Ay ≡ v mod q then return y

return None 

We remark that any algorithm for k-sparse recovery that uses approximately k words of space and aims to be robust against white-box adversaries must assume that the adversary is limited to at most nk time. This is because an algorithm with k words of memory can only maintain at most 

nk distinct internal states. Consequently, there exists some k-sparse vector x and some k′ sparse vector x′ with k′ > k that results in the same internal state as x. An adversary with nk time could efficiently find such a pair (x, x′). If the adversary inserts either x or x′ into the stream, followed by −x, the algorithm would be unable to distinguish whether the true input is the zero vector or 

x′ − x. Therefore, the above algorithm achieves near-optimality by using only ˜O(k) bits of space, assuming the adversary is limited to o(2 k log n) = o(nk) time. 

Fast recovery. On the other hand, Algorithm 15 performs a brute-force enumeration over all possible k-sparse vectors in the post-processing stage, which is time-inefficient. To achieve a faster version of k-sparse recovery, it suffices to run in parallel an existing deterministic fast recovery scheme for k-sparse inputs. 

Theorem 6.2.9. [ Jaf11 ] There exists a deterministic sparse recovery scheme that uses ˜O (k) bits of space and outputs a k-sparse vector defined by a data stream on a universe of size n. The algorithm uses poly( n) post-processing time. 

The algorithm in Theorem 6.2.9 can recover the input vector if it is k-sparse, even against white-box adversaries, since the algorithm is deterministic. However, the algorithm fails when the – 217 – input vector is not k-sparse. Therefore, we can run Algorithm 15 and the algorithm of Theorem 6.2.9 in parallel. If the deterministic k-sparse recovery scheme outputs a vector y∗, then it suffices to check whether Ay ∗ matches the vector y stored by Algorithm 15. Putting these together, we have: 

Theorem 6.2.10. [ FW23 ] Suppose Assumption 6.2.5 holds. Given any constant c > 0 and parameter k ∈ Θ  

> (nc
> log n
> )

, there exists a streaming algorithm that operates in poly (n) time and uses only ˜O(k) bits of space in the random oracle model. Given an input vector of length n defined by a turnstile stream generated by a white-box adversary with o(nk) runtime, the algorithm determines whether the input is k-sparse and, if so, correctly recovers the k-sparse vector. 

Applications to F0 estimation. By leveraging our k-sparse recovery algorithm as a subroutine, we can design an efficient algorithm for estimating the F0 value of vectors defined by white-box adversaries with bounded runtime. Namely, we set k = n1−ε to provide an nε-approximation to the 

F0 value of a vector, under the assumption that the vector’s entries are bounded by poly (n). Now, if the k-sparse algorithm returns None , then the F0 value is larger than n1−ε. However, since the vector has n coordinates, then the F0 value is certainly at most n, and thus we can simply output 

n1−ε as an nε-approximation to the F0 value. Otherwise, the vector has F0 value at most n1−ε, in which case the k-sparse algorithm will recover it exactly. 

Theorem 6.2.11. [FW23 ] Suppose Assumption 6.2.5 holds. Then given any constant ε < 1,there exists a streaming algorithm that, in the random oracle model, computes a multiplicative 

nε-approximation to the F0 value of a vector of length n, using ˜O(n1−ε) bits of space and poly (n)

time. Moreover, the algorithm is robust against white-box adversaries running in o(nn1−ε

) time. Proof. Let v be the underlying vector. Observe that if ∥v∥0 ≥ n1−ε, then the k-sparse algorithm will return None for k = n1−ε, in which case our algorithm will output n1−ε. Since ∥v∥0 ≤ n, then 

n1−ε is an nε-approximation to ∥v∥0. On the other hand, if ∥v∥0 ≤ n1−ε then the k-sparse algorithm will recover it exactly. The space and runtime guarantees follow from the guarantees of the k-sparse algorithm in Theorem 6.2.10, with the setting of k = n1−ε.

6.2.4 Matrix Recovery 

6.2.4.1 Low-Rank Matrix Recovery 

Beyond sparse vector recovery, these techniques also extend to low-rank matrix recovery. We introduce a white-box adversarially robust algorithm by [ FW23 ] for this task that is both time and space-efficient. Analogous to the method used in the k-sparse vector setting, the approach ensures fast update time while maintaining correctness when the input matrix exceeds the target rank by simultaneously maintaining two sketches: one constructed using a uniformly random matrix to detect when the input rank is too high for recovery, and another designed to enable recovery when the input is indeed of low rank.

We state our main theorem for matrix recovery: 

Theorem 6.2.13. [ FW23 ] Suppose Assumption 6.2.5 holds. Then given any integer parameter k,there exists a streaming algorithm that takes an input matrix generated by a white-box adversary with o(nnk ) runtime and either correctly reports that the input matrix has rank exceeding k, or successfully recovers the matrix if its rank is at most k. The algorithm uses ˜O(nk ) bits of space and runs in poly( n) time within the random oracle model. Proof. Consider Algorithm 16. Given an input matrix X ∈ Zn×nβ with β ∈ poly (n) and a modulus 

q ≥ n · β, if rank (X) ≤ k, then by the uniqueness guarantee of Theorem 6.2.12, X can be recovered by solving a convex program, and the sketch v computed via H matches the product H · vec (X).Conversely, if rank (X) > k , then by Lemma 6.2.7 under the SIS assumption, no other low-rank matrix Y satisfies H · vec( Y) = H · vec( X) mod q, so the algorithm correctly returns None .In the random oracle model, the sketching matrices H and A can be generated on demand, allowing the algorithm to maintain only two vectors of length ˜O(nk ) with entries bounded by 

poly (n), resulting in a total space usage of ˜O(nk ) bits. Recovery involves solving a convex program using the ellipsoid method and verifying the result, both of which can be done in poly (n) time. – 219 – We again remark the near-optimality of the low-rank matrix recovery algorithm: using ap-proximately ˜O(nk ) bits of space, any algorithm that is robust against white-box adversaries must assume that the adversary is bounded to nnk time. Without this time constraint, an adversary could identify two matrices X̸ = X′ such that the algorithm reaches the same internal state on both, and 

rank( X′ − X) > k . By inserting X followed by −X, or X′ followed by −X, the input would result in either the zero matrix or X′ − X, which the algorithm could not distinguish. Thus, the previous algorithm, which uses ˜O(nk ) space and assumes an adversary with runtime o(2 nk log n) = o(nnk ), is essentially optimal under these constraints. 

Applications to rank decision problem. The low-rank matrix recovery algorithm can be applied to a number of other problems on data streams. For example, consider the following definition of the rank decision problem: 

Definition 6.2.14 (Rank Decision Problem) . Given an integer k ≥ 0, and a matrix A ∈ Rn×n,determine whether the rank of A is larger than k.

Then the following holds by running Algorithm 16 with parameter k.

Theorem 6.2.15. [ FW23 ] Suppose Assumption 6.2.5 holds. Then given any integer parameter k,there exists a streaming algorithm that, in the random oracle model, solves the rank decision problem using ˜O(nk ) bits of space and poly (n) time. Moreover, the algorithm is robust against white-box adversaries running in time o(nnk ).

Applications to graph matching. Next, we consider the maximum matching problem, defined as follows: 

Definition 6.2.16 (Maximum Matching Problem) . Given an undirected graph G = ( V, E ) defined by a sequence of insertions and deletions to edges in a stream, the maximum matching problem is to identify a maximum cardinality set of vertex disjoint edges in G.

Theorem 6.2.17. [ FW23 ] Suppose Assumption 6.2.5 holds and suppose an integer upper bound 

k′ on the maximum matching size is known. Then there exists a streaming algorithm that, in the random oracle model, computes a maximum matching using ˜O(nk ′) bits of space and poly (n) time. The algorithm is robust against white-box adversaries with running time bounded by o(n2nk ′

).Proof. To recover a maximum matching, we use the fact that the rank of the n × n Tutte matrix 

A associated with a graph G is equal to twice the size of a maximum matching in G. Specifically, 

Ai,j = 0 if there is no edge between vertices i and j, and otherwise, Ai,j = xi,j and Aj,i = −xi,j for distinct indeterminates xi,j . The rank of A is defined as the maximum rank over all real assignments to the xi,j . However, unlike traditional approaches, e.g., Sections 4.2.1 and 4.2.2 in [ CKL13 ], we cannot assign the xi,j values randomly during the stream in the white-box setting, as an adversary with access to the algorithm’s internal state could exploit this. To overcome this, we deterministically replace each xi,j with 1 as edges arrive in the stream, resulting in a fixed matrix A′. Since A′ is a special case of A, its rank is at most that of A, which is bounded above by twice the maximum matching size. We then apply our low-rank matrix recovery algorithm with parameter k = 2 k′, where k′ is an upper bound on the size of the maximum matching. If the rank of A′ exceeds 2k′, it implies the true matching size exceeds k′, and we terminate and report this. Otherwise, we recover A′ successfully. Once the stream ends, the nonzero entries in A′

– 220 – correspond exactly to the edges in G, allowing us to reconstruct A and thereby the original graph 

G. We can then apply any offline algorithm to compute a maximum matching. We remark that while Theorem 6.2.17 provides an efficient algorithm for maximum matching when the white-box adversary is computationally bounded, [ EKSZ26 ] established an unconditional 

Ω( n) memory lower bound for estimating the maximum matching size against information-theoretic (unbounded) white-box adversaries. 

6.2.5 Extension to Robust PCA and Tensors 

A common approach in the previous algorithms is to run two procedures in parallel: (1) an algorithm that identifies whether the input belongs to a restricted class of inputs, such as those that are sparse, low rank, or both, and (2) a deterministic algorithm that efficiently recovers the input if it is confirmed to be from that class. The correctness of the first algorithm depends on the hardness of the SIS problem, while the second algorithm is any deterministic method that is efficient in time and space, making it robust against white-box adversaries. [ FW23 ] observes that this framework can also be applied to robust PCA and tensors. 

6.2.5.1 Robust Principal Component Analysis 

The problem of Robust Principal Component Analysis is defined as follows: 

Definition 6.2.18 (Robust Principal Component Analysis) . Consider a data matrix M ∈ Zn×nq ,with q ≥ poly (n), which admits a decomposition M = L + S, where L ∈ Zn×nq has rank at most k,i.e., rank (L) ≤ k, and S ∈ Zn×nq is a sparse matrix containing no more than r non-zero entries. The goal of the robust principal component analysis (RPCA) problem is to recover the components L

and S.

The required precision for the rounded Gaussian random variables can be achieved using uniformly random bits generated by a random oracle, as described in [Kar16]. # Robust Algorithms for Turnstile Streams 

- -

In some cases, adversarially robust algorithms for turnstile streams with minimal space overhead are possible! 

As a number of adaptive attacks in Chapter 5 focus on linear sketches, one might ask whether there exist adaptive attacks on general streaming algorithms. In this chapter, we present a result of [GLW +26b ] that surprisingly shows that for many problems that satisfy an approximate notion of triangle inequality, there exists an adversarially robust streaming algorithm that can handle both insertions and deletions. In particular, [ GLW +26b ] showed that there exists an adversarially-robust algorithm that uses poly 

( 1 

> ε

, log n

)

space for (1 + ε)-approximation for F2 moment estimation on turnstile streams of length m = poly (n) with a universe of size n. We first give a technical overview of the various approaches in Section 7.1. We then describe the robust F2 estimation algorithm of [GLW +26b ] in Section 7.2 and the robust framework for many functions that satisfy approximate triangle inequality in Section 7.3. Finally, we present the robust algorithm of [ GLW +26b ], for L2

heavy-hitters on insertion-deletion streams in Section 7.4. Throughout the section, we follow the presentation of [GLW +26b]. 

# 7.1 Technical Overview 

In this section, we provide a technical overview of the main approach of [ GLW +26b ]. Recall from the discussion in the previous chapters that, given a data stream inducing a frequency vector 

x ∈ Rn, a standard approach is to sample a random sketching matrix A ∈ Rr×n and maintain the sketch Ax as the stream evolves. The matrix is chosen so that there exists a recovery function g

for which g(Ax ) serves as an estimator for the target quantity on x, for instance g(Ax ) ≈ ∥ x∥p.Because Ax consists of only r entries, the algorithm typically stores just O (r log n) bits over the course of the stream, and in many settings one can take r ≪ n. Recall that as a concrete example, constant-factor approximations to the Lp norm are achievable with r = polylog (n) when p ≤ 2, and with r = O

(

n1−2/p )

when p > 2.– 225 – However, the lower bounds by [ HW13 , GLW +24 , GLW +25 ] presented in Chapter 5 show that any linear sketch that is adversarially robust to insertion-deletion streams must have dimension 

Ω( n). A key issue is that linear sketches draw all their randomness once, at initialization, after which the algorithm behaves deterministically. An adaptive adversary can exploit this by issuing poly (n)

queries to progressively uncover the initial random choices, and then create an adversarial stream that “defeats” the fixed sketching matrix. This strategy essentially describes the approaches by [ HW13 , GLW +24 , GLW +25 ] presented in Chapter 5. Consequently, overcoming these lower bounds appears to require injecting some sort of fresh randomness during the execution of the streaming algorithm, rather than committing all randomness upfront. Our approach accomplishes this while maintaining small space by introducing new, independent sketches partway through the stream. To formalize this idea, suppose the frequency vector x decomposes as x = z + q, where z

represents the contribution of all updates up to some time t, and q captures the updates that occur after time t. In other words, z corresponds to a prefix of the stream, while q corresponds to the remaining suffix. Now consider a sketching matrix B that is initialized at time t + 1 , so that the algorithm maintains Bq . If B is a random sketch designed for estimating the F2 moment, then knowing Bz would allow us to recover Bx = Bz + Bq , and hence approximate ∥x∥22. Of course, Bz 

is not directly available, since B is only created after the updates defining z have already occurred. In what follows, we show that either ∥x∥22 can still be estimated accurately without access to Bz , or the algorithm can incrementally recover sufficient information about Bz to proceed. 

7.1.1 Technical Overview for F2 Moment Estimation 

We will first introduce the F2 moment because its inner product structure provides a clean and easily digestible exposition. However, we emphasize from the outset that the core algorithmic ideas are not limited solely to F2. As we will detail later in Section 7.1.2 and Section 7.3, a similar approach extends naturally to a broad class of norms and symmetric functions satisfying an approximate triangle inequality. There is, however, one crucial mathematical distinction regarding the approximation guarantees. F2 is unique in that the geometric properties of Hilbert spaces allow our algorithm to achieve a (1 + ε)-approximation, while general functions that rely solely on looser triangle inequalities give O (1) -approximations. Keeping this distinction in mind, we now detail the 

F2 construction. Now suppose there is an additional random sketch matrix A for F2 moment estimation that is initialized at the start of the stream, allowing the algorithm to maintain Az and later continue tracking Ax after time t. In this setting, the information that the algorithm has about the prefix z

is captured entirely by the sketch Az .If the suffix vector q points in a random direction that is independent of z, then it is unrealistic to expect to extract meaningful information about Bz from q. Crucially, this is also the regime in which such information is unnecessary for estimating ∥x∥22. Indeed, in high dimensions random vectors are nearly orthogonal, and thus we have 

∥z + q∥22 ≈ ∥ z∥22 + ∥q∥22,

which can be estimated separately using Az and Bq .On the other hand, if ∥z∥22 + ∥q∥22 provides a poor approximation to ∥x∥22, then, since 

∥x∥22 = ∥z + q∥22 = ∥z∥22 + 2 ⟨z, q⟩ + ∥q∥22,

– 226 – it must be the case that |⟨ z, q⟩| is somewhat large relative to both ∥z∥22 and ∥q∥22. This situation can only arise when z exhibits non-trivial alignment with q, in which case the suffix q potentially reveals information about z.Using the sketch Ax , we can test whether ∥z∥22 + ∥q∥22 is an inaccurate estimate. Depending on the outcome, the algorithm either outputs a reliable approximation to ∥x∥22 or extracts additional information about z. This intuition motivates a simplified version of our algorithm, which we describe next. 

Estimator, corrector, learner framework. We consider the following simplified setting: (1) Initially, we are given the sketch Az , after which vectors q arrive in a stream; for each q, we are able to maintain both Aq and Bq .(2) Upon receiving each q, the goal is to output an estimate of ∥z + q∥22.(3) We focus only on protecting the sketch A from an adversary; that is, we assume that norm estimates obtained from B are always accurate (for the moment, one may think of B as the identity matrix). Our simplified algorithm for F2 moment estimation in this setting is built around three key components: (1) An estimator , which produces an estimate of ∥z + q∥22.(2) A learner , whose purpose is to gradually acquire information about z in order to improve future estimates. Whenever the estimator fails, the learner extracts information about z from the current query q.(3) A corrector , which detects and informs the learner/estimator when the estimate is incorrect. The corrector maintains the sketch Ax and uses it to identify incorrect estimates. The learner maintains a vector z′, formed as a linear combination of the queries q on which the algorithm previously erred. The learner explicitly stores only the sketches Az ′ and Bz ′. The estimator maintains both Az and Bq . Given a query q, the estimator outputs ∥z − z′∥22 + ∥z′ + q∥22 as an estimate of ∥x∥22 = ∥z + q∥22, where the first term is approximated using Az − Az ′ and the second term is approximated using Bz ′ + Bq .As a consistency check, observe that if z′ = 0n, meaning no information about z has been learned, the estimator reduces to ∥z∥22 + ∥q∥22. At the other extreme, if z′ = z, so that z has been fully learned, the estimator exactly recovers ∥z + q∥22. Finally, whenever the estimator is detected to be incorrect, the algorithm simply outputs the estimate provided by the corrector for that query, based on Ax .

Robustness and progress. Using standard minimax arguments, it is enough to consider a deterministic adversary. Within our framework, one potential vulnerability is that the corrector relies on Ax to detect incorrect estimates. Each such interaction could reveal some information about A, so after multiple interactions, an adversary might be able to exploit their knowledge of A

to “fool” the corrector. To handle this, suppose that the estimator makes errors at most L times. We then apply the key idea from the bounded computation paths technique of [ BJWY22 ]: there are at most (mL

> )

– 227 – possible sets of times at which the corrector can flag errors over a stream of length m. Consequently, there are only (mL 

> )

adaptive input streams that a deterministic adversary can generate based on the information revealed by the corrector. By setting the failure probability of the corrector to 

δ = 

> (1poly( n)
> )L

· (mL

> )−1

, then by a union bound argument, the corrector will be robust against all possible streams from the adversary. Since the space of streaming algorithms typically scales with log 1 

> δ

, the resulting space complexity grows roughly linearly with L. We also use the estimate of the corrector at most L times as output, and A is used at most L times to estimate ∥z − z′∥22 after each update to z′. A sketch of size L can safely be used O (L) times even in the presence of an adaptive adversary (for example, by assigning a different block of rows to each query). Therefore, the main remaining task is to bound the total number L of times the estimator produces an incorrect output. To do this, consider each instance when the estimator is inaccurate. In such a case, z − z′ cannot be nearly orthogonal to z′ + q. By updating z′ towards −q (or away from −q), the distance to 

z decreases significantly, meaning that z′ becomes a better approximation of z. Formally, we use 

∥z − z′∥22 as our “progress measure”. An incorrect estimate of ∥z + q∥22 then implies

When the algorithm errs on query q, we update z′ to z′′ = z′ + α(q + z′) with a carefully chosen α.Note that in the sketch space, this can be done by scaling the sketch of z′ by (1 + α), followed by adding a multiple of the sketch of q. Then 

∥z − z′′ ∥22 = ∥z − z′ − α(q + z′)∥22

rows in A.– 228 – Recursive estimator. To extend the previous approach to a full streaming algorithm, the main challenge is that while the sketching matrix B allows access to Bq and lets us gradually learn Bz ,it still needs to correctly answer a large number of queries involving Bq throughout the stream. In other words, B itself must be robust against adaptive queries that appear along the way to q. To handle this, we apply recursion over the stream. Recall that our estimator is ∥z − z′∥22 + ∥z′ + q∥22, and we maintain both Az ′ and Bz ′ while updating z′. The first term, ∥z − z′∥22, is estimated using Az − Az ′. The key insight is that estimating the second term, ∥z′ + q∥22, is essentially the same problem on a shorter stream: given a (rounded) sketch Bz ′ for an unknown z′, estimate ∥z′ + q∥22 for q arriving in a stream. This enables a recursive approach for estimating the second term. 

Two levels and beyond. To illustrate the idea, consider a two-level construction in which we decompose the query as q = q1 + q2, so that 

∥z′ + q∥22 = ∥z′ + q1 + q2∥22.

We use one sketching matrix B1 to maintain B1(z′ + q1) and another sketching matrix B2 to maintain B2q2. At the second level, we additionally introduce a learner whose task is to approximate 

z′ + q1 throughout the stream updates corresponding to q2.Concretely, this learner maintains a vector u, formed as a linear combination of those queries on which the second-level estimator is inaccurate when using 

∥z′ + q1 − u∥22 + ∥u + q2∥22

as a proxy for ∥z′ + q1 + q2∥22. By the preceding intuition, this proxy fails precisely when the inner product ⟨z′ + q1, q2⟩ is large, indicating that the procedure handling q2 has implicitly learned information about z′ + q1 from the updates in q2.To detect such inaccuracies, we again couple the learner and the estimator at the second level with a corrector for ∥z′ + q1 + q2∥22. One subtlety is that the iterate z′ may change during the stream of updates to q2, since the first-level estimator might fail on some query q. To accommodate this, we initiate a new block at the second level whenever z′ is modified. In other words, instead of fixing two blocks corresponding to streams of length m 

> 2

, we form blocks dynamically—either when the stream length reaches a multiple of m 

> 2

or when z′ is updated to z′ + α(q + z′) due to an inaccurate query q. Since we have already bounded the total number of updates to z′ by L = O  

> (1
> ε2

log n

> )

, it follows that the total number of second-level blocks is at most O  

> (1
> ε2

log n

> )

.More generally, let B = O  

> (1
> ε2

log n

> )

. We further limit each recursive estimator for ∥z′ + q∥22 to run only for a 1 

> B

fraction of the steps before deliberately terminating it and starting a new instance. We emphasize that this forced termination is an important mechanism that enforces the boundaries of a B-ary tree, so that the full algorithm has at most H = log B m = O (log n) levels of recursion, which avoids an exponential blow-up in the overall space. Since at most poly (B, H ) = poly   

> (1
> ε

, log n

> )

sketches are maintained simultaneously, the algorithm achieves the desired space complexity. By starting with z = 0n at the beginning of the stream, the algorithm outputs a correct estimate of 

∥x∥22 at all times. – 229 – Comparison to other techniques. It is instructive to describe the differences between our approach with various other strategies. First, we contrast our strategy with the difference estimators presented in Section 3.5. Difference estimators operate on value-based granularity, partitioning the stream based on dyadic increases in the function’s value and allocating lower-accuracy sketches to later blocks. This is highly efficient for insertion-only streams due to the small flip number. However, turnstile streams can fluctuate wildly in value, potentially resulting in large flip numbers. Our framework overcomes this by utilizing time-based recursive decomposition, dividing the stream into blocks based on timestamps and reusing memory by aggressively discarding subroutines once their specific time block expires, avoiding polynomial space dependency. Furthermore, the learner only updates the iterator z′ when the corrector detects a significant error from the estimator. This shares a conceptual connection with the “update-until-converge” phenomenon found in the Private Multiplicative Weights (MWU) method from the differential privacy literature [ HR10 , HLM12 ]. In Private MWU, a synthetic dataset is updated only when a DP query mechanism reveals it is inaccurate, and the total number of updates is bounded by a potential function (relative entropy). Similarly, our learner updates z′ only when the estimator errs. Using 

∥z − z′∥22 as a potential function, we can upper bound the number of updates to poly   

> (1
> ε

, log n

> )

.In both paradigms, upper bounding the number of updates minimizes the exposure of internal randomness, which is the crucial for maintaining robustness and privacy. 

7.1.2 Technical Overview for Approximate Triangle Inequality 

As a natural extension of our F2 algorithm, we can design an algorithm to robustly estimate functions 

F that satisfy an approximate triangle inequality and have non-robust turnstile sketches. Concrete examples of such functions, which we formally discuss in Section 7.3, include the Fp frequency moments, i.e., F (x) = ∥x∥pp, distinct elements, symmetric norms, and robust M -estimators and loss functions commonly used in machine learning, such as the Pseudo-Huber and Cauchy losses. Notice that for any function F satisfying the triangle inequality and F(v) = F(−v) for all v ∈ Rn, if we aim for an α-approximation with a sufficiently large constant α > 3, we can use 

F(z − z′) + F(z′ + q)

as an estimator for F(z + q). By the β-approximate triangle inequality, this provides a lower bound of 1 

> β

· F (z + q). The estimator only fails when 

F(z − z′) + F(z′ + q) ≥ α · F (z + q).

In this case, the triangle inequality gives 

F(z + q) + F(z − z′) ≥ F (z′ + q),

and therefore 

F(z + q) + 2 F(z − z′) ≥ α · F (z + q),

which implies 

F(z + q) ≤ 2

α − 1 · F (z − z′).

Now, if we set z′′ to be the vector in the span of q and z′ that is closest to z under F, we get 

F(z − z′′ ) ≤ F (z + q) ≤ 2

α − 1 · F (z − z′),

– 230 – demonstrating progress. Thus, the learner can iteratively update z′, and within O (log n) steps, z′

converges to z.A similar reasoning applies for functions F that only satisfy the approximate triangle inequality. Using this framework with a corrector, an estimator, and a learner, combined with recursion, we can design a robust algorithm that outputs a Θ(1) -approximation to F, using n1/C levels of recursion for some constant C > 1.

# 7.2 Turnstile Streaming Algorithm for F2 Moment Estimation 

In this section, we provide our robust algorithm for F2 moment estimation on turnstile streams of length m = poly (n) with a universe of size n. Our algorithm uses poly   

> (1
> ε

, log n

> )

space for 

(1 + ε)-approximation, as compared to the optimal algorithms that use O  

> (1
> ε2

log 2 n

> )

bits of space on non-adaptive streams [AMS99]. First, we recall the following guarantees of the classical AMS algorithm: 

Theorem 7.2.1 (AMS sketch for estimating F2). [AMS99 ] Given an accuracy parameter ε ∈ (0 , 1) 

and a failure probability parameter δ ∈ (0 , 1) , let k = O

> (

ε−2 log 1

> δ
> )

. There exists a turnstile streaming algorithm AMS that maintains a linear sketch Ax , where A ∈ Rk×n is sampled from an explicit distribution over random matrices, uses O (k log n) bits of space, and outputs, with probability at least 1 − δ, a (1 + ε)-approximation to the second frequency moment F2 for streams of length 

m = poly( n).

Algorithm :(1) Maintain a hierarchical decomposition of the stream into a tree, where level 1 consists of individual updates and each internal node has branching factor B.(2) A node at level i is created either after observing B consecutive nodes from level i − 1 in the stream, or when the iterate node at the parent level i + 1 is updated. (3) For every block Ci,j , apply an associated sketching matrix Bi,j to summarize relevant vectors, e.g., the frequency vector of the block and any iterate vectors required by subroutines. (4) Upon processing an update, let H denote the current height of the tree, and maintain a sketch matrix BH+1 at the root level. (5) Return EstLevel (H + 1 , BH+1 z), where z denotes the frequency vector of the entire stream. 

Fig. 7.1: Adversarially robust F2 norm estimation algorithm on turnstile streams from [ GLW +26b ]

Algorithm overview. The algorithm organizes the stream into a hierarchical tree structure. At the base level 1, the algorithm partitions the stream into blocks consisting of B individual updates, while each higher level aggregates B nodes from the level below into a single block. Every – 231 – block Ci,j is summarized via an associated sketching matrix Bi,j , which is used to maintain the frequency vector of the block, as well as any auxiliary iterate vectors. As the stream evolves, these sketches are recursively combined and passed upward, culminating in a root-level sketch BH+1 that represents the entire stream. An estimate of the F2 moment is obtained by invoking the subroutine 

EstLevel at the highest level of this recursive construction, which aggregates information from lower levels to yield an adversarially robust estimate. This hierarchical organization ensures that each sketch is subjected to only a bounded number of adaptive queries. With a choice of block size 

B = O (ε−2 log n) and tree height H = O (log n), the algorithm uses poly (1 /ε, log n) space while retaining robustness against adaptive updates. The full algorithm is given in Figure 7.1, with a schematic depiction shown in Figure 7.2. Stream

> q0q1Guess z′′ for z′+q0Level H−2
> ...

Fig. 7.2: Illustration of the recursive tree decomposition of the stream of [ GLW +26b ], with branching factor B = 2 and height H. At level H − 1, the estimate for ∥x∥22 = ∥z + q∥22 is given by 

∥z − z′∥22 + ∥z′ + q∥22. At level H − 2, the estimate for ∥z′ + q∥22 = ∥z′ + q0 + q1∥22 is computed as 

∥z′ + q0 − z′′ ∥22 + ∥z′′ + q1∥22.

The EstLevel subroutine. We next describe the subroutine EstLevel and its guarantees. The role of EstLevel is to produce a robust estimate of the squared L2 norm at a specified level i of the tree. For simplicity of notation, we present the description assuming that q denotes the query vector at level i; in the general execution, however, q is further decomposed as q = q0 + q1, where q0

corresponds to contributions from the left siblings in the preceding block and q1 corresponds to the contribution from the active block (namely, the blocks along the ancestor path of the current update). We denote by z the portion of the stream accumulated in completed blocks of the parent node, so that the task of the subroutine is to estimate ∥z + q∥22.Intuitively, the subroutine EstLevel operates by coordinating three components: an estimator, a learner, and a corrector. The learner maintains an iterate vector v, formed as a linear combination of past queries on which the estimator failed, thereby incrementally inferring information about z.Using this iterate, the estimator seeks to approximate ∥z + q∥22 by combining estimates of ∥z − v∥22

and ∥v + q∥22. The corrector observes the sketches Biz and Az to identify when the estimator’s output deviates significantly from the true value, and then signals the learner to update v. Notably, the sketch Bi is initialized once the preceding block at level i + 1 is finalized. Consequently, it captures the frequency vectors contributed by all left siblings of the active block at level i, and in particular maintains access to Biz.At a high level, EstLevel decomposes the estimate into two terms: Pi, representing the contribution from the current block after adjustment by the learner’s iterate, and Qi, which is obtained recursively from the level below. Concretely, Pi approximates ∥z − v∥22, whereas Qi approximates ∥v + q∥22.When the combined estimate Pi + Qi roughly agrees with an independent estimate Ai of the target – 232 – Algorithm 17 EstLevel (i, Biz) for F2 moment estimation [GLW +26b]

This completes the description of the estimator–corrector interaction, which is formalized in Algorithm 17. 

The MaintainIter subroutine. We now turn to the learner, implemented by the subroutine 

MaintainIter , whose task is to update the iterate v within the currently active block so as to approximate z. At the beginning of each block, the iterate v is set to zero, reflecting the absence of information about the accumulated frequency vector z from earlier blocks. As the stream evolves, the estimator provides tentative estimates to the F2 moment. If such an estimate is inconsistent with the reference value Ai, the corrector signals that the estimator has failed. In response, MaintainIter 

updates v by adding a small, appropriately scaled multiple of the current query vector, in the direction that reduces the estimation error. Through this iterative process, the learner progressively accumulates information about z over the duration of the block, while operating exclusively within the sketched space. We give the full details of the algorithm in Algorithm 18, where we emphasize that all computations occur in the sketch space, i.e., under the image of the sketch matrix Bi at level i, since the algorithm never has direct access to the vectors v and q.The main idea is to argue that the learner can recover z after only a small number of updates. Each update occurs exactly when the estimator makes a mistake, and thus each such event effectively “charges” one additional adaptive interaction to the corrector. We upper bound the total number of these updates in Lemma 7.2.5, using the following reasoning. Let z′ denote the value of the iterate

# 7.3 Approximate Triangle Inequality 

In this section, we generalize our robust algorithm for F2 estimation to a wider class of functions F

that satisfy an approximate triangle inequality. Recall that for some constant β > 0, a function F

obeys a β-approximate triangle inequality if 

F(x − z) ≤ β · (F(x − y) + F(y − z)) ,

for all vectors x, y, z ∈ Rn.As before, the framework uses an estimator, a corrector, and a learner, so that the algorithm maintains an iterate z′ approximating the previous stream vector z. Each incoming query q is processed by computing Pi ≈ F (z−z′) and Qi ≈ F (z′ +q), where Pi is obtained using a non-adaptive sketch and Qi via a recursive procedure. The estimator then outputs the sum Pi + Qi, which by the triangle inequality is at least F(z + q). If this sum significantly overestimates F(z + q), then, by the approximate triangle inequality, the iterate can be updated to a new vector z′′ = −q in a way that provably reduces the distance to the true vector under F. Specifically, Lemma 7.3.1 formalizes how such a precondition triggers an update, ensuring that F(z − z′′ ) is a constant factor smaller than F(z − z′), reflecting progress toward learning z. Consequently, Lemma 7.3.2 shows that the – 239 – algorithm performs at most O (log n) updates per level. Finally, we employ the same recursive structure to bound the number of adaptive interactions at each level. The key subroutine EstLevel 

is described in Algorithm 19, and it is embedded within the same tree structure as in Figure 7.2. We first argue that when the estimator produces an inaccurate output on a query, there is significant progress: F(z − z′′ ) becomes a constant factor smaller than F(z − z′), where z′ and z′′ 

are the iterates before and after the query. Since F satisfies a β-approximate triangle inequality, we have 

F(z − z′) + F(z′ + q) ≥ 1

β · F (z + q).

Lemma 7.3.1 (Precondition triggering) . [ GLW +26b ] Let A be an algorithm that outputs a κ-approximation to a symmetric function F satisfying the β-triangle inequality, and define Z =

A(z − z′) + A(z′ + q). Suppose Z > κ 2 · A (z + q). Then after setting z′′ = −q, with high probability, we have 

F(z − z′′ ) ≤ β + 1 

κ − β · F (z − z′).

Proof. We first condition on the correctness of the subroutines. Then with high probability, we have: (1) Z ∈ [F(z − z′) + F(z′ + q), κ · (F(z − z′) + F(z′ + q))] 

(2) A(z + q) ∈ (F(z + q), κ · F (z + q)] 

Hence, if Z > κ 2 · A (z + q), we obtain 

κ · (F(z − z′) + F(z′ + q)) ≥ Z > κ 2 · A (z + q) ≥ κ2 · F (z + q),

which implies 

F(z − z′) + F(z′ + q) ≥ κ · F (z + q).

Using the β-approximate triangle inequality of F, we also have β·(F(z+q)+ F(z′−z)) ≥ F (z′+q).Since F is symmetric, then F(z′ − z) = F(z − z′), so that 

(β + 1) · F (z − z′) ≥ (κ − β) · F (z + q).

Therefore, updating the iterate from z′ to z′′ = −q gives 

F(z − (−q)) ≤ β + 1 

κ − β · F (z − z′),

which establishes our notion of progress. Consequently, the number of times the iterate v approximating z can be updated is at most 

O (log n).

Lemma 7.3.2 (Bounded iterations) . [ GLW +26b ] Suppose F is a function that, on a stream of length m = poly (n), takes values in   

> [1poly( m)

, poly( m)

> ]

. Let Li denote the number of updates to the iterate z′ at level i. Then with high probability, Li ≤ O (log n) throughout the stream for all i ∈ [H].

– 240 – Proof. Fix a level i ∈ [H] and a point in the stream, and let Pi be the active block at level i. Let 

q1 be the active portion of the query in Pi, and q0 be the contribution from left siblings of Pi, so that q = q0 + q1. Let z represent the current state of the stream, and z′ be the previous iterate maintained by EstLevel .Conditioned on the correctness of the subroutines in Algorithm 19, if the iterate v is not updated, we have Pi + Qi ≤ κi · Ai, where Pi and Qi estimate F(z + q0 − z′) and F(z′ + q1), respectively, and Ai is a κ4i-approximation to F(z + q). If the iterate v is updated, then the precondition of Lemma 7.3.1 triggers, replacing z′ with z′′ = −q1. By Lemma 7.3.1, this ensures progress: 

F(z − z′′ ) = F(z + q) ≤ β + 1 

κ − β · F (z − z′).

Thus, each update to z′ guarantees a constant-factor reduction in F(z − z′), provided κ > 2β + 1 .Since initially F(z) ≤ poly (n) and the minimum value of F is at least 1poly( n) , it follows that the iterate v can be updated at most Li ≤ O (log n) times per level i. Taking a union bound over all H

levels gives the claimed high-probability bound.

Theorem 7.3.3. [GLW +26b ] Suppose F is a function over a stream of length m = poly (n), taking values in the range

Any randomized adversary is just a distribution over private random tapes, and its success probability is upper bounded by the maximum over deterministic sequences. Thus by fixing the adversary’s private random tape, it suffices to consider only deterministic adversaries. Next, we examine the interactions with the sketches used in Algorithm 19. At each step, EstLevel 

queries the sketch at level i to decide whether to update the iterate v. We can view the sketch outputs as symbols: ⊥ if no update is performed, and ⊤ if the iterate is updated. By Lemma 7.3.2, the number of ⊤ events at level i is at most Li = O (log n) with high probability. Let B denote the number of blocks in each level. Each update at level i triggers a query to level i − 1, but does not leak further information about level i. Additionally, at the start of each block, the sketch outputs a κi-approximation encoded in O (1) words. Therefore, the total number of possible output transcripts at level i is upper bounded by  

> (

mB · Li

> )

·

Moreover, each output from the estimator or from Pi uses at most O (log n) bits. Hence, at the bottom level, the number of possible transcripts is bounded by 

> (

Finally, correctness is guaranteed by the approximate triangle inequality argument: the iterate updates always ensure the output remains a κO(C)-approximation to F at all times. Furthermore, while the β-approximate triangle inequality only guarantees a lower bound of 1 

> β

F(z + q) at each recursive step, across the H levels of recursion this lower bound degrades by at most a factor of βH ;because H = O(1) and β is a constant, this degradation is absorbed into the final κO(C) two-sided approximation factor. Thus, the algorithm is adversarially robust and achieves the stated space and approximation guarantees. 

Applications. We now give several examples of functions that obey the approximate triangle inequality. Our first example is F(x) = ∥x∥pp for 0 ≤ p ≤ 1.

Lemma 7.3.4. [ GLW +26b ] For any x, y ∈ Rn and p ∈ (0 , 1] , we have ∥x + y∥pp ≤ ∥ x∥pp + ∥y∥pp.Moreover, we have ∥x + y∥0 ≤ ∥ x∥0 + ∥y∥0.Proof. It is known how to achieve (1 + ε)-approximation for Fp estimation for all p on non-adaptive turnstile streams [ AMS99 , IW05 , Ind06 , Li08 , KNW10a , KNW10b , AKO11 , Gan11 , KNPW11 ,GW18]. We also recall the following definition of a symmetric norm: 

Definition 7.3.6 (Symmetric Norm) . A norm ∥ · ∥ on Rn is called a symmetric norm if it is invariant under permutations and sign changes of coordinates. That is, for every x ∈ Rn, every permutation π of {1, . . . , n }, and every choice of signs σi ∈ {− 1, 1}, we have 

∥x∥ = ∥(σ1xπ(1) , σ 2xπ(2) , . . . , σ nxπ(n))∥.

Equivalently, ∥x∥ depends only on the multi-set of absolute values {| x1|, . . . , |xn|} .

– 243 – It is known how to achieve (1 + ε)-approximation to all symmetric norms on non-adaptive turnstile streams [ BBC +17 , BWZ21 , BMWZ23 ]. Additionally, because all norms must satisfy the triangle inequality, symmetric norms must also satisfy the triangle inequality. Hence, we can apply our framework for robust estimation on turnstile streams. Next, recall that a Bernstein function satisfies the following definition: 

Definition 7.3.7 (Bernstein function, e.g., [ SSV12 ]) . A function f : (0 , ∞) → [0 , ∞) is said to be a 

Bernstein function if it is infinitely differentiable on (0 , ∞) and its first derivative f ′ is completely monotone. Formally, this means that for every n ∈ N0 and all x > 0,

(−1) n · f (n+1) (x) ≥ 0.

We now turn to functions F of the form F(x) = ∑ni=1 g(xi), where g satisfies g(t) = f (t2) for some Bernstein function f . In particular, we list several representative choices of the function g, all of which are commonly encountered in robust statistics.

To establish the approximate triangle inequality, following the same high-level approach as in Lemma 7.3.4 and Lemma 7.3.5, it is enough to analyze the one-dimensional setting. 

Lemma 7.3.8. [ GLW +26b ] Assume g(t) = f (t2) for some Bernstein function f , with g(0) = 0 .Then for all a, b ∈ R, we have 

g(a + b) ≤ 2 ( g(a) + g(b)) .

Proof. From the definition of a Bernstein function, the derivative f ′(x) is non-negative but non-increasing. We also have f (0) = 0 since g(0) = 0 . Thus, f is sub-additive on [0 , ∞), so f (a + b) ≤

f (a) + f (b) for all a, b ≥ 0. Hence, we have f (2 a) ≤ 2f (a) for all a ≥ 0.Consequently, for any 0 ≤ a ≤ b, we have 

(a + b)2 ≤ 2a2 + 2 b2,

so that 

g(a + b) = f (( a + b)2)

≤ f (2 a2 + 2 b2)

≤ f (2 a2) + f (2 b2)

≤ 2( f (a2) + f (b2)) = 2( g(a) + g(b)) ,

as desired. – 244 – To apply our framework, we additionally require a (non-robust) streaming algorithm for estimat-ing F(x). In particular, the work of [ BCWY16 ] establishes the following zero-one law characterizing functions of the form g.
